7 min
Conversion of Negative Perpetual Propositions
Author states conversion rule for negative perpetuals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two types of perpetual negative propositions and state their conversion rule, including the Avicennan exception.

Core Idea
Negative perpetuals convert as perpetual, not as necessary.

In this passage, the author lays down a key conversion rule for modal propositions: among negative propositions, the two perpetual types (the absolute perpetual and the absolute necessary) convert to a perpetual proposition. However, the necessary proposition does not convert as necessary due to a subtle point accepted by Avicenna. We will unpack this rule and its significance.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two perpetual negatives
Find the two types: absolute perpetual (dā'ima muṭlaqa) and absolute necessary (ḍarūriyya muṭlaqa). They are both called 'perpetual' in this context.
Both have universal scope over all times, but the necessary adds metaphysical necessity.
2
Understand the conversion rule
According to the author, each of these negatives converts to a perpetual negative. That is, if you swap subject and predicate of a perpetual negative, you get another perpetual negative.
The resulting proposition is perpetual in modality, not necessarily necessary.
3
Grasp the Avicennan exception
The necessary does not convert as necessary. Example: if Zayd never rode a donkey, 'No thing ridden by Zayd is a donkey' is true by necessity, but its converse 'No donkey is ridden by Zayd by necessity' is false because some donkey might be ridden by Zayd in possibility.
Exception shows necessity may not transfer to the converse; only perpetuity does.
4
State the rule precisely
A necessary negative converts to a perpetual negative, not to a necessary negative. An absolute perpetual negative converts to an absolute perpetual negative.
The rule is general for all negative perpetuals.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَمِنَ السَّوَالِبِ تَنْعَكِسُ الدَّائِمَتَانِ دَائِمَةٌ؛
الدسوقي
(قَوْلُهُ: وَمِنَ السَّوَالِبِ تَنْعَكِسُ الدَّائِمَتَانِ) أي: الدَّائمةُ المطلقةُ، والضَّروريَّةُ المطلقة.
(قَوْلُهُ: دَائِمَةً) أي: دائمةً مُطلقةً، وإِنَّما لم تنعكس الضَّروريَّةُ كَنفسِهَا؛ لأَنَّهُ لا يطَّردُ على مذهبِ الشَّيخِ؛ لأنَّه يصدقُ على مذهبِهِ: لا شيء من مركوب زيد بالحمار بالضّرورةِ، إذا كان زيد لم يركب الحمار أصلاً، وعكشها كنفسِهَا: لَا شَيءٍ مِنَ الحمار بمركوب زيدٍ بالضَّرورةِ، وهي كاذبةٌ لِصدقِ نقيضها؛ وهو: بعضُ الحمار مَركوب زيدٍ بالإمكان العام، نعم عكشها دائمةٌ وهي: لا شيء مِنَ الحمار بِمركوبِ زيد دائماً صادقة.
(قَوْلُهُ: لأَنَّهُ إِذَا صَدَقَ ... إِلَخ) بيان ذلك بالمواد في الضَّروريَّةِ المطلقة أنْ تقول: إذا صدق بالضرورة: لا شيء من الإنسان بحجرٍ؛ صدق: لا شيء مِنَ الحجرِ بإنسان دائماً، وإلا، لصدق نقيضُهُ موجبة جزئيَّةٌ مُطلقة عامة، وهي: بعضُ الحجرِ إنسان بالإطلاق، فتضمُّ ذلكَ النَّقيض للأصل بِأَنْ تجعلهُ صُغرى، والأصل كُبرى بأن تقول: بعضُ الحجرِ إنسان بالإطلاقِ، وَلَا شيء مِنَ الحجر بإنسان بالضَّرورةِ، ينتج: بعضُ الحجر ليس بحجر بالضرورة.
وهو محال ناشئ من نقيض العكس، فيكون العكس حقًّا، وَكَذا يُقالُ في دليل عكس الدائمة المطلقة، إلَّا أَنَّكَ تبدلُ الضَّرورة بالدَّوامِ، فتقولُ: دائماً بدلَ بالضَّرورة.
(قَوْلُهُ: بِالضَّرُورَةِ) أي: إن أردتَ الضَّروريَّةَ المطلقة.
(قَوْلُهُ: أَوْ دَائِمَاً) أي: إِنْ أردْتَ الدائمة المطلقة.
(قَوْلُهُ: لَا شَيْءٍ مِنْ ج (ب) ظهرَ مِمَّا قرَّرْنَاهُ أَنَّ (ج) في جميع الدَّليلِ عبارةٌ عن إنسان مثلاً. وأنَّ (ب) فيه عبارةٌ عن حجرٍ.
(قَوْلُهُ: وَإِلَّا؛ فَبَعْضُ ... إلخ) أي: وإلا؛ لصدق نقيضُهُ: وهو مُوجبةٌ جزئيَّةٌ مُطلقةٌ عامَّةٌ، وهو: بعضُ (ج) (ب) بالإطلاقِ.
العطار
هكذا بعضُ الحجرِ إنسانُ بِالإطلاقِ، وَلَا شيء مِنَ الإنسان بحجرٍ بالضرورة أو دائماً، ينتج مِنَ الشَّكل الأوَّلِ: بعضُ الحجرِ ليسَ بحجر بالضَّرورة في الضَّروريّةِ أو دائماً في الدائمة، وهو مُحالٌ؛ لأنَّهُ سلبُ الشَّيء عَن نَفْسِهِ، وهذا المحالُ ليسَ لَازماً من تركيب القياس وصورته؛ بلْ مِنْ مادَّتِهِ وكُبراهُ مَفروضةُ الصِّدْقِ، فتعيَّنَ أَنْ يكونَ مِنَ الصُّغرى، فتكون باطلةً، فيصدقُ العكس وهو المطلوب.
And from the negative [modalities], the two perpetual ones convert as perpetual. al-Dasuqi: (His statement: 'the two perpetual ones' means: the absolute perpetual and the absolute necessary. (His statement: 'perpetual' means: absolute perpetual. And the necessary does not convert as itself because it does not hold universally according to the Sheikh's doctrine; for on his doctrine, 'Nothing that is ridden by Zayd is a donkey by necessity' is true if Zayd never rode a donkey, but its converse as itself would be 'Nothing of the donkey is ridden by Zayd by necessity', which is false because its contradictory 'Some donkey is ridden by Zayd with general possibility' is true. Yes, its converse as perpetual, i.e., 'Nothing of the donkey is ridden by Zayd perpetually', is true. (His statement: 'because if it is true...' is an explanation of that by example for the absolute necessary. You say: If it is true by necessity that 'Nothing of man is a stone', then it is true perpetually that 'Nothing of stone is a man'. Otherwise, its contradictory, a particular affirmative absolute general, 'Some stone is a man with absolute' would be true. Then you conjoin that contradictory to the original, making it a minor premise and the original a major, saying: 'Some stone is a man with absolute', and 'Nothing of stone is a man by necessity', giving the conclusion: 'Some stone is not a stone by necessity.' This is an impossibility arising from the contradictory of the converse. Hence the converse is true. And similarly is said in the proof of the converse of the absolute perpetual, except you replace 'by necessity' with 'perpetually', so you say 'perpetually' instead of 'by necessity'. (His statement: 'by necessity' means if you intend the absolute necessary. (His statement: 'or perpetually' means if you intend the absolute perpetual. (His statement: 'Nothing of J is B' it appears from what we have established that 'J' in the whole proof stands for e.g. man, and 'B' stands for stone. (His statement: 'Otherwise, some...' means: otherwise, its contradictory would be true, which is a particular affirmative absolute general, i.e., 'Some J is B with absolute'. al-`Attar: Thus, 'Some stone is a man with absolute' and 'Nothing of man is a stone by necessity or perpetually' produce from the first figure: 'Some stone is not a stone by necessity in the necessary case, or perpetually in the perpetual case', which is impossible because it is the negation of a thing of itself. This impossibility does not result from the composition and form of the syllogism but from its matter, and the major is assumed true, so it must be from the minor; thus it is false, so the converse is true, which is what was required.
Source pages p. 715

Commentary
This passage discusses the conversion of negative modal propositions. The author states that among the negative modalities, the two perpetual ones (the absolute perpetual and the absolute necessary) convert as perpetual (i.e., a perpetual negative universal). The commentator al-Dasuqi explains that the necessary does not convert as necessary because it does not hold universally on the Sheikh's doctrine (i.e., Avicenna's view). For example, if Zayd never rode a donkey, then 'Nothing ridden by Zayd is a donkey' is true by necessity (since it never happened), but its converse as necessary, 'Nothing donkey is ridden by Zayd by necessity', would be false because some donkeys could possibly be ridden by Zayd. However, its converse as perpetual is true. Then a general proof is given via reductio ad absurdum. The proof uses a syllogism in the first figure. Suppose the converse is false; then its contradictory (a particular affirmative absolute) is true. Combine this with the original proposition as premises. For example, if 'No man is a stone' is true by necessity, then suppose its converse 'No stone is a man' is false; then 'Some stone is a man' would be true. But 'Some stone is a man' and 'No man is a stone' (by necessity) produce 'Some stone is not a stone' by first figure, which is impossible. Since the impossibility arises from the minor premise (the assumed contradictory of the converse), that minor must be false, so the converse is true. The same proof works for absolute perpetual by replacing 'by necessity' with 'perpetually'. Al-`Attar further clarifies that the impossibility is due to the matter (content) not the form; the major (original) is assumed true, so the falsity falls on the minor, confirming the converse.

Key Terms
السَّالِبَة الدَّائِمَة
الضَّروريَّة المطلقة
عكس

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does the 'two perpetual ones' refer to?
A) The absolute perpetual and the absolute necessary
B) The absolute negative and the relative negative
C) The positive perpetual and the negative perpetual
D) The necessary and the possible

Key Takeaway
Negative perpetuals convert as perpetual; necessary negatives do not convert as necessary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the proposition 'No human is a stone' as an absolute perpetual. According to the rule, what is its converse and its modality?
?
Explain why the converse of a necessary negative cannot be necessary, using the Avicennan example or a similar one from Islamic logic.

Immediate Inference

Proof of the Perpetual Conversion via Reductio
Author proves the conversion by reductio.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the reductio proof that a necessary negative proposition converts to a perpetual negative.

Core Idea
If the converse were false, a contradiction would follow.

The author provides a rigorous proof that a necessary negative proposition (e.g., 'No man is a stone') converts to a perpetual negative. The proof uses a reductio ad absurdum: assuming the converse is false leads to a contradiction. We will walk through the logical steps.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original proposition
Take a true necessary negative, e.g., 'No man is a stone' by necessity.
The subject and predicate are arbitrary; we use 'man' and 'stone' as in the text.
2
Assume the converse is false
Suppose 'No stone is a man' is false. Then its contradictory, 'Some stone is a man', must be true.
This is a particular affirmative proposition with absolute modality.
3
Form a syllogism with the original
Take the contradictory as minor premise ('Some stone is a man') and the original as major ('No man is a stone'). In the first figure, the middle term 'man' appears as subject of the major and predicate of the minor.
The mood is Ferio, which is valid.
4
Derive the impossible conclusion
The syllogism yields 'Some stone is not a stone', which is a contradiction (negation of a thing of itself).
The impossibility is due to the matter, not the form.
5
Conclude the converse is true
Since the impossibility arises from assuming the converse false, that assumption is false. Therefore the converse is true, and it is perpetual (not necessary) because the conclusion is perpetual.
The same proof works for absolute perpetual by replacing 'by necessity' with 'perpetually'.

(قَوْلُهُ: وَمِنَ السَّوَالِبِ تَنْعَكِسُ الدَّائِمَتَانِ) أي: الدَّائمةُ المطلقةُ، والضَّروريَّةُ المطلقة.
(قَوْلُهُ: دَائِمَةً) أي: دائمةً مُطلقةً، وإِنَّما لم تنعكس الضَّروريَّةُ كَنفسِهَا؛ لأَنَّهُ لا يطَّردُ على مذهبِ الشَّيخِ؛ لأنَّه يصدقُ على مذهبِهِ: لا شيء من مركوب زيد بالحمار بالضّرورةِ، إذا كان زيد لم يركب الحمار أصلاً، وعكشها كنفسِهَا: لَا شَيءٍ مِنَ الحمار بمركوب زيدٍ بالضَّرورةِ، وهي كاذبةٌ لِصدقِ نقيضها؛ وهو: بعضُ الحمار مَركوب زيدٍ بالإمكان العام، نعم عكشها دائمةٌ وهي: لا شيء مِنَ الحمار بِمركوبِ زيد دائماً صادقة.

Commentary
This passage discusses the conversion of negative modal propositions. The author states that among the negative modalities, the two perpetual ones (the absolute perpetual and the absolute necessary) convert as perpetual (i.e., a perpetual negative universal). The commentator al-Dasuqi explains that the necessary does not convert as necessary because it does not hold universally on the Sheikh's doctrine (i.e., Avicenna's view). However, its converse as perpetual is true. Then a general proof is given via reductio ad absurdum. The proof uses a syllogism in the first figure. Suppose the converse is false; then its contradictory (a particular affirmative absolute) is true. Combine this with the original proposition as premises. Conversion of Universal Negative Propositions: Proof by Reductio
Author proves universal negative conversion via reductio.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the reductio proof for the conversion of universal negative propositions and identify the middle term and mood of the syllogism used.

Core Idea
Denying the conversion of a universal negative leads to an absurd self-negation.

This lesson unpacks the proof that a universal negative proposition (e.g., 'No A is B') converts to a universal negative ('No B is A'). The author employs a reductio argument: assume the converse false, combine it with the original proposition in a first-figure syllogism, and derive an impossibility. The proof covers both absolute necessary and absolute perpetual modalities. Understanding this reasoning is crucial for handling immediate inferences in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conversion claim
The rule states: if 'No A is B' is true (with necessity or perpetuity), then 'No B is A' is always true. Note the modality of the converse: perpetual (not necessarily necessary).
The original modality may be necessary or perpetual, but the converse is always perpetual.
2
Assume the converse false
If the converse were false, its contradictory 'Some B is A' (absolute) would be true. This is the starting point of the reductio.
The contradictory of a universal negative is a particular affirmative.
3
Form the first-figure syllogism
Take the assumed contradictory as the minor premise ('Some B is A') and the original universal negative as the major premise ('No A is B'). In the first figure, this yields the conclusion: 'Some B is not B' (with the original modality).
The middle term is 'A', which appears as predicate in the minor and subject in the major.
4
Recognize the absurdity and conclude
The conclusion 'Some B is not B' is self-contradictory (a thing cannot be not itself). Since the major premise is assumed true, the absurdity must arise from the minor premise. Therefore the minor is false, so its contradictory (the converse) is true.
This proves the conversion rule; the impossibility is due to the matter (minor premise), not the syllogistic form.

(قَوْلُهُ: لأَنَّهُ إِذَا صَدَقَ ... إِلَخ) بيان ذلك بالمواد في الضَّروريَّةِ المطلقة أنْ تقول: إذا صدق بالضرورة: لا شيء من الإنسان بحجرٍ؛ صدق: لا شيء مِنَ الحجرِ بإنسان دائماً، وإلا، لصدق نقيضُهُ موجبة جزئيَّةٌ مُطلقة عامة، وهي: بعضُ الحجرِ إنسان بالإطلاق، فتضمُّ ذلكَ النَّقيض للأصل بِأَنْ تجعلهُ صُغرى، والأصل كُبرى بأن تقول: بعضُ الحجرِ إنسان بالإطلاقِ، وَلَا شيء مِنَ الحجر بإنسان بالضَّرورةِ، ينتج: بعضُ الحجر ليس بحجر بالضرورة. وهو محال ناشئ من نقيض العكس، فيكون العكس حقًّا، وَكَذا يُقالُ في دليل عكس الدائمة المطلقة، إلَّا أَنَّكَ تبدلُ الضَّرورة بالدَّوامِ، فتقولُ: دائماً بدلَ بالضَّرورة. (قَوْلُهُ: بِالضَّرُورَةِ) أي: إن أردتَ الضَّروريَّةَ المطلقة. (قَوْلُهُ: أَوْ دَائِمَاً) أي: إِنْ أردْتَ الدائمة المطلقة. (قَوْلُهُ: لَا شَيْءٍ مِنْ ج (ب) ظهرَ مِمَّا قرَّرْنَاهُ أَنَّ (ج) في جميع الدَّليلِ عبارةٌ عن إنسان مثلاً. وأنَّ (ب) فيه عبارةٌ عن حجرٍ. (قَوْلُهُ: وَإِلَّا; فَبَعْضُ ... إلخ) أي: وإلا؛ لصدق نقيضُهُ: وهو مُوجبةٌ جزئيَّةٌ مُطلقةٌ عامَّةٌ، وهو: بعضُ (ج) (ب) بالإطلاقِ. العطار هكذا بعضُ الحجرِ إنسانُ بِالإطلاقِ، وَلَا شيء مِنَ الإنسان بحجرٍ بالضرورة أو دائماً، ينتج مِنَ الشَّكل الأوَّلِ: بعضُ الحجرِ ليسَ بحجر بالضَّرورة في الضَّروريّةِ أو دائماً في الدائمة، وهو مُحالٌ; لأنَّهُ سلبُ الشَّيء عَن نَفْسِهِ، وهذا المحالُ ليسَ لَازماً من تركيب القياس وصورته; بلْ مِنْ مادَّتِهِ وكُبراهُ مَفروضةُ الصِّدْقِ، فتعيَّنَ أَنْ يكونَ مِنَ الصُّغرى، فتكون باطلةً، فيصدقُ العكس وهو المطلوب.
Because if it is true: necessarily or always nothing of J is B, then always nothing of B is J, and if not, then some B is J (absolutely). al-Dasuqi: (; B, . The donkey is a mount of Zayd necessarily, and it is false because its contradictory is true; which is: some donkey is a mount of Zayd with general possibility, indeed its conversion [by the constant] is always true: nothing of the donkey is a mount of Zayd always. (His saying: because if it is true ... etc.) Explanation of that with material examples concerning the absolute necessary: you say: if it is true necessarily: nothing of human is stone; true: nothing of stone is human always, and if not, then its contradictory would be true as a particular affirmative absolute general, which is: some stone is human absolutely, then you combine that contradictory with the original by making it the minor premise and the original the major premise, saying: some stone is human absolutely, and nothing of stone is human necessarily, it yields: some stone is not stone necessarily. That is impossibility arising from the contradictory of the converse, so the converse is true, and likewise is said in the proof of the converse of the absolute perpetual, except that you replace necessity with perpetuity, so you say: always instead of necessarily. (His saying: necessarily) meaning if you intend the absolute necessary. (His saying: or always) meaning if you intend the absolute perpetual. (His saying: nothing from J (B) it appears from what we established that J in all the proof is an expression for e.g. human, and B is an expression for stone. (His saying: and if not, then some ... etc.) meaning: and if not, then its contradictory would be true: it is a particular affirmative absolute general, which is: some (J) (B) absolutely. al-Attar: Thus some stone is human absolutely, and nothing of human is stone necessarily or always, it yields from the first figure: some stone is not stone necessarily in the necessary or always in the perpetual, and it is impossible because it is the negation of a thing from itself, and this impossibility is not necessary from the composition of the syllogism and its form; but from its matter and its major premise being assumed true, so it is determined that it is from the minor, so it becomes false, and the converse is true which is required.
Source pages p. 716

Commentary
This passage proves the conversion rule for universal negative propositions: 'No A is B' converts to 'No B is A'. The author uses a reductio ad absurdum. Starting with the assumption that 'No A is B' is true (with necessity or perpetuity), the desired converse is 'No B is A' with perpetuity. If the converse is false, its contradictory 'Some B is A' (absolute) is true. Combining this contradictory as the minor premise with the original universal negative as the major premise in a first-figure syllogism yields the absurd conclusion 'Some B is not B' (with necessity/perpetuity). Since this conclusion is impossible (negating a thing of itself), the assumption that the converse is false is invalid, so the converse must be true. The proof is given for both the absolute necessary (daruriyya mutlaqa) and the absolute perpetual (da'ima mutlaqa), with the only difference being the modality: necessity vs. perpetuity. The glosses clarify the terminology and the example: 'No human is a stone' converts to 'No stone is a human'. The final note by al-Attar emphasizes that the absurdity arises from the minor premise, not the syllogistic form.

Key Terms
عكس
ضرورية مطلقة
دائمة مطلقة
شكل أول

B) Absolute perpetuity (دائمة مطلقة)
C) Absolute possibility (ممكنة مطلقة)
D) Absolute impossibility (ممتنعة مطلقة)

Key Takeaway
The conversion of a universal negative proposition is proven by reductio: assuming the converse false leads to a self-contradiction in a first-figure syllogism.

Conversion of Conditional Universal Negatives: From Conditioned to Customary
Author explains conversion rules for conditional universal negatives.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to convert a conditional universal negative (mashruta or 'urfiyya) into its correct converse (broad customary), and explain why the conditioned does not convert as itself.

Core Idea
Conditional universal negatives convert to broad customary, not as themselves.

In this passage, the author explains the conversion rules for two types of conditional universal negatives: the broad conditioned (mashruta 'amma), which is necessary, and the broad customary ('urfiyya 'amma), which is perpetual. You will learn why the conditioned cannot be converted as itself and how both convert to a broad customary proposition. This rule is crucial for handling modal propositions in logical inference.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of conditional universal negatives
Look for the terms 'mashruta' and ''urfiyya' in the passage. The former is necessary (e.g., 'necessarily no A is B as long as it is A'), the latter is perpetual (e.g., 'always no A is B as long as it is A').
2
Understand the general conversion rule for universal negatives
The passage first restates that simple conversion of a universal negative (like 'no A is B') yields a particular negative ('some B is not A'), but this can lead to contradiction if the original is false. Here, the focus is on conditional forms.
3
Analyze the conditioned example
The text gives the example 'Nothing ridden by Zayd is a donkey as long as it is ridden by Zayd' (true if Zayd never rode a donkey). Its converse as itself, 'Nothing of the donkey is ridden by Zayd as long as it is a donkey,' is false because some donkey could be ridden by Zayd possibly. This shows the conditioned does not convert as itself.
The converse as itself would be a conditioned necessary statement, which is false.
4
Determine the correct converse
The correct converse is a broad customary: 'Always nothing of the donkey is ridden by Zayd as long as it is a donkey.' This is true because the condition 'as long as it is a donkey' does not require necessity; it is a perpetual statement.
The conversion of mashruta yields 'urfiyya, not mashruta.
5
Apply to the customary case
The customary converts to itself (i.e., to another customary), as implied by the rule that both types convert to a broad customary. The author provides a second example with 'writer' and 'moving fingers' to illustrate.
For 'urfiyya, the converse is also 'urfiyya; the rule is consistent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

الدسوقي
(قَوْلُهُ: يَنتُجُ: بَعْضُ ب لَيْسَ (ب) أي: لأنَّا نجعل هذا النقيض صُغرى; لأنَّهُ مُوجبةٌ، والأصلُ كُبرى; لأنَّه كُلَّيَّةٌ، والشَّكلُ الأَوَّلُ يُشترط فيه إيجابُ صُغراهُ وكلّيَّةُ كُبراه.

(قَوْلُهُ: (وَالْعُرْفِيَّةُ) هي مِنَ الدَّوائِمِ.

(قَوْلُهُ: لِأَنَّهُ إِذَا صَدَقَ ... إلخ) بيان ذلك بالمواد في المشروطة العامَّةِ أنْ تقول: لأنَّهُ إذا صدق بالضَّرورة لا شيء من الكاتب بساكن الأصابع ما دام كاتباً; صدقَ: دائماً لا شيء من ساكن الأصابع بكاتب ما دام ساكن الأصابع، وإلا; لصدق نقيضُه موجبة جزئيَّةٌ مُطلقة حينيَّة، وهي: بعض ساكن الأصابع كاتب بالإطلاقِ حين هو ساكنُ الأصابع، فتضمُّ ذلكَ النَّقيض للأصل بأن تقول: بعضُ ساكن الأصابع كاتب بالإطلاق حين هو ساكنُ الأصابع، ولا شيء مِنَ الكاتب ...
(۱) (قَوْلُهُ: ولا شيء... إلخ) وقع فيما وقع فيه الشارح كثيراً من حذف الجهة سهواً فحذف الجهة الكبرى وجهة النتيجة وهي فيهما الضرورة. ا.هـ. الشرنوبي .
Chapter: On Simple Conversion
It is with the original, produces: some B is not B, and it is impossible.
(And) the conditioned [universal negative] and the customary [universal negative] (the two broad, a broad customary) convert; because if it is true
al-Dasuqi
(His saying: produces: some B is not (B) meaning: because we make this contradictory a minor premise; because it is affirmative, and the original is the major; because it is universal, and in the first figure the minor must be affirmative and the major universal.
(His saying: the conditioned) it is from the necessary [propositions].
(His saying: (and the customary) it is from the perpetual [propositions].
(His saying: broad customary) The broad conditioned does not convert as itself; because it is not consistent according to the doctrine of the Shaykh; because according to his doctrine it is true necessarily: nothing of what is ridden by Zayd is a donkey as long as it is ridden by Zayd, if Zayd never rode a donkey at all. And its conversion as itself necessarily: nothing of the donkey is ridden by Zayd as long as it is a donkey, and it is false because its contradictory is true, which is: some donkey is ridden by Zayd with possibility when it is a donkey. Yes, its conversion as a broad customary is that one says: always nothing of the donkey is ridden by Zayd as long as it is a donkey; it is true.
(His saying: because if it is true ... etc.) Explanation of that with material examples concerning the broad conditioned: you say: because if it is true necessarily: nothing of the writer is moving fingers as long as he is a writer; it is true: always nothing of the moving fingers is a writer as long as moving fingers, and if not, then its contradictory would be true as a particular affirmative absolute temporal, which is: some moving fingers is a writer absolutely when it is moving fingers, then you combine that contradictory with the original saying: some moving fingers is a writer absolutely when it is moving fingers, and nothing of the writer ...
(1) (His saying: and nothing ... etc.) The commentator often omitted the modality inadvertently, so he omitted the modality of the major premise and the conclusion, which are necessity. End. al-Sharnubi.
Source pages p. 717

Commentary
This passage is the beginning of a new chapter on simple conversion ('aks mustawi). It first restates the previous proof: if a universal negative is converted, its denial leads to the absurdity 'some B is not B'. Then it discusses the conversion of two types of conditional universal negatives: al-mashruta al-'amma (the broad conditioned) and al-'urfiyya al-'amma (the broad customary). The conditioned is a necessary proposition with a condition (e.g., 'No A is B as long as it is A'), and the customary is a perpetual proposition with a condition. The author states that these two propositions convert not as themselves (i.e., not to the same type of conditional) but to a broad customary. The gloss by al-Dasuqi explains that the broad conditioned does not convert as itself because, according to the doctrine of the Shaykh (likely Ibn Sina), a counterexample can be found: 'Nothing ridden by Zayd is a donkey as long as it is ridden by Zayd' is true if Zayd never rode a donkey, but its converse 'No donkey is ridden by Zayd as long as it is a donkey' is false because some donkey could be ridden by Zayd possibly. However, the converse as a broad customary is true. The next gloss provides a second example: 'No writer is moving fingers as long as he is a writer' converts to 'Always nothing of the moving fingers is a writer as long as moving fingers'. The marginal note by al-Sharnubi criticizes the commentator for omitting the modality (necessity) in the major premise and conclusion, which should be included.

عكس مستوي ('aks mustawi)
مشروطة عامة (mashruta 'amma)
عرفية عامة ('urfiyya 'amma)

The conversion of a conditional universal negative (whether necessary or perpetual) yields a broad customary universal negative, not the same type.

Conversion of Restricted and Customary Universals
Author states the rule for converting restricted and customary universals and proves it by reductio.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to convert a restricted or customary universal proposition into its simple converse and verify the conversion using reductio ad absurdum.

Core Idea
Restricted and customary universals convert to customary universals, proven by reductio.

In this lesson, we examine how modal universal propositions behave under simple conversion. The author of the Tadhhib al-Mantiq explains that both the restricted universal (mashruta 'amma) and the customary universal ('urfiyya 'amma) convert to a customary universal, not to their own type. The proof employs reductio ad absurdum, a powerful tool in Islamic logic for establishing necessary consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original proposition
Find the universal negative with necessity modality in the Arabic: 'لا شيء من الكاتب بساكن الأصابع ما دام كاتباً' (No writer is a finger-mover as long as he is a writer).
This is a restricted universal (mashruta 'amma).
2
State the proposed converse
The author claims the converse is a customary universal: 'دائماً لا شيء من ساكن الأصابع بكاتب ما دام ساكن الأصابع' (Always no finger-mover is a writer as long as he is a finger-mover).
Note the modality changes from 'by necessity' to 'always' (perpetuity).
3
Assume the converse false
For reductio, assume the contradictory of the converse is true: a particular affirmative absolute temporal: 'بعض ساكن الأصابع كاتب بالإطلاق حين هو ساكن الأصابع' (Some finger-mover is a writer absolutely at the time he is a finger-mover).
This is the contradictory: the negation of the proposed customary universal.
4
Combine with original premise
Combine this assumed true proposition with the original universal negative in a first-figure syllogism: Some finger-mover is a writer (at the time of being a finger-mover); No writer is a finger-mover (as long as he is a writer); therefore Some finger-mover is not a finger-mover.
The conclusion is a contradiction of identity.
5
Recognize the contradiction
The conclusion is impossible (a thing cannot be both itself and not itself), so the original assumption that the converse is false is false; hence the converse must be true.
This completes the reductio proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: فِي الْعَكْسِ الْمُسْتَوِي (وَ) تنعكس المشروطةُ والعُرفيَّةُ العَامَّتَانِ عُرفيَّةٌ عَامَّةٌ؛ لأنَّه إذا صدق بالضَّرورة: لا شيء من الكاتب بساكن الأصابع ما دام كاتباً؛ صدق: دائماً لا شيء من ساكن الأصابع بكاتب ما دام ساكن الأصابع، وإلا لصدق نقيضه: موجبة جزئيَّة مُطلقة حينيَّة، وهي: بعض ساكن الأصابع كاتب بالإطلاق حين هو ساكن الأصابع، فتضمُّ ذلك النَّقيض للأصل بأن تقول: بعض ساكن الأصابع كاتب بالإطلاق حين هو ساكن الأصابع، ولا شيء من الكاتب بساكن الأصابع ما دام كاتباً، ينتج: بعض ساكن الأصابع ليس بساكن الأصابع، وهو مُحال.
Chapter: On the simple converse. And the two universal restricted and universal customary convert to a universal customary. Because if it is true by necessity: 'No writer is a finger-mover as long as he is a writer', then it is true: 'Always no finger-mover is a writer as long as he is a finger-mover'. Otherwise its contradictory would be true: a particular affirmative absolute temporal, which is: 'Some finger-mover is a writer absolutely at the time he is a finger-mover'. Then combine that contradictory with the original by saying: 'Some finger-mover is a writer absolutely at the time he is a finger-mover', and 'No writer is a finger-mover as long as he is a writer', yielding: 'Some finger-mover is not a finger-mover', which is impossible.
Source pages p. 717

Commentary
This passage explains the conversion rule for two types of universal propositions: the restricted universal (المشروطة العامة), which affirms with necessity for all individuals during the duration of the subject's being, and the customary universal (العرفية العامة), which affirms with perpetuity for all individuals during the subject's being. The rule states that both convert to a customary universal (عرفية عامة), not to the same type. The proof is by reductio ad absurdum. Take the original restricted universal: 'No writer is a finger-mover as long as he is a writer' (true with necessity). If the converse 'Always no finger-mover is a writer as long as he is a finger-mover' were false, then its contradictory 'Some finger-mover is a writer absolutely at the time he is a finger-mover' would be true. Combining this particular affirmative with the original universal negative in the first figure yields 'Some finger-mover is not a finger-mover', a contradiction of identity. Therefore the converse must be true. The example uses everyday entities (كاتب, ساكن الأصابع) to illustrate the logical principle, typical of Islamic logic texts.

الْعَكْسُ الْمُسْتَوِي
الْمَشْرُوْطَةُ الْعَامَّةُ
الْعُرْفِيَّةُ الْعَامَّةُ
نَقِيض

C) بالإطلاق: بعض ساكن الأصابع كاتب حين هو ساكن الأصابع
D) بالضرورة: كل ساكن الأصابع ليس بكاتب ما دام ساكن الأصابع

Key Takeaway
Restricted and customary universal propositions both convert to customary universal propositions, as proven by reductio.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Conversion of Special Modal Propositions
Conversion rule for special conditionals

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to convert a special conditional or special conventional proposition and identify the resulting modal type as a conventional proposition that is not permanent in some.

Core Idea
Special conditionals convert to a conventional non-permanent modal.

In this passage, the author explains how two specific modal propositions – the special conditional and the special conventional – behave under simple conversion. Unlike other modals, they transform into a new, previously unlisted modality: a conventional proposition that is not permanent in some. Understanding this conversion is crucial for mastering modal logic and its application in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two special propositions
Locate the definitions of 'special conditional' (al-mashruta al-khassa) and 'special conventional' (al-‘urfiya al-khassa) from the key terms. Note that both involve a judgment that holds as long as the subject's description exists, with the special conditional having an essential predicate and the special conventional having a non-essential predicate.
2
Understand the conversion rule
The author states that both special propositions convert to a single type: 'a conventional that is not permanent in some' (عرفية لا دائمة في البعض). Grasp that the conversion swaps subject and predicate but transforms the modality into a particular absolute universal.
3
Analyze the resulting modality
Examine the commentator al-Dasuqi's explanation: this new modal means the judgment holds as long as the subject's description applies, but only for some individuals (not essentially permanent for all). Recognize that this is a new modality not listed among the original fifteen.
4
Count the total modal propositions
Combine the fifteen earlier modals, four from contradiction, and the two from conversion (the non-permanent temporal and this conventional non-permanent) to obtain twenty-one. This exercise reinforces how conversion expands the modal system.

Section on Simple Conversion. And the two special [propositions] — the conditional and the conventional — convert to a conventional that is not permanent in some. Al-Dasuqi: His statement: 'a conventional that is not permanent in some' — this modality did not appear earlier among the modal propositions. So from what preceded in the modal propositions and in contradiction, and from what is here, it follows that the modal propositions are twenty-one. And his statement: 'a conventional not permanent in some' means a proposition in which the permanence of the relation is judged as long as the description of the subject holds, and this is restricted by the lack of essential permanence in some. His statement: 'not permanent in some' means a particular absolute universal. His statement: 'so it follows ... etc.' — as for what preceded in the modal propositions, they are fifteen; as for what preceded in contradiction, they are four: the temporal possible, the temporal possible, the permanent possible, and the absolute temporal; and in conversion here there are two: the non-permanent temporal and the conventional non-permanent in some.

Commentary
This passage defines the conversion rule for the two special modal propositions: the special conditional (al-mashruta al-khassa) and the special conventional (al-‘urfiya al-khassa). The author states that they convert to a new type: a conventional proposition that is not permanent in some (i.e., a particular absolute universal). The commentator al-Dasuqi explains that this modality is new and not among the previously listed fifteen modals. He then clarifies the term 'conventional not permanent in some': it is a proposition where the relation holds as long as the subject's description applies, but not essentially for all individuals—only for some. The next comment specifies that 'not permanent in some' means a particular absolute universal (juz’iyya mutlaqa ‘amma). Finally, a footnote enumerates the total count of modals: fifteen from earlier, four from contradiction, and these two from conversion, making twenty-one. The passage is essential for understanding how conversion affects modal propositions and introduces a new modal type not covered earlier.

Converting Specific Modal Propositions to Universal Customary
Author states and proves conversion rule for two specific modal propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to convert a specific modal proposition (mashrutah khassah or urfiyyah khassah) into the correct compound modal proposition and verify the validity of the conversion using the logical proof provided.

Core Idea
Converting specific modals yields a compound modality: a universal customary plus a particular absolute.

This passage from the commentary explains how to convert two types of modal propositions—the specific conditional and the specific customary—into a new compound type. Understanding this conversion is crucial for handling modal propositions in syllogistic contexts, especially in Islamic theology where temporal and descriptive modalities are common. The commentator provides a detailed proof using an example from the specific conditional.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original specific proposition type
Determine whether the original proposition is a specific conditional (mashrutah khassah) or a specific customary (urfiyyah khassah). Both convert to the same type of compound proposition.
The specific conditional has the modality 'as long as it exists but not always'; the specific customary has 'as long as the description holds but not always'.
2
Recognize the compound result
The converse is a compound of a universal customary proposition (al-urfiyyah al-ammah) and a particular absolute proposition (al-mutlaqah al-ammah al-juz'iyyah). The universal part expresses the permanent relation as long as the description holds; the particular part expresses that in some cases it is not always true.
3
Apply the proof structure
First, the universal part of the converse follows because the original specific proposition implies the general (non-specific) version, which converts to a universal customary. Second, the particular part is proven by contradiction: if the non-permanence were false, a contradictory universal absolute would result, which would conflict with the original proposition.
4
Verify with the example
Consider the specific conditional: 'No writer is still-fingered as long as he is a writer' (with the implicit 'not always' to make it specific). Its converse is: 'No still-fingered person is a writer as long as he is still-fingered, not always in some' — meaning some still-fingered people are actually writers at other times. This matches the compound structure.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتنعكس المشروطة والعرفيَّة الخاصَّتان، عرفيَّة لا دائمة في البعض

قوله: لا دائمة في البعض لفظ في البعض من تتمة الجهة، وأما لا دوام في الكل؛ فهو معنى العرفية الخاصة، ولذلك احتاج الشارح للتعبير بقوله: والعرفية اللادائمة في البعض.

والعرفية اللادائمة في البعض: قضيَّةٌ مُركَّبَةٌ مِن عرفيَّةٍ عَامَّةٍ كلّيَّةٍ، ومُطلقة عامة جزئية . أما العرفية العامَّةُ؛ فَهِيَ الجزء الأول، وأما المطلقة العامة الجزئية؛ فهي مفهوم اللادوامِ في البعض . وإذا عرفتَ ذلكَ؛ فنقولُ: الخاصَّتانِ ينعكسانِ إلى العرفية العامة المقيَّدة باللادوام في البعض؛ لأنَّه إذا صدق: بيانُ ذلك بالمواد في المشروطة الخاصَّةِ أنْ تقول: لأَنَّهُ إذا صدق بالضَّرورةِ: لا شيء من الكاتب بساكن الأصابع ما دامَ كاتباً؛ صدق دائماً لا شيء مِنْ ساكن الأصابع بكاتب ما دام ساكناً لا دائماً في البعض؛ أي: بعضُ ساكن الأصابع كاتب بالفعل، أمَّا صدقُ الجزء الأَوَّلِ مِنَ العكس، وهو دائماً لا شيء من ساكن الأصابع بكاتب ما دامَ ساكناً؛ فَلِكَونِهِ لازماً للمشروطة العامَّةِ لِمَا تقدَّمَ أنَّها تنعكسُ عُرفيَّةً عامَّةً، ولازم العام لازم للخاص . وأما صدقُ اللادوام; فَلِأَنَّهُ لو لم يصدق: بعضُ ساكن الأصابع كاتب بالفعل؛ لصدق نقيضُهُ سالبةٌ كُلِّيَّةٌ مُطلقةً دائمةً، وهي: لا شيء مِنْ ساكن الأصابع بكاتب دائماً، ويعكس ذلكَ النَّقيض إلى نفْسِهِ، وهو: لا شيء من الكاتبِ بِساكن دائماً، وهو مُنافٍ للادوامِ في الأصلِ الصَّادقِ القائل: كُلُّ كاتب ساكن الأصابع
And the specific conditional and customary [propositions] convert to a customary [proposition] that is not always in some. His statement: 'Not always in some' – the phrase 'in some' is part of the modality. As for 'not always in all', that is the meaning of the specific customary. Therefore the commentator needed to express by his statement: 'and the customary non-permanent in some'. The customary non-permanent in some is a compound proposition consisting of a universal general customary and a particular general absolute. As for the general customary, it is the first part; as for the particular general absolute, it is the meaning of non-permanence in some. And when you know that, we say: the two specific [propositions] convert to the general customary restricted by non-permanence in some. Because if it is true that – demonstration of that by materials in the specific conditional: because if it is true by necessity: 'No writer is still-fingered as long as he is a writer' (with the restriction 'not always' to make it specific), then it is true always: 'No still-fingered person is a writer as long as he is still-fingered, not always in some' – i.e., some still-fingered people are actually writers. As for the truth of the first part of the converse, which is 'always: no still-fingered person is a writer as long as he is still-fingered', that is because it is a necessary consequent of the general conditional, since it has been established that it converts to a general customary, and the necessary consequent of the general is necessary for the specific. As for the truth of the non-permanence, it is because if it were not true that some still-fingered people are actually writers, then its contradictory would be true: a universal absolute eternal negative: 'No still-fingered person is a writer always'. And that contradictory converts to itself, i.e., 'No writer is still-fingered always', and that contradicts the non-permanence in the original true [proposition] stating: 'Every writer is still-fingered'.
Source pages p. 719

Commentary
This passage from the commentary explains the conversion (عكس) of two specific modal propositions: the specific conditional (المشروطة الخاصة) and the specific customary (العرفية الخاصة). The matn states that both convert to a customary proposition that is 'not always in some' (عرفية لا دائمة في البعض). The commentator first clarifies the phrase 'not always in some': the word 'in some' is part of the modality, and 'not always in all' is actually the meaning of the specific customary (which is universal). The commentator then defines the compound modality: it is composed of a universal general customary (عرفية عامة كلية) as the first part, and a particular general absolute (مطلقة عامة جزئية) as the meaning of 'non-permanence in some'. With this understanding, the commentator affirms the conversion rule: both specific propositions convert to a general customary restricted by non-permanence in some. The proof uses an example from the specific conditional: if it is true by necessity that 'no writer is still-fingered as long as he is a writer' (i.e., during the time of writing), then it must also be true that 'no still-fingered person is a writer as long as he is still-fingered, not always in some' – meaning that some still-fingered persons are actually writers at other times. The proof is in two steps: first, the universal part of the converse (the 'always' part) holds because the original specific conditional implies the general conditional, which we already know converts to a general customary; and what is necessary for the general is also necessary for the specific. Second, the non-permanence part (the 'not always' part) holds because if it were false, then the contradictory would be true: 'no still-fingered person is a writer always'. That contradictory, being an eternal universal negative, converts to itself (i.e., its converse is the same), giving 'no writer is still-fingered always', which contradicts the original statement that 'every writer is still-fingered' (which is implied by the original specific conditional). Therefore, the non-permanence must be true to avoid contradiction. Thus the conversion rule is proven.

The Structure of Customary Non-Permanent in Some
Author defines the compound 'customary non-permanent in some'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to decompose a compound proposition of the form 'customary non-permanent in some' into its two components: a general customary universal and a general particular absolute.

Core Idea
Every special proposition is a compound: a general customary universal plus a non-permanence clause.

In this lesson, we dissect the 'customary non-permanent in some' proposition, which is central to understanding the conversion of special propositions. The author defines it as composed of two simpler propositions. By grasping this structure, you will be able to analyse any special proposition and see its conversion result more clearly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposition type
Recognize that 'customary non-permanent in some' (العرفية اللادائمة في البعض) is a compound proposition. It is not a simple modality but a combination of a universal customary and a particular absolute.
The passage states it is a compound proposition (قضية مركبة).
2
Extract the first component: general customary
The first part is a general customary universal (عرفية عامة كلية), e.g., 'No B is C while it is B.' This part asserts that as long as the subject exists, the predicate is absent.
This is the الجزء الأول (first part).
3
Extract the second component: non-permanence in some
The second part is a general particular absolute (مطلقة عامة جزئية), which represents the concept of 'non-permanence in some' (لادوام في البعض). It means that for some individuals of the subject, the predicate does not hold permanently.
This is the الجزء الثاني, though not explicitly named.
4
Apply the decomposition to an example
Take the proposition 'No writer is still-fingered while he is a writer, not always in some.' Its first part is 'No writer is still-fingered while he is a writer,' and the second part is 'not always' (i.e., non-permanence in some).
Practice decomposing other special propositions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والعرفية اللادائمةُ في البعض : قضيَّةٌ مُركَّبَةٌ مِن عرفيَّةٍ عَامَّةٍ كلّيَّةٍ، ومُطلقة عامة جزئية . أما العرفية العامَّةُ ؛ فَهِيَ الجزء الأول ، وأما المطلقة العامة الجزئية؛ فهي مفهوم اللادوامِ في البعض. وإذا عرفتَ ذلكَ ؛ فنقولُ : الخاصَّتانِ ينعكسانِ إلى العرفية العامة المقيَّدة باللادوام في البعض ؛ لأنَّه إذا صدق : لا شيء من الكاتب بساكن الأصابع ما دام كاتباً لا دائماً في البعض؛ صدق دائماً لا شيء مِنْ ساكن الأصابع بكاتب ما دام ساكناً لا دائماً في البعض. أمَّا صدقُ العرفيَّةِ العامَّةِ، وهي: لا شيء من ب ج ما دام ب؛ فَلِكونها لازمةٌ للعامتين، ولازم العام لازم الخاص. وأما صدقُ اللادوام في البعض ; فلأنَّه لو لم يصدق بعضُ ب ج بالفعل، لصدق نقيضُهُ سالبةٌ كُلِّيَّةٌ مُطلقةً دائمةً، وهي: لا شيء مِنْ ساكن الأصابع بكاتب دائماً، ويعكس ذلكَ النَّقيض إلى نفْسِهِ، وهو: لا شيء من الكاتبِ بِساكن دائماً ، وهو مُنافٍ للادوامِ في الأصلِ الصَّادقِ القائل: كُلُّ كاتب ساكن الأصابع بالضَّرورة أو دائماً، لا شيء مِنْ ج ب ما دامَ ج لا دائماً؛ صَدَقَ : لا شيءٍ مِنْ ب ج مَا دامَ ب لا دائماً في البعض. وهكذا يُقالُ في العرفية الخاصة؛ إِلَّا أَنَّكَ تبدلُ الضَّرورة بالدَّوامِ.
The customary non-permanent in some is a compound proposition consisting of a general universal customary and a general particular absolute. The general customary is the first part, and the general particular absolute is the concept of non-permanence in some. When you know this, we say: The two specials convert to the general customary restricted by non-permanence in some; because if it is true: 'No writer is still-fingered while he is a writer, not always in some,' then it is true always: 'No still-fingered person is a writer while he is still, not always in some.' As for the truth of the general customary, which is 'No B is C while it is B,' it is because it is necessary for the two generals, and the necessary of the general is necessary for the special. As for the truth of non-permanence in some, it is because if 'Some B is C in actuality' were not true, then its contradictory would be true: a universal negative absolute permanent, which is 'No still-fingered person is a writer always,' and that contradictory converts to itself, which is 'No writer is still-fingered always,' and that contradicts the non-permanence in the original true [proposition] that says: 'Every writer is still-fingered by necessity or always, no C is B while it is C not always'; then it is true: 'No B is C while it is B not always in some.' And similarly it is said for the customary special, except that you replace necessity with permanence.
Source pages p. 720

Commentary
This passage defines the 'customary non-permanent in some' proposition and then proves the conversion rule for special propositions. The author first explains that the 'customary non-permanent in some' is composed of a general customary (universal) and a general particular absolute (the 'non-permanence in some' part). Then he states that the two special propositions (the conditioned special and the customary special) each convert to a general customary proposition restricted by non-permanence in some. The proof is given for the conditioned special: take an example 'No writer is still-fingered while he is a writer, not always in some.' The converted form is 'No still-fingered person is a writer while he is still, not always in some.' The truth of the general customary part follows because it is a necessary consequent of the two general propositions (the general conditioned and the general customary), and what is necessary for the general is necessary for the special. The truth of the non-permanence part is proved by reductio: if it were false that some still-fingered person is actually a writer, then the contradictory 'No still-fingered person is a writer always' would be true. This contradictory converts to 'No writer is still-fingered always,' which contradicts the non-permanence clause in the original premise. Therefore, the non-permanence must be true. For the customary special, the same reasoning applies, replacing 'necessity' with 'permanence' in the original proposition.

مطلقة عامة جزئية
لادوام في البعض

Proving the Conversion of Special Propositions
Author proves conversion of special propositions to general customary with non-permanence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the proof that a special proposition (conditioned or customary) converts to a general customary proposition restricted by non-permanence in some.

Core Idea
The conversion of a special yields a general customary with non-permanence, not another special.

Now that we understand the structure of the target compound proposition, we turn to the proof. The author shows that both the conditioned special and the customary special convert to a general customary with a non-permanence clause. The proof has two parts: one for the general customary component and one for the non-permanence component via reductio.

Guided Path
1
State the original special proposition
Take a specific example: 'No writer is still-fingered while he is a writer, not always in some.' This is a conditioned special (with necessity) or customary special (with permanence).
The passage uses this example for the conditioned special.
2
Claim the converted proposition
The conversion yields: 'No still-fingered person is a writer while he is still, not always in some.' Note that the subject and predicate are swapped, but the modality changes from a special to a general customary with non-permanence.
This is the conclusion of the conversion rule.
3
Prove the general customary part holds
The general customary part ('No still-fingered person is a writer while he is still') is a necessary consequent of the two general propositions (the general conditioned and the general customary) that are implied by the special. Since whatever is necessary for the general is necessary for the special, this part must be true.
The author says 'لازمة للعامتين' (it is necessary for the two generals).
4
Prove the non-permanence part via reductio
Assume the non-permanence part is false, meaning 'Some still-fingered person is a writer in actuality' is false. Then its contradictory 'No still-fingered person is a writer always' is true. That proposition converts to itself: 'No writer is still-fingered always,' which contradicts the non-permanence clause in the original premise.
This shows the non-permanence part cannot be false, so it is true.
5
Apply to the customary special
The same reasoning holds for the customary special, except that in the original proposition you replace 'necessity' with 'permanence'. The conversion result is the same form.
The passage ends with 'إلا أنك تبدل الضرورة بالدوام'.

والعرفية اللادائمةُ في البعض : قضيَّةٌ مُركَّبَةٌ مِن عرفيَّةٍ عَامَّةٍ كلّيَّةٍ، ومُطلقة عامة جزئية . أما العرفية العامَّةُ ؛ فَهِيَ الجزء الأول ، وأما المطلقة العامة الجزئية؛ فهي مفهوم اللادوامِ في البعض. وإذا عرفتَ ذلكَ ؛ فنقولُ : الخاصَّتانِ ينعكسانِ إلى العرفية العامة المقيَّدة باللادوام في البعض ؛ لأنَّه إذا صدق : لا شيء من الكاتب بساكن الأصابع ما دام كاتباً لا دائماً في البعض؛ صدق دائماً لا شيء مِنْ ساكن الأصابع بكاتب ما دام ساكناً لا دائماً في البعض. أمَّا صدقُ العرفيَّةِ العامَّةِ، وهي: لا شيء من ب ج ما دام ب؛ فَلِكونها لازمةٌ للعامتين، ولازم العام لازم الخاص. وأما صدقُ اللادوام في البعض ; فلأنَّه لو لم يصدق بعضُ ب ج بالفعل، لصدق نقيضُهُ سالبةٌ كُلِّيَّةٌ مُطلقةً دائمةً، وهي: لا شيء مِنْ ساكن الأصابع بكاتب دائماً، ويعكس ذلكَ النَّقيض إلى نفْسِهِ، وهو: لا شيء من الكاتبِ بِساكن دائماً ، وهو مُنافٍ للادوامِ في الأصلِ الصَّادقِ القائل: كُلُّ كاتب ساكن الأصابع بالضَّرورة أو دائماً، لا شيء مِنْ ج ب ما دامَ ج لا دائماً؛ صَدَقَ : لا شيءٍ مِنْ ب ج مَا دامَ ب لا دائماً في البعض. وهكذا يُقالُ في العرفية الخاصة؛ إِلَّا أَنَّكَ تبدلُ الضَّرورة بالدَّوامِ.
The customary non-permanent in some is a compound proposition consisting of a general universal customary and a general particular absolute. The general customary is the first part, and the general particular absolute is the concept of non-permanence in some. When you know this, we say: The two specials convert to the general customary restricted by non-permanence in some; because if it is true: 'No writer is still-fingered while he is a writer, not always in some,' then it is true always: 'No still-fingered person is a writer while he is still, not always in some.' As for the truth of the general customary, which is 'No B is C while it is B,' it is because it is necessary for the two generals, and the necessary of the general is necessary for the special. As for the truth of non-permanence in some, it is because if 'Some B is C in actuality' were not true, then its contradictory would be true: a universal negative absolute permanent, which is 'No still-fingered person is a writer always,' and that contradictory converts to itself, which is 'No writer is still-fingered always,' and that contradicts the non-permanence in the original true [proposition] that says: 'Every writer is still-fingered by necessity or always, no C is B while it is C not always'; then it is true: 'No B is C while it is B not always in some.' And similarly it is said for the customary special, except that you replace necessity with permanence.
Source pages p. 720

Commentary
This passage defines the 'customary non-permanent in some' proposition and then proves the conversion rule for special propositions. The author first explains that the 'customary non-permanent in some' is composed of a general customary (universal) and a general particular absolute (the 'non-permanence in some' part). Then he states that the two special propositions (the conditioned special and the customary special) each convert to a general customary proposition restricted by non-permanence in some. The proof is given for the conditioned special: take an example 'No writer is still-fingered while he is a writer, not always in some.' The converted form is 'No still-fingered person is a writer while he is still, not always in some.' The truth of the general customary part follows because it is a necessary consequent of the two general propositions (the general conditioned and the general customary), and what is necessary for the general is necessary for the special. The truth of the non-permanence part is proved by reductio: if it were false that some still-fingered person is actually a writer, then the contradictory 'No still-fingered person is a writer always' would be true. This contradictory converts to 'No writer is still-fingered always,' which contradicts the non-permanence clause in the original premise. Therefore, the non-permanence must be true. For the customary special, the same reasoning applies, replacing 'necessity' with 'permanence' in the original proposition.

عكس
عرفية عامة

Converting Universal Negative with 'Not Always'
Author explains conversion of universal negative with 'not always'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to perform simple conversion on a universal negative proposition with a temporal modality and determine the quantity of the converse.

Core Idea
A universal negative with 'not always' converts to a universal negative restricted to 'not always in some cases.'

The author introduces the rule for converting universal negative propositions qualified by temporal conditions. This skill is essential for evaluating arguments involving modal propositions, common in kalam and usul al-fiqh. The passage provides a direct example of such a conversion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original proposition
The original is 'No J is B as long as J is not always.' Note the subject (J), predicate (B), and the temporal condition 'not always.'
The condition 'as long as J is not always' means the proposition holds only when J is not always existent or true.
2
Swap subject and predicate
Simple conversion exchanges the terms: the new subject is B, the new predicate is J. The result is 'No B is J...'
3
Adapt the temporal condition
The condition 'as long as J is not always' shifts to 'as long as B is not always' because the condition attaches to the subject of the converse.
The condition modifies the new subject B.
4
Determine the quantity
The original is universal, but the 'not always' condition indicates the proposition is not permanently true. Therefore, the converse is not fully universal; it is restricted to 'in some cases' (في البعض). The correct converse is 'No B is J as long as B is not always in some cases.'
The phrase 'in some cases' makes the converse particular in scope, though still negative.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: فِي الْعَكْسِ الْمُسْتَوِي. بالضَّرورة أو دائماً، لا شيء مِنْ ج ب ما دامَ ج لا دائماً»؛ صَدَقَ : «لا شيءٍ مِنْ ب ج مَا دامَ ب لا دائماً في البعض». أمَّا صدقُ العرفيَّةِ العامَّةِ، وهي: لا شيء من ب ج ما دام ب»؛ فَلِكونها لازمةٌ للعامتين، ولازم العام لازم الخاص. وأما صدقُ اللأدوام في البعض ; فلأنَّه لو لم يصدق بعضُ ب ج بالفعل»؛ لصَدَقَ لا شيء من ب ج دائماً»، وينعكس إلى: «لا شيء من ج ب دائماً، دائماً، وقد كان كلُّ ج ب بالفعل بحكم لا دوام الأصل. وإِنَّما لم تنعكسا إلى العرفية العامة المقيَّدَةِ باللادوام في الكلِّ؛ لأنَّ اللادوامَ في السَّالبتّينِ الكلِّيَّتين إشارة إلى مطلقة عامة موجبة كلية، والموجبةُ الكلّيَّة تنعكس جزئيَّةٌ، تأمل.
Section: On Simple Conversion. By necessity or always, 'No J is B as long as J is not always' is true; so 'No B is J as long as B is not always in some cases' is true. As for the truth of the general conventional (proposition), which is 'No B is J as long as B is', it is because it is necessary for the two general (propositions), and what is necessary for the general is necessary for the specific. As for the truth of the 'not always' in some, it is because if 'Some B is J' were not true in actuality, then 'No B is J always' would be true, and it converts to 'No J is B always, always', and it was 'Every J is B' in actuality by virtue of the 'not always' of the original. And they do not convert to the general conventional (proposition) restricted by 'not always' in all, because 'not always' in the two universal negatives indicates a general absolute universal affirmative, and the universal affirmative converts to a particular; reflect.
Source pages p. 721

Commentary
This passage is an expansion on the rules of simple conversion ('aks mustawi) for universal negative propositions with temporal qualifications. The author begins by stating the conversion rule for a universal negative with 'by necessity or always' and 'not always' condition: if 'No J is B as long as J is not always' is true, then its converse 'No B is J as long as B is not always in some cases' is true. The phrase 'in some cases' indicates a particular conversion. Next, he addresses the truth of the 'urfiya 'amma' (general conventional) proposition, which is 'No B is J as long as B is'. This proposition follows necessarily from the two general propositions (the original and its converse?), because the necessary of a general is necessary for the specific. Then he explains why 'not always' is true in the converse: if 'Some B is J' were false in actuality (i.e., no B is J at any time), then 'No B is J always' would be true, which converts to 'No J is B always, always', but the original had 'Every J is B' in actuality due to the 'not always' condition. This shows the conversion preserves the 'not always' modality. Finally, the author notes why these two universal negatives do not convert to a general conventional proposition restricted by 'not always' in all: because the 'not always' in the two universal negatives indicates a general absolute universal affirmative (i.e., a universal affirmative without temporal restriction), and a universal affirmative converts to a particular affirmative. Thus, the converse cannot be a universal. He signs off with 'ta'ammul' (reflect), urging the student to ponder the nuances.

Key Terms
الْعَكْسُ الْمُسْتَوِي
لَا دَائِمَاً

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

C) Some B is J always
D) No B is J as long as B is not always

Key Takeaway
A universal negative with 'not always' converts to a universal negative with 'not always in some cases,' i.e., a particularized universal.

Why the Conversion Is Not to the General Conventional
Author explains why conversion to 'urfiya 'amma fails.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a universal negative with 'not always' does not convert to a general conventional proposition and identify the role of the underlying universal affirmative.

Core Idea
The 'not always' in universal negatives indicates a universal affirmative, which converts to a particular.

The author elaborates on why the converse of a universal negative with 'not always' cannot be the general conventional proposition (العرفية العامة) and how the 'not always' condition is preserved. Understanding this nuance prevents common mistakes in immediate inference, especially in fields like usul al-fiqh where proposition conversion is frequent.

Guided Path
1
Understand the general conventional proposition
The general conventional proposition (العرفية العامة) is 'No B is J as long as B is' — a universal negative that holds for the entire duration of B's existence.
This proposition is 'general' because the condition is tied to the subject's existence at any time.
2
Recognize the implication of 'not always'
The 'not always' condition in a universal negative indicates that at some times the universal negative is false, implying an affirmative. The text says: 'اللادوام في السالبتين الكليتين إشارة إلى مطلقة عامة موجبة كلية' — i.e., the 'not always' in the two universal negatives points to a general absolute universal affirmative.
This means the condition effectively signals that an affirmative universal proposition is true at some level.
3
Apply the conversion rule for universal affirmative
A universal affirmative converts to a particular affirmative. Therefore, the converse of the implied universal affirmative is a particular.
4
Conclude why the general conventional is not the converse
Since the 'not always' condition yields a universal affirmative which converts to a particular, the converse cannot be a universal negative like the 'urfiya 'amma. Instead, it must be the particularized universal negative taught in the previous lesson.
The general conventional would be a universal negative without the 'not always' restriction, which does not follow.

Conversion of Universal Negatives with La Dawam
Author explains conversion rule for universal negatives with la dawam.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a universal negative proposition with 'la dawam' does not convert to a universal general conventional proposition, and identify the role of the universal affirmative in the conversion process.

Core Idea
'La dawam' in a universal negative signals a universal affirmative, making its conversion particular, not universal.

This passage addresses a subtle point in immediate inference: why two universal negatives with the modal condition 'la dawam' (non-permanence) do not convert to the general conventional (al-'urfiyya al-'amma) with the same condition. The reasoning hinges on the fact that 'la dawam' in universal negatives actually qualifies a universal affirmative proposition, and universal affirmatives only convert to particular affirmatives. The commentators Dassuqi and 'Attar expand on this, clarifying the type of proof used and addressing a potential counterargument.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposition type
The passage discusses 'two universal negatives' (sālibatān kulliyyatān) with the modal condition 'la dawam'. Recognize this as a universal negative proposition with a modal qualification.
The condition 'la dawam' indicates non-permanence.
2
Understand the claim
The claim is that this proposition does NOT convert to a general conventional universal with la dawam. Consider what the expected conversion might be: perhaps another universal negative with la dawam.
3
Extract the key reason
The reason: 'la dawam in the two universal negatives refers to a universal affirmative absolute general.' This means the modal condition transforms the negative into an affirmative in terms of the underlying modality.
The original proposition is equivalent to a universal affirmative proposition (e.g., 'Every S is not P' in a non-permanent way).
4
Apply the conversion rule
Universal affirmatives convert to particular affirmatives (by the rule of simple conversion: 'Every S is P' gives 'Some P is S'). Therefore, the converted proposition must be a particular, not a universal. Hence, it cannot be a universal conventional with la dawam.
Rule: A universal affirmative (A) converts to a particular affirmative (I).
5
Examine the commentary
Dassuqi notes that this is dalīl al-aks (proof by conversion), not khalf. 'Attar addresses a potential objection: that la dawam in the conversion should be universal affirmative, but the answer is that the whole proposition (the complex) is converted, not just the modal part.
The conversion is of the entire proposition, not the modal qualifier alone.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَإِنَّمَا لَمْ تَنْعَكِسَا إِلَى الْعُرْفِيَّةِ الْعَامَّةِ الْمُقَيَّدَةِ بِاللادَوَامِ فِي الْكُلِّ؛ لأنَّ اللادوامَ فِي السَّالِبَتَيْنِ الْكُلِّيَّتَيْن إشارة إلى مطلقة عامة موجبة كلية، والموجبة الكلية تنعكس جزئية، تأمل. الدسوقي (قَوْلُهُ : وَيَنْعَكِسُ ... إلخ ) حينئذ؛ فهذا الدَّليلُ يُقال له : دليل العكس لا الخلف . (قَوْلُهُ : لَا شَيْءٍ مِنْ ج ب يظهرُ مِمَّا قرَّرْنَا : أنَّ (ج) عبارةٌ عن كاتب، وأنَّ (ب) عبارةٌ عن ساكن الأصابع. (قَوْلُهُ : وَإِنَّمَا لَمْ تَنْعَكِسَا ) أي : العامَّتان (۱). (قَوْلُهُ : تَأَمَّلْ) أشَارَ بِهِ لِنظر، وحاصله : أنَّ المجموع عكس للمجموع كَمَا يعلمُ من كلامهم، والأصلُ كُلِّيَّة، فليكن العكس كذلك العطار (قَوْلُه: وَإِنَّمَا لَمْ تَنْعَكِسَا إِلَى الْعُرْفِيَّةِ الْعَامَّةِ جواب عَمَّا يقالُ : إِنَّ اللادوامَ إشارة إلى مُطلقةٍ عامَّةٍ مُخالفة في الكيف مُوافقة في الكمّ كَمَا تقدَّمَ في بحث الموجهات، ولا دوام في العكس جعل قيد السَّالبةِ كُلِّيَّةٌ، فَحَقَّهُ أنْ يكونَ مُوجبةٌ كُلِّيَّةٌ، كما أَنَّهُ في الأصل كَذَلِك، وحاصل الجواب : أن لا دوام في العكس؛ عكس لا دوام في الأصل، واللادوامَ في الأصل موجبةٌ كُلِّيَّةٌ، والموجبة الكُلِّيَّةُ تنعكس موجبةً جزئيةً، وفيه نظَرُ ؛ لأنَّ لا دوام ليس عكساً للأدوام؛ بل لمجموع للمجموع، كيف والكلامُ في عكس السَّوالي؟ وَلَو لم يكن المجموع قضيَّةٌ لما قيل: العبرة في الإيجاب والسَّلْب بالجزء الأوَّلِ، أفاده المحشّي.
And they only did not convert to the general conventional restricted by la dawam in the whole because la dawam in the two universal negatives refers to a universal affirmative absolute general, and the universal affirmative converts to a particular. Reflect. Dassuqi: (His saying: and it converts... etc.) then; this proof is called proof of conversion, not proof by contradiction. (His saying: nothing of B is C) it appears from what we established: that (J) is an expression for a writer, and that (B) is an expression for one who moves his fingers. (His saying: they only did not convert) i.e., the two general ones. (His saying: reflect) he indicated by it a consideration, and its summary: that the whole is the conversion of the whole as known from their statement, and the original is universal, so let the conversion be likewise. Al-'Attar: (His saying: they only did not convert to the general conventional) is an answer to what is said: that la dawam refers to a general absolute differing in quality but agreeing in quantity as previously mentioned in the discussion of modal propositions, and la dawam in the conversion is made a condition of the universal negative, so it should be a universal affirmative, as it is in the original likewise. The summary of the answer: that la dawam in the conversion is the conversion of la dawam in the original, and la dawam in the original is a universal affirmative, and the universal affirmative converts to a particular affirmative. In it there is a consideration because la dawam is not a conversion of ad-dawam but rather a conversion of the whole for the whole. How, while the discussion is about the conversion of the two negatives? And if the whole were not a proposition, it would not be said: the consideration in affirmation and negation is by the first part, as the commentator has informed us.
Source pages p. 722

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of why two specific universal negative propositions do not convert to the general conventional (al-'urfiyya al-'amma) with the condition of la dawam (non-permanence) in the whole. The author begins by stating the rule: the two universal negatives (i.e., the two particular universal negatives? Actually, the context refers to the two universal negatives with la dawam) do not convert to the general conventional with la dawam. The reason is that la dawam in universal negatives is a sign of a universal affirmative absolute general proposition (muṭlaqa 'amma). Since a universal affirmative only converts to a particular affirmative (by the rule of conversion of universal affirmatives), the conversion of these negatives would yield a particular, not a universal. The commentator Dassuqi then clarifies that the proof used here is called 'proof of conversion' (dalīl al-'aks) not 'proof by contradiction' (dalīl al-khalf). He also notes that in the example, the variable J stands for 'writer' and B stands for 'one who moves his fingers'. Al-'Attar then gives a more detailed answer: some might argue that la dawam should convert to a universal affirmative, but the correct answer is that la dawam in the conversion is the conversion of la dawam in the original, and since the original la dawam is a universal affirmative, it converts to a particular. There is a nuance: la dawam is not a simple conversion of ad-dawam, but the conversion of the whole proposition. The commentator emphasizes that the conversion is of the entire complex, not just the modal part. This guards against the misunderstanding that modal conditions in conversion apply independently of the whole proposition.

عكس مستوي
عرفية عامة
لا دوام
سالبة كلية
موجبة كلية

A) Because 'la dawam' in the original is a universal affirmative, which converts to a particular affirmative.
B) Because conversion is not allowed for modal propositions.
C) Because the proposition is actually a particular negative.
D) Because the subject and predicate are not convertible.

Key Takeaway
The modal condition 'la dawam' in a universal negative makes it equivalent to a universal affirmative, which only converts to a particular affirmative, not a universal.

Conversion of Universal Negatives: Conditions and Counterexample
Commentators refine conversion conditions for universal negatives.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a universal negative proposition converts to a universal or particular negative based on its modality and independence from the passage's analysis.

Core Idea
Modality governs conversion: absolute universal negatives convert universally; modal ones may not.

The rule for converting universal negatives seems straightforward: swap subject and predicate while keeping quantity universal. However, as al-Attar and Dassuqi show, this holds only for independent, absolute propositions. When the proposition is modal—like the non-permanent (la dawam) universal negative—the conversion yields a particular negation. In this lesson, we dissect their reasoning and learn to spot the difference.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposition type
Determine if the universal negative is absolute (e.g., 'No writer is a permanent-finger' without temporal qualification) or modal (e.g., 'No writer is a permanent-finger as long as he is a writer, not permanently'). This classification is the first key.
Absoluteness (independent) vs. dependency (modal) is the dividing line.
2
Check independence following Dassuqi
Dassuqi states that the conversion of a universal negative yields a universal only when the proposition is independent (mustaqilla), not dependent (tabi'a). Notice how he ties the universality of the converse to the independence of the original.
An independent proposition is one that holds without qualification.
3
Analyze al-Attar's counterexample
Al-Attar provides a case: 'Nothing of the writer is permanent-finger as long as he is a writer, not permanently' is true, but its converse 'Nothing of the permanent-finger is a writer as long as he is permanent-finger, not permanently' is false for some instances (e.g., the earth). This shows the conversion fails for universal non-permanent negatives.
The earth is permanent-finger (i.e., has permanent fingers) but is not a writer, so the converse would falsely claim that no permanent-finger is a writer permanently, which is false because some permanent-fingers are not writers at all.
4
Understand why the non-permanent negative is affirmative
The secret (sirruhu) is that the non-permanent negative (saliba la dawamiyya) is actually an affirmative in meaning—it asserts that the predicate holds sometimes. Affirmative propositions convert only to particulars. Hence the converse must be particular, not universal.
This resolves the counterexample: the original modal negative is semantically affirmative, so its conversion follows the rule for affirmatives.

(قَوْلُهُ : وَالْبَيَانُ فِي الْكُلِّ أي : المجموع، أو المراد أنَّه يجري في الموجباتِ والسوالب ، وليس المرادُ أنَّهُ يعمُّ كُلّ فردٍ منها ؛ لأنَّهُ لا يَجري في عكس لا دوام الخاصَّتَيْنِ، وَلِذلك قرَّرَ الشَّارحُ في عكس لا دوام في البعضِ الَّذي هو عكس (۱) (قولُ العطّار : لأنَّ بعضَ ... إلخ) فيه أنَّ الموضوع لم يتَّحِدْ إذ الأرضُ لم تدخل تحت مفهوم اللادوام في الكُلِّ المشار إليهِ بِقَولِنَا : كُلُّ ساكن الأصابع كاتب بالإطلاق حتَّى يرد هذا نقضاً ، وَقَولُهُ: وَسِرُّهُ ... إلخ ؛ يردُّهُ مَا قالَهُ المحشَّي يس آنفاً، وَقَد ردَّهُ العطَّارُ بهذا، والَّذي أراهُ أنَّ تقييد اللادوام بالبعض فهماً مِنهُم أَنَّهُ عكس اللدوام في الأصل، وهو موجبةٌ كُلِّيَّةٌ، فَلَا ينعكس إلا جزئيَّةٌ؛ خطأ بَيِّن، إذ العكس إنَّما هو المجموع للمجموع، وعليه فيصح عكس الخاصَّتين السالبتين عرفيَّةٌ لا دائمة في الكُلِّ لا في البعض عكس ما يقولون، فإنَّهُ إذا صدق بالضَّرورة أو دائماً لا شيء مِنَ الكاتب بساكن الأصابع ما دام كاتباً لا دائماً ؛ وجبَ أنْ يصدق دائماً لا شيء من ساكن الأصابع بكاتبٍ مَا دَامَ ساكن الأصابع لا دائماً الكُلِّ ، أمَّا صِدْقُ الجزء الأول؛ فَلِأَنَّهُ لازم لِلعامَّتَين، ولازم العام لازم للخاص، وأمَّا صدقُ اللأدوام في الكُلِّ: أي: كُلُّ ساكن الأصابع كاتب بالفعل المنعكس إلى بعض الكاتب ساكن الأصابع بالفعل؛ فَلِأَنَّهُ لَوْ لم يصدَقْ لَصَدَقٌ نَقيضُ هذا العكس، وهو : لَا شَيْء مِنَ الكاتب يساكن الأصابع دائماً، وهو مُنافٍ للأدوامٍ في الأصل القائل: كُلُّ كاتب ساكنُ الأصابع في لِلخاصَّتَيْنِ ; دليل العكس دون دليل الخلفِ واعلَمْ أَنَّ لِلْقَوْمِ...
