And as for the truth of the non-permanence in all: i.e., every permanent-finger is a writer actually converted to some of the writer is permanent-finger actually; because if it were not true, then the contradictory of this converse would be true, which is: Nothing of the writer is permanent-finger permanently, and it contradicts the permanence in the original which says: Every writer is permanent-finger in the two properties; proof of conversion without proof of khulf.
Source pages p. 723

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical discussion on the conversion of universal negative propositions, specifically focusing on the method of proof called 'khulf' (proof by contradiction). The commentators are refining the understanding of when and how conversion works, particularly for non-permanent (la dawam) propositions. The text opens with a comment on the phrase 'wal-bayan fi al-kull' (the explanation in all), interpreting it as the proof of conversion for all types of propositions. Dassuqi proposes that the subject of conversion for a universal negative is universal only when the proposition is independent, not dependent. Al-Attar then intervenes with a quote from the author's commentary on the Epistle, arguing that universal negative propositions that are non-permanent do not convert to common universal non-permanent propositions. He provides an example: 'Nothing of the writer is permanent-finger as long as he is a writer, not permanently' is true, but its converse 'Nothing of the permanent-finger is a writer as long as he is permanent-finger, not permanently' is false in all (i.e., for every permanent-finger), because some permanent things like the earth are not writers permanently. He then states that the non-permanent negative is actually an affirmative (since 'not permanently' implies sometimes), and affirmatives only convert to particulars. The commentator 'Attar clarifies that 'al-bayan fi al-kull' means the aggregate, or that it applies to both affirmatives and negatives, not to every individual. He then criticizes the restriction of non-permanence to some, arguing that the original conversion is of the aggregate, and that the two negative particular properties convert as common non-permanent in all, not in some. He provides a detailed reasoning: if the original is true, then the converse must be true; if not, its contradictory would contradict the original. This is essentially the khulf method. The passage ends with a note that this is the proof of conversion without khulf, though the context suggests that khulf is still the underlying principle.

The Khulf Method: Proving Conversion by Contradiction
Proof of conversion using the khulf method for modal universals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the khulf (proof by contradiction) argument used to validate the conversion of modal universal negatives, as presented in the passage.

Core Idea
Khulf proves conversion by showing the contradictory of the converse contradicts the original, forcing the converse to be true.

In the previous lesson, we saw that non-permanent universal negatives do not convert universally. However, the commentators still affirm that a correct conversion exists using the khulf method. Here, we examine the detailed logical proof: assuming the converse is false leads to a contradiction with the original proposition, thus proving the converse must be true. This lesson walks through that proof step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original and proposed converse
The original proposition is a universal negative with modality: e.g., 'Nothing of the writer is a permanent-finger as long as he is a writer, not permanently.' The proposed converse (the one to be proven) is: 'Nothing of the permanent-finger is a writer as long as he is permanent-finger, not permanently in all.' Note that 'in all' means universally.
The original and converse share the same non-permanent modality.
2
Assume the contradictory of the converse
To use khulf, assume the converse is false. Its contradictory is: 'Some permanent-finger is a writer permanently' (i.e., there exists a permanent-finger that is a writer all the time). This is the negation of the universal negative.
Khulf works by assuming the opposite of what we want to prove and deriving a contradiction.
3
Derive a contradiction from the original
From the original 'No writer is a permanent-finger...', we know that any writer, while writing, is not a permanent-finger permanently. Now, if some permanent-finger were a writer permanently, then that thing would be both a writer and a permanent-finger permanently, contradicting the original. Thus the assumption leads to inconsistency.
The original implies that at any moment a writer is writing, he is not a permanent-finger permanently; the assumed converse contradicts this.
4
Conclude the converse must be true
Since the contradictory of the converse leads to a contradiction with the original, the converse itself must be true. This is the khulf method: proof by contradiction.
The passage states: 'if it were not true, then the contradictory of this converse would be true, and it contradicts the permanence in the original.'

For if it is true necessarily or permanently that Nothing of the writer is permanent-finger as long as he is a writer not permanently; then it must be true permanently that Nothing of the permanent-finger is a writer as long as he is permanent-finger not permanently in all. As for the truth of the first part; it is because it is necessary for the two general propositions, and the necessary of the general is necessary for the particular. And as for the truth of the non-permanence in all: i.e., every permanent-finger is a writer actually converted to some of the writer is permanent-finger actually; because if it were not true, then the contradictory of this converse would be true, which is: Nothing of the writer is permanent-finger permanently, and it contradicts the permanence in the original which says: Every writer is permanent-finger in the two properties; proof of conversion without proof of khulf.
Source pages p. 723

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical discussion on the conversion of universal negative propositions, specifically focusing on the method of proof called 'khulf' (proof by contradiction). The commentators are refining the understanding of when and how conversion works, particularly for non-permanent (la dawam) propositions. The text opens with a comment on the phrase 'wal-bayan fi al-kull' (the explanation in all), interpreting it as the proof of conversion for all types of propositions. Dassuqi proposes that the subject of conversion for a universal negative is universal only when the proposition is independent, not dependent. Al-Attar then intervenes with a quote from the author's commentary on the Epistle, arguing that universal negative propositions that are non-permanent do not convert to common universal non-permanent propositions. He provides an example: 'Nothing of the writer is permanent-finger as long as he is a writer, not permanently' is true, but its converse 'Nothing of the permanent-finger is a writer as long as he is permanent-finger, not permanently' is false in all (i.e., for every permanent-finger), because some permanent things like the earth are not writers permanently. He then states that the non-permanent negative is actually an affirmative (since 'not permanently' implies sometimes), and affirmatives only convert to particulars. The commentator 'Attar clarifies that 'al-bayan fi al-kull' means the aggregate, or that it applies to both affirmatives and negatives, not to every individual. He then criticizes the restriction of non-permanence to some, arguing that the original conversion is of the aggregate, and that the two negative particular properties convert as common non-permanent in all, not in some. He provides a detailed reasoning: if the original is true, then the converse must be true; if not, its contradictory would contradict the original. This is essentially the khulf method. The passage ends with a note that this is the proof of conversion without khulf, though the context suggests that khulf is still the underlying principle.

Key Terms
الخلف
البيان
اللادوام
السالبة الكلية
الموجبة الجزئية

Key Takeaway
Khulf proves conversion by assuming the opposite of the desired converse and showing it contradicts the original, thereby confirming the converse.

Three Methods for Proving Conversion
Author enumerates and distinguishes the three methods: khulf, 'aks, and iftiraad.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three methods (khulf, 'aks, iftirad) for proving conversion of propositions and identify which method is appropriate for a given proposition type.

Core Idea
Three methods prove conversion: contradiction, contradictory conversion, and supposition.

In this passage, the author introduces the three methods logicians use to prove that a proposition's converse is true. Understanding these methods is crucial for mastering immediate inference, which is foundational for syllogistic reasoning. We will examine each method's procedure and its scope of applicability.

Guided Path
1
List the three methods
Identify al-khulf, al-'aks, and al-iftirad as the three methods. Notice that each has a distinct procedure and logical basis.
2
Understand al-khulf (proof by contradiction)
This method joins the contradictory of the desired converse (or its part) with the original proposition (or its part) to derive an absurdity. The impossibility of that absurdity proves the converse.
Khulf works for all propositions: affirmative/negative, simple/compound.
3
Understand al-'aks (conversion of the contradictory)
This method takes the contradictory of the converse, converts it (via simple conversion), and obtains a proposition that contradicts the original. The contradiction proves the converse.
The passage warns that this method is circular if used for both affirmatives and negatives together.
4
Understand al-iftirad (supposition)
This method supposes a specific individual that instantiates the subject term. Then it checks whether both the subject and predicate descriptions apply to that individual. If they do, the converse is true.
Iftirad only works for compound propositions (affirmative or negative).
5
Compare the scopes
Note why the author prefers khulf: it avoids the circularity of 'aks and covers all proposition types, unlike iftirad which is limited to compound propositions.

(قَوْلُهُ : وَهَذَا الْبَيَانُ يُسَمَّى بِالْخُلْفِ بِضَمِّ الْخَاءِ بِمَعْنَى الْبَاطِلِ؛ لِأَنَّهُ يُنْتِجُ بَاطِلاً، وَبِفَتْحِهَا بِمَعْنَى وَرَاءَ؛ لِأَنَّ مَا يُنْتِجُهُ يُنْبَذُ إِلَى خَلْفٍ؛ أَيْ: وَرَاءَ . (قَوْلُهُ : وَهُوَ إثْبَاتُ الْمَطْلُوبِ بِإِبْطَالِ نَقِبْضِهِ) سَوَاءٌ كَانَ الْإِبْطَالُ بِضَمِّ نَقِيضِ الْعَكْسِ مَعَ الْأَصْلِ لِيُنْتِجَ مُحَالاً، أَوْ بِعَكْسِ النَّقِيضِ لِيَحْصُلَ بِانْعِكَاسِهِ مَا يُنَافِي الْأَصْلَ.
Know that for the people there are three methods in explaining the conversion of propositions: The first is al-khulf (proof by contradiction): it is the joining of the contradictory of the converse or its part to the original or to its part to produce the impossible. The second is al-'aks (conversion of the contradictory): it is that you convert the contradictory of the converse or its two parts to obtain something that contradicts the original. The third is al-iftirad (supposition): it is that we suppose the subject's essence as a specific thing and each of the two attributes (subject and predicate) is predicated of it until the truth of the meaning of the converse becomes clear. And since the proof of khulf is applicable in affirmatives and negatives, simple and compound, and it is possible to explain their conversion by it without necessitating a circle, the author limited himself to it here, unlike the proof of 'aks, because explaining the conversion of all by it necessitates a circle; necessarily because explaining the conversion of affirmatives by it depends on knowledge of the conversion of negatives and vice versa. And supposition does not apply except in compound affirmatives and negatives. (His statement: And this explanation is called al-khulf, with a damma on the kha' meaning falsehood; because it produces falsehood, and with a fatha meaning 'behind'; because what it produces is cast behind; i.e., backward.) (His statement: And it is the establishment of the desired by falsifying its contradictory) whether the falsification is by joining the contradictory of the converse with the original to produce an impossibility, or by converting the contradictory to obtain by its conversion something that contradicts the original.
Source pages p. 724

Commentary
This passage is a definitional enumeration of the three methods used by logicians to prove the conversion of propositions. The author lists them in order: (1) al-khulf (proof by contradiction): take the contradictory of the desired converse and combine it with the original proposition (or its part) to derive an absurdity, thus proving the converse. (2) al-'aks (conversion of the contradictory): convert the contradictory of the converse (i.e., perform inverse conversion) to obtain a proposition that contradicts the original. (3) al-iftirad (supposition): assume a specific individual that satisfies the subject term, then test whether both the subject and predicate descriptions apply to it; if they do, the converse is true. After listing them, the passage explains why the author (of the matn) confined himself to the khulf method: it works for all types of propositions (affirmative/negative, simple/compound) and does not lead to circular reasoning. In contrast, the 'aks method is circular because proving the conversion of affirmatives by it depends on prior knowledge of the conversion of negatives, and vice versa. The supposition method is limited to compound propositions. The passage then gives a brief etymology of the word 'khulf' (with damma meaning falsehood, with fatha meaning behind) and clarifies that the method works either by combining the contradictory with the original or by converting the contradictory itself.

Key Terms
الخلف
العكس (كطريقة)
الافتراض

Understanding al-Khalf: Reductio ad Absurdum
Defines al-khalf and explains its application in proving conversion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the logical structure of the khalf method for proving conversion and apply it to a simple categorical proposition.

Core Idea
Al-khalf proves the converse by assuming its opposite leads to an absurdity.

The method of al-khalf (reductio ad absurdum) is one of the primary tools used by logicians to establish the validity of propositional conversion. In this lesson, we will dissect its definition and logical mechanism as explained in the commentary. We will see how joining the contradictory of the converse with the original premise yields an absurdity, forcing the truth of the converse.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposition type
Determine whether the proposition to be converted is affirmative or negative, simple or compound. This affects how the contradictory is used as a premise.
Affirmatives and negatives have different conversion rules.
2
State the converse
Write the proposition you aim to prove (the converse). For example, from 'No S is P' the converse is 'No P is S'.
3
Assume its contradictory
Take the negation of the converse. For 'No P is S', the contradictory is 'Some P is S'.
The contradictory must be the exact logical opposite.
4
Combine with the original
Join the contradictory with the original proposition. The commentary specifies that if the contradictory is affirmative, make it the minor premise; if negative, make it the major.
This forms a syllogism that yields an absurd conclusion.
5
Derive absurdity
Show that the combination leads to a contradiction or an absurdity (e.g., both affirming and denying the same thing).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الموجبة والسالبة (أَنَّ نَقِيضَ العَكْسِ مَعَ الأَصْل يُنْتِجُ الْمُحَالَ). وهذا البيانُ يسمَّى بالخُلفِ ، وهو : إثباتُ المطلوب بإبطال نقيضه على ما سيجيء في القياس .
الدسوقي
(قَوْلُهُ : أَنَّ نَقِيضَ الْعَكْسِ مَعَ الْأَصْلِ) أي : يُجعلُ ذلكَ النَّقيضُ صُغرى إِنْ كَانَ مُوجبةٌ كما في عكس السَّوالب ، وكُبرى إنْ كانَ سالبة كما في عكس الموجبات.
(قَوْلُهُ : الْمَطْلُوبِ :أي: العكس.
(قَوْلُهُ : وَهُوَ) أي : النَّقيض.
العطار
لِلخاصَّتَيْنِ ؛ دليل العكس دون دليل الخلفِ واعلَمْ أَنَّ لِلقَومِ في بيان انعكاس القضايا طُرُقَاً ثلاثة، أحدها : الخلفُ ؛ وَهُوَ ضَمُّ نقيض العكس أو جزئه إلى الأصل أو إلى جزئه ؛ لينتج المحال، وثانيها : العكسُ ؛ وَهُوَ أنْ تعكس نقيض العكس أو جزئيه ليحصلَ مَا يُنافي الأصل وثالثها الافتراض؛ وَهُوَ أَنْ نفرضَ ذَاتَ الموضوع شيئاً مُعيَّناً ويُحملَ كُلَّ واحدٍ مِن وَصفَي الموضوع والمحمول عليه حتّى يتَّضِحَ صَدقُ مفهوم العكس، وَلَمَّا كانَ دليل الخلفِ جارياً في الموجبات والسوالب؛ بَسيطها ومركبها ، وأمكن بيان انعكاسها بِهِ مِنْ غيرِ لزومِ دورٍ ؛ اقتصرَ المصنِّفُ عليه هنا بخلافِ برهان العكس، فإنَّ بيان انعكاس الكُلِّ بِهِ يستلزمُ الدَّورَ؛ ضرورة أنَّ بيان انعكاس الموجبات بِهِ يتوقف على معرفة انعكاس السوالب وبالعكس، والافتراضُ لا يجري إلا في الموجبات والسوالب المركَّبة. (قَوْلُهُ : وَهَذَا الْبَيَانُ يُسَمَّى بِالْخُلْفِ بضمّ الخاء بمعنى الباطل؛ لأنَّه يُنتج باطلاً، وَبِفَتْحِها بمعنى وراء؛ لأنَّ مَا يُنتجه يُنبَذُ إلى خَلْف؛ أي: وراء . (قَوْلُهُ : وَهُوَ إثْبَاتُ الْمَطْلُوبِ بِإِبْطَالِ نَقِبْضِهِ) سواءٌ كان الإبطال بضمّ نقيض العكس معَ الأصل لينتج محالاً، أو بعكس النَّقيض؛ ليحصل بانعكاسه ما يُنافي الأصل، وهذا هو السر في أمر العلامة الشارح بالتأمل لا ما يقولونَ .ا.هـ. الشَّرنوبي.
وحاصلُهُ : أَنَّه لو لم يصدقِ العكسُ لَصَدَقَ نقيضُه، وهو مع الأصل الْمُحالَ ـ كما ذكرنا غيرَ مرَّةٍ - والمُحالُ ناشئ من نقيض العكس، فيلزم صِدقُ العكس.
The affirmative and negative (that the contradictory of the converse with the original produces an absurdity). This statement is called al-khalf, and it is: establishing the required by nullifying its contradictory, as will come in the syllogism. Dusuqi: His statement: 'that the contradictory of the converse with the original' means: that contradictory is made the minor if it is affirmative as in the conversion of negatives, and the major if it is negative as in the conversion of affirmatives. His statement: 'the required' means the converse. His statement: 'and it' means the contradictory. 'Attar: For the two properties; the proof of the converse without the proof of al-khalf. And know that the people have three methods in explaining the conversion of propositions: first, al-khalf, which is joining the contradictory of the converse or its part to the original or its part to produce an absurdity; second, al-'aks, which is that you convert the contradictory of the converse or its parts to obtain what contradicts the original; third, al-iftirad, which is that we assume the subject to be a particular thing and we predicate each of the two descriptions (of subject and predicate) upon it until the truth of the concept of the converse becomes clear. And since the proof of al-khalf is applicable to affirmatives and negatives, simple and compound, and the explanation of their conversion by it is possible without necessitating circularity, the author limited himself to it here, unlike the proof of al-'aks, for explaining the conversion of all by it necessitates circularity necessarily, because explaining the conversion of affirmatives by it depends on knowing the conversion of negatives and vice versa. And al-iftirad applies only to affirmatives and compound negatives. His statement: 'And this explanation is called al-khalf' with damm of khā' meaning false, because it produces falsehood; and with fatḥ meaning behind, because what it produces is cast behind. His statement: 'And it is establishing the required by nullifying its contradictory' whether the nullification is by joining the contradictory of the converse with the original to produce an absurdity, or by converting the contradictory to obtain by its conversion what contradicts the original, and this is the secret in the order of the commentator for reflection, not as they say. End of al-Sharnubi. Its summary: that if the converse were not true, its contradictory would be true, and it together with the original is absurd, as we have mentioned repeatedly, and the absurdity arises from the contradictory of the converse, so the truth of the converse necessarily follows.
Source pages p. 724

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on the method of proving propositional conversion (al-'aks) via reductio ad absurdum (al-khalf). It begins by stating the core principle: assuming the contradictory of the converse together with the original premise leads to an absurdity, thus proving the converse. Dusuqi clarifies how the contradictory is used as a premise depending on its quantity. 'Attar then enumerates three methods for proving conversion: (1) al-khalf, which combines the contradictory with the original; (2) al-'aks, which involves converting the contradictory to obtain a contradiction with the original; and (3) al-iftirad, which tests a particular instance. The author chose al-khalf because it works for all proposition types without circularity, unlike al-'aks which requires prior knowledge of the conversion of the opposite type, and al-iftirad which only applies to compound propositions. The passage also discusses the etymology of 'khalf' (falsehood or behind) and emphasizes that the method can be implemented either by direct combination or by converting the contradictory. The summary reiterates the logical structure: if the converse were false, its contradictory would be true, leading to absurdity with the original, so the converse must be true.

العكس
نقيض
محال

Three Methods of Proving Conversion: Khalf, 'Aks, and Iftirad
Enumerates and evaluates three methods of proving conversion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three methods of proving conversion (khalf, 'aks, iftirad) and explain why the author chose khalf as the primary method.

Core Idea
Among three methods, khalf is universal; 'aks is circular; iftirad is limited.

The logicians developed three distinct methods to prove that propositions convert. This lesson compares them, highlighting their strengths and weaknesses. The author of the matn preferred khalf because it applies to all proposition types without circularity. Understanding this comparison helps you choose the right tool for proving conversions in Islamic logical arguments.

Guided Path
1
List the three methods
From the passage, identify: (1) al-khalf – assuming the contradictory of the converse and deriving an absurdity; (2) al-'aks – converting the contradictory of the converse to get a contradiction; (3) al-iftirad – assuming a particular subject and testing the predicates.
Each method has a different procedure and scope.
2
Analyze the scope of each method
Khalf works for all proposition types (affirmative/negative, simple/compound). 'Aks requires prior knowledge of the conversion of the opposite type, leading to circularity. Iftirad only works for affirmatives and compound negatives.
Khalf is the most comprehensive.
3
Understand the circularity problem of 'aks
To prove conversion of affirmatives by 'aks, you need to know conversion of negatives, and vice versa. This creates a circle of dependence, making 'aks unfit as a universal method.
The passage explicitly states this.
4
Explain why the author chose khalf
Since khalf avoids circularity and covers all proposition types without exception, the author limited his proof of conversion to khalf alone.
This is a practical decision for pedagogical clarity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الموجبة والسالبة (أَنَّ نَقِيضَ العَكْسِ مَعَ الأَصْل يُنْتِجُ الْمُحَالَ). وهذا البيانُ يسمَّى بالخُلفِ ، وهو : إثباتُ المطلوب بإبطال نقيضه على ما سيجيء في القياس .
الدسوقي
(قَوْلُهُ : أَنَّ نَقِيضَ الْعَكْسِ مَعَ الْأَصْلِ) أي : يُجعلُ ذلكَ النَّقيضُ صُغرى إِنْ كَانَ مُوجبةٌ كما في عكس السَّوالب ، وكُبرى إنْ كانَ سالبة كما في عكس الموجبات.
(قَوْلُهُ : الْمَطْلُوبِ :أي: العكس.
(قَوْلُهُ : وَهُوَ) أي : النَّقيض.
العطار
لِلخاصَّتَيْنِ ؛ دليل العكس دون دليل الخلفِ واعلَمْ أَنَّ لِلقَومِ في بيان انعكاس القضايا طُرُقَاً ثلاثة، أحدها : الخلفُ ؛ وَهُوَ ضَمُّ نقيض العكس أو جزئه إلى الأصل أو إلى جزئه ؛ لينتج المحال، وثانيها : العكسُ ؛ وَهُوَ أنْ تعكس نقيض العكس أو جزئيه ليحصلَ مَا يُنافي الأصل وثالثها الافتراض؛ وَهُوَ أَنْ نفرضَ ذَاتَ الموضوع شيئاً مُعيَّناً ويُحملَ كُلَّ واحدٍ مِن وَصفَي الموضوع والمحمول عليه حتّى يتَّضِحَ صَدقُ مفهوم العكس، وَلَمَّا كانَ دليل الخلفِ جارياً في الموجبات والسوالب؛ بَسيطها ومركبها ، وأمكن بيان انعكاسها بِهِ مِنْ غيرِ لزومِ دورٍ ؛ اقتصرَ المصنِّفُ عليه هنا بخلافِ برهان العكس، فإنَّ بيان انعكاس الكُلِّ بِهِ يستلزمُ الدَّورَ؛ ضرورة أنَّ بيان انعكاس الموجبات بِهِ يتوقف على معرفة انعكاس السوالب وبالعكس، والافتراضُ لا يجري إلا في الموجبات والسوالب المركَّبة. (قَوْلُهُ : وَهَذَا الْبَيَانُ يُسَمَّى بِالْخُلْفِ بضمّ الخاء بمعنى الباطل؛ لأنَّه يُنتج باطلاً، وَبِفَتْحِها بمعنى وراء؛ لأنَّ مَا يُنتجه يُنبَذُ إلى خَلْف؛ أي: وراء . (قَوْلُهُ : وَهُوَ إثْبَاتُ الْمَطْلُوبِ بِإِبْطَالِ نَقِبْضِهِ) سواءٌ كان الإبطال بضمّ نقيض العكس معَ الأصل لينتج محالاً، أو بعكس النَّقيض؛ ليحصل بانعكاسه ما يُنافي الأصل، وهذا هو السر في أمر العلامة الشارح بالتأمل لا ما يقولونَ .ا.هـ. الشَّرنوبي.
وحاصلُهُ : أَنَّه لو لم يصدقِ العكسُ لَصَدَقَ نقيضُه، وهو مع الأصل الْمُحالَ ـ كما ذكرنا غيرَ مرَّةٍ - والمُحالُ ناشئ من نقيض العكس، فيلزم صِدقُ العكس.
The affirmative and negative (that the contradictory of the converse with the original produces an absurdity). This statement is called al-khalf, and it is: establishing the required by nullifying its contradictory, as will come in the syllogism. Dusuqi: His statement: 'that the contradictory of the converse with the original' means: that contradictory is made the minor if it is affirmative as in the conversion of negatives, and the major if it is negative as in the conversion of affirmatives. His statement: 'the required' means the converse. His statement: 'and it' means the contradictory. 'Attar: For the two properties; the proof of the converse without the proof of al-khalf. And know that the people have three methods in explaining the conversion of propositions: first, al-khalf, which is joining the contradictory of the converse or its part to the original or its part to produce an absurdity; second, al-'aks, which is that you convert the contradictory of the converse or its parts to obtain what contradicts the original; third, al-iftirad, which is that we assume the subject to be a particular thing and we predicate each of the two descriptions (of subject and predicate) upon it until the truth of the concept of the converse becomes clear. And since the proof of al-khalf is applicable to affirmatives and negatives, simple and compound, and the explanation of their conversion by it is possible without necessitating circularity, the author limited himself to it here, unlike the proof of al-'aks, for explaining the conversion of all by it necessitates circularity necessarily, because explaining the conversion of affirmatives by it depends on knowing the conversion of negatives and vice versa. And al-iftirad applies only to affirmatives and compound negatives. His statement: 'And this explanation is called al-khalf' with damm of khā' meaning false, because it produces falsehood; and with fatḥ meaning behind, because what it produces is cast behind. His statement: 'And it is establishing the required by nullifying its contradictory' whether the nullification is by joining the contradictory of the converse with the original to produce an absurdity, or by converting the contradictory to obtain by its conversion what contradicts the original, and this is the secret in the order of the commentator for reflection, not as they say. End of al-Sharnubi. Its summary: that if the converse were not true, its contradictory would be true, and it together with the original is absurd, as we have mentioned repeatedly, and the absurdity arises from the contradictory of the converse, so the truth of the converse necessarily follows.
Source pages p. 724

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on the method of proving propositional conversion (al-'aks) via reductio ad absurdum (al-khalf). It begins by stating the core principle: assuming the contradictory of the converse together with the original premise leads to an absurdity, thus proving the converse. Dusuqi clarifies how the contradictory is used as a premise depending on its quantity. 'Attar then enumerates three methods for proving conversion: (1) al-khalf, which combines the contradictory with the original; (2) al-'aks, which involves converting the contradictory to obtain a contradiction with the original; and (3) al-iftirad, which tests a particular instance. The author chose al-khalf because it works for all proposition types without circularity, unlike al-'aks which requires prior knowledge of the conversion of the opposite type, and al-iftirad which only applies to compound propositions. The passage also discusses the etymology of 'khalf' (falsehood or behind) and emphasizes that the method can be implemented either by direct combination or by converting the contradictory. The summary reiterates the logical structure: if the converse were false, its contradictory would be true, leading to absurdity with the original, so the converse must be true.

Key Terms
الخلف
العكس
الافتراض
دور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

C) 'Aks only works for negatives.
D) Khalf is required by tradition.

Key Takeaway
Khalf is the preferred method because it is universally applicable without circularity.

Propositions That Do Not Convert: The Naqd Principle
Lists exceptions to simple conversion with naqd rationale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which categorical propositions lack simple conversion and explain the counterexample (naqd) rationale.

Core Idea
Not all categorical propositions convert; temporal, existential, possible, and absolute universal are refuted by counterexample.

In the study of immediate inference, simple conversion is a key tool, but it does not apply to all proposition types. This passage from al-Tadhheeb lists the exceptions and introduces the concept of al-naqd—a counterexample that breaks the conversion. Understanding these exceptions prevents errors in reasoning and deepens your grasp of propositional logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the exception list
Locate the four pairs of proposition types that lack simple conversion: the two temporal (waqtiyya and muntashira), the two existential, the two possible, and the absolute universal.
2
Understand al-naqd
Al-naqd means a counterexample: for each type, there exists a true proposition whose converse is false, so conversion does not hold necessarily.
3
Check compound nature
Dusuqi notes that the temporal and spread are compound propositions (murakkabāt). This affects their conversion properties.
4
Note ‘Attar’s critique
‘Attar points out that the listing omits the absolute temporal and absolute spread, which also lack conversion—criticizing the author’s silence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: فِي الْعَكْسِ الْمُسْتَوِي
يُنتج
وَلَا عَكْسَ لِلبَوَاقِي من القضايا، وهي الوقتيتان، والوجوديتان، والممكِنَتانِ، والمطلقة العامة.
وإنَّما لم تنعكس هذه القضايا ((بِالنَّقْضِ ) ؛ أي : بسببِ النَّقض الوارد على الانعكاس،

الدسوقي
(قَوْلُهُ : الْوَقْتِيَّتَانِ) أي : الوقتيَّةُ والمنتشرة، وهما مُركّبتانِ، ومثلُهما : الوقتيَّةُ المطلقة والمنتشرة المطلقة (قَوْلُهُ : بِالنَّقْضِ ) أي : التَّخلُفِ الوارد على الانعكاس؛ أي: انعكاس تلك البواقي. وحاصله أنَّ هذه القضايا الباقية لما كانت في بعض المواد صادقة دون عكسها ؛ علم أنّ العكس غير لازم لها .

العطار
الأصل المفروض الصدقِ ; فليس عكس النقيض خارجاً عن طريقِ الخُلْفِ، إِلَّا أَنْ يدَّعي أنَّ الخُلْفَ في باب العكس اصطلاح مُغايرٌ لِمُطلق الخلفِ، وَلَا موجب لهذهِ الدعوى ; قالَهُ العصامُ، وقال في موضع آخَرَ : وَلَكَ في إثباتِ العكس بطريقِ الخُلفِ أن تضمَّ نقيض العكس معَ مَا هوَ أعَمُّ مِنَ الأصلِ أَوْ مَعَ مَا بينَهُ وبينَ الأصلِ المحالُ ; فَيبطل نقيضُ العكس، وهو طريق واضح وإنْ لم يُستخرج ملازمة لينتج إلى الآن.
(قَوْلُهُ : لَصَدَقَ نَقِيْضُهُ) لو قالَ : لَصَدَقَ نقيضُهُ أو جزؤُهُ، وَهُما مع الأصل أو جزيه ... إلخ ; لكان أحسنَ ; بناءً على ما سبق . (قَوْلُهُ : وَالْمُطْلَقَةُ الْعَامَّةُ ... إِلَخ) وَكَذا لا عكس للوقتيَّة المطلقة والمنتشرة المطلقة، فَالسُّكوتُ عنهما في معرض البيان قصور .
Chapter: On Simple Conversion. (It produces?) And there is no conversion for the remaining propositions, which are: the two temporal, the two existential, the two possible, and the absolute universal. And these propositions are not converted because of al-naqd; i.e., because of the counterexample occurring to the conversion. Dusuqi: His statement: 'the two temporal' means the temporal and the spread (al-muntashira), and they are compound, and similar to them are the absolute temporal and the absolute spread. His statement: 'by al-naqd' means the deficiency occurring in the conversion; i.e., the conversion of those remaining. Its summary: since these remaining propositions are in some matters true without their converse, it is known that the converse is not necessary for them. 'Attar: The original is assumed true; so the converse of the contradictory is not outside the method of al-khalf, unless one claims that al-khalf in the chapter of conversion is a different term from absolute al-khalf, and there is no basis for this claim. Al-'Isam said it, and he said elsewhere: You have in proving the converse by way of al-khalf that you join the contradictory of the converse with what is more general than the original or with what between it and the original produces absurdity; so the contradictory of the converse is invalidated, and it is a clear method even if it has not been extracted as a consequence to produce until now. His statement: 'its contradictory would be true'—if he said: its contradictory or its part, and both with the original or its part ... etc., it would be better, based on what preceded. His statement: 'and the absolute universal ... etc.' And likewise there is no conversion for the absolute temporal and the absolute spread, so silence about them in the context of explanation is deficient.
Source pages p. 725

Commentary
This passage is a chapter on simple conversion (al-'aks al-mustawi) that identifies which types of propositions do not have a valid conversion. It lists four pairs: the two temporal (waqtiyya and muntashira, which are compound), the two existential (wujudiyya mutlaqa and wujudiyya?), the two possible (mumkina), and the absolute universal (mutlaqa 'amma). The reason given is 'al-naqd', meaning a counterexample: for each of these types, there exists a true instance of the proposition whose converse is false, so conversion does not hold necessarily. Dusuqi clarifies that 'temporal' includes the 'spread' (muntashira) and that these are compound propositions; similar are the absolute temporal and absolute spread. He explains that al-naqd refers to the failure of conversion in some matters. 'Attar then adds a note about the method of al-khalf, arguing that using a more general premise can also prove conversion, and criticizes the omission of the absolute temporal and absolute spread from the list.

النقض
المطلقة العامة

Why Temporal Propositions Do Not Convert
Author proves temporal propositions do not convert, thus other modals also do not convert.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to test the validity of conversion for temporal modal propositions using the principle that failure of the specific implies failure of the general.

Core Idea
If the most specific modal cannot convert, the more general ones must also fail.

In this passage, the commentator proves that temporal propositions—a class of modal propositions—do not undergo valid conversion (aks). The argument rests on a logical principle: if a more specific type fails to convert, the more general type cannot succeed. A concrete example from lunar astronomy demonstrates the failure: a true temporal universal negative yields a false particular negative converse. Understanding this helps you evaluate modal inferences in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim being proved
The commentator claims that temporal propositions (al-waqtiyya) do not convert, and because they are the most specific of the modal propositions mentioned, all other modals also fail to convert. Note the logical principle: if the specific fails, the general fails.
2
Extract the modal hierarchy
Recognize that temporal propositions are the most specific (al-akhas) among the group, and other modals are more general (al-a'amm). The textual hint 'with minimal reflection' suggests that temporal modality includes a fixed time, making it narrower than purely possibility-based modals.
3
Verify the counterexample for temporal conversion
Write out the original proposition: 'No moon is eclipsed at quadrature, not perpetually' (universal negative temporal). Attempt to convert: 'Some eclipsed thing is not a moon, in common possibility.' Check truth values: the original is true (eclipses cannot occur at quadrature); the converse is false (every eclipsed thing is necessarily a moon). Hence conversion fails because the truth is not preserved.
The example uses lunar astronomy: quadrature (tarbi') is a 90-degree angle between moon and sun, preventing eclipses.
4
Apply the general principle
Since the temporal (most specific) fails, all more general modalities also fail conversion. This blocks invalid inferences in other modal contexts.
This principle is only one-way: if the specific fails, the general fails; the converse does not hold.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: فِي الْعَكْسِ الْمُسْتَوِي
وذلك أنَّ الوقتيَّةً أخصُّ تلك القضايا المذكورة، وهي لا تنعكس، فلا تنعكس القضايا المذكورة؛ لأنَّه إذا لم ينعكس الأخصُّ، لم ينعكس الأعم . أمَّا أنَّ الوقتيّة أخصُّ القضايا المذكورة؛ فيظهرُ بأدنى تأمل. وأمَّا أنَّها لا تنعكسُ؛ فَلِصِدْقِ قولِنَا: لا شيء من القمر منخسف وقت التربيع لا دائماً، مع كذب: بعض المنخسف ليس بقمر بالإمكان العام، الذي هو أعم الجهات.
Chapter on Regular Conversion: And that is because the time-bound [proposition] is the most specific of those mentioned propositions, and it does not convert, so the mentioned propositions do not convert; because if the most specific does not convert, the more general does not convert. As for the time-bound being the most specific of the mentioned propositions, it appears with minimal reflection. And as for it not converting, it is because of the truth of our statement: 'Nothing from the moon is eclipsed at the time of quadrature, not perpetually,' along with the falsehood of: 'Some eclipsed thing is not a moon, in the common possibility, which is the most general of modalities.'
Source pages p. 726

Commentary
This passage is about the conversion (aks) of temporal propositions (al-waqtiyya). The author argues that the time-bound proposition is the most specific (akhas) among the modal propositions previously mentioned. Since the most specific does not convert, the more general (a'amm) ones also do not convert—a logical principle: if the specific fails, the general also fails. The author then provides two justifications. First, that the temporal proposition is the most specific is apparent upon minimal reflection (because it includes a specific time, while other modals are broader). Second, he gives a counterexample to prove it does not convert. The original universal negative temporal: 'No moon is eclipsed at quadrature, not perpetually' is true (since eclipses cannot occur at quadrature). Its converse would be a particular negative: 'Some eclipsed thing is not a moon' in the common possibility modality, but this is false because every eclipsed thing is necessarily a moon. Thus the conversion fails. The example relies on lunar astronomy: 'quadrature' (tarbi') is when the moon and sun are at a 90-degree angle, preventing an eclipse. This illustrates that temporal modality is more restrictive than general possibility.

العكس
الأخص
الأعم

Key Takeaway

The Temporal Proposition’s Non-Conversion: The Lunar Eclipse Example
Author explains why temporal propositions do not convert using the lunar eclipse counterexample.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a temporal proposition and test its convertibility by checking the truth of its converse against actual facts.

Core Idea
Temporal propositions fail to convert because their time condition restricts the subject-predicate relationship.

In this lesson, we examine why a proposition that includes a specific time condition—like 'at the time of quadrature'—cannot be converted while preserving truth. The author uses the example of the lunar eclipse to demonstrate that the converse would be false, proving that temporal propositions remain non-convertible. We will follow his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the temporal proposition
Locate the temporal qualifier in the Arabic statement. Here it is 'وقت التربيع' (at the time of quadrature).
2
Form the converse
Swap the subject and predicate: from 'Nothing of the moon is eclipsed at quadrature' to 'Some eclipsed thing is not the moon'.
The quality (negative) and quantity (universal to particular) follow conversion rules for universal negatives.
3
Test the truth of the converse
Determine if the converse is true. It is false because eclipses are exclusive to the moon; no non-moon object is eclipsed.
The commentator emphasizes that eclipse is specific to the moon.
4
Conclude non-conversion
Since the original proposition is true but its converse is false, the temporal proposition cannot convert.
This counterexample suffices to show the rule.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: فِي الْعَكْسِ الْمُسْتَوِي
وأمَّا أَنَّها لا تنعكسُ؛ فَلِصِدْقِ قولِنَا: لا شيء من القمر منخسف وقتَ التربيع لا دائماً، مع كذب: بعض المنخسف ليس بقمر بالإمكان العام، الذي هو أعم الجهات.
(قَوْلُهُ: وَقْتَ التَّرْبِيع) التربيعُ: هو أن يكونَ بينَ الشَّمس والقمرِ ربع الفلك.
(قَوْلُهُ: مَعَ كَذِبِ: بَعْضُ الْمُنْخَسِفِ لَيْسَ بِقَمَرِ) أي: لأَنَّ الانخسافَ مُختص بالقمر لا يوجد في غيره.
وأمَّا أنَّه إذا لم ينعكس الأخصّ لم ينعكس الأعم؛ فلأنَّه لو انعكس الأعم؛ لانعكس الأخصّ؛ لأنَّ العكس لازم للقضيَّة، ولازم الأعم لازم الأخصّ.
(قَوْلُهُ: فَلِأَنَّهُ لَوِ انْعَكَسَ الْأَعَمُ) على تقدير عدم انعكاس الأخصّ.
(قَوْلُهُ: لَانْعَكَسَ الْأَخَصُّ) معَ أَنَّهُ غيرُ مُنعَكِس.
(قَوْلُهُ: لأَنَّ الْعَكْسَ) تعليل لانعكاس الأخصّ على تقدير انعكاس الأعم.
(قَوْلُهُ: وَلَازِمُ الْأَعَمِّ لَازِمُ الْأَخَصِّ) فيلزمُ انعكاسه مع كونه غير مُنعكس وهو باطل.
Chapter: On Direct Conversion. And as for it not converting, it is because our statement is true: 'Nothing of the moon is eclipsed at the time of quadrature, not always', while the falsehood: 'Some eclipsed thing is not the moon' [is false] with the general possible, which is the most general of modes. (His statement: 'at the time of quadrature') The quadrature is that the sun and moon are a quarter of the celestial sphere apart. (His statement: 'while the falsehood: some eclipsed thing is not the moon') i.e., because eclipse is specific to the moon and does not occur in other things. And as for that if the specific does not convert, the general does not convert, because if the general converted, the specific would convert, since conversion is a necessary consequence of the proposition, and the necessary consequence of the general is a necessary consequence of the specific. (His statement: 'for that if the general converted') on the assumption of the non-conversion of the specific. (His statement: 'the specific would convert') while it is not converting. (His statement: 'because conversion') is an explanation for the conversion of the specific on the assumption of conversion of the general. (His statement: 'and the necessary consequence of the general is a necessary consequence of the specific') so its conversion follows while it is not converting, which is false.
Source pages p. 727

Commentary
This passage explains why the temporal proposition (الوقتية) does not convert. The author first states that the proposition 'Nothing of the moon is eclipsed at the time of quadrature, not always' is true, but its converse 'Some eclipsed thing is not the moon' is false because eclipses only happen to the moon. This shows the original proposition cannot convert. The commentator clarifies that the time 'quadrature' refers to the moon being at a right angle relative to the sun. Then he explains a general principle: if the specific (the temporal) does not convert, the more general (like the absolute) also does not convert because if the general could convert, the specific would too due to the necessity of conversion. This is proven by showing that if the general converted, the specific would convert, contradicting the fact that it doesn't. Therefore, the temporal proposition remains non-convertible.

Key Terms
العكس المستوي
الوقتية
التربيع

Key Terms from Source Passage

A temporal proposition cannot convert because its time condition is not preserved in the converse, making the converse false.

The Principle: Non-Conversion of the Specific Implies Non-Conversion of the General
Author proves that non-conversion of the temporal proposition implies non-conversion of the absolute proposition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the principle that if a specific type of proposition (e.g., temporal) does not convert, then a more general type also does not convert.

Core Idea
If a specific proposition fails to convert, the general cannot convert either; otherwise, the specific would too.

This lesson explains a crucial principle: if the more specific temporal proposition does not convert, then the more general absolute proposition also does not convert. The author provides a logical proof: if the general could convert, the specific would convert because conversion is a necessary property of a proposition, and the necessary property of the general is also necessary for the specific. Since we already know the specific does not convert, the general cannot convert either.

Guided Path
1
Identify the specific and general
The temporal proposition (e.g., 'Nothing of the moon is eclipsed at quadrature') is the specific (al-akhass). The absolute proposition (without time condition) is the general (al-a‘amm).
2
State the principle
If the specific does not convert, the general does not convert.
3
Understand the proof
Assume the general converts. Then the specific would convert because conversion is a necessary consequence (lazim) of a proposition. Since the general proposition has this necessary property, the specific (which is a subset) must also have it.
The commentator clarifies that this is a hypothetical assumption.
4
Reach the contradiction
But we already know the specific does not convert (from the previous lesson). Therefore, the assumption that the general converts leads to a contradiction. Hence, the general cannot convert.

Source Text

The Conversion Rule for Positive Quantified Propositions
Author states the general rule that all positive quantified propositions convert to particular positive except the two possible propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the conversion rule for affirmative categorical propositions to convert universal and particular positives to particular positives, and to recognize the exception of the two possible propositions.

Core Idea
All affirmative quantified propositions convert to particular affirmative, except the two possible propositions.

This passage presents a concise rule for immediate inference (conversion) specific to affirmative categorical propositions. The rule states that both universal affirmative (A) and particular affirmative (I) propositions convert to a particular affirmative (I) proposition, with the sole exception of propositions that express possibility rather than actuality. This rule is foundational for syllogistic reasoning and will be used extensively in checking the validity of arguments in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of proposition
Determine if the proposition is affirmative (positive) and quantified (universal or particular). The rule only applies to affirmative quantified propositions.
The proposition must be categorical and affirmative, not negative or modal.
2
Apply the conversion pattern
For any affirmative universal or particular, the converse is a particular affirmative. Do not change the quality of the proposition.
Quantity changes to particular; the subject and predicate swap positions.
3
Check for the exception
Verify if the proposition is one of the two possible propositions (mumkinatan). If it expresses possibility (one-sided or two-sided possible), the rule does not apply and the conversion does not yield a particular positive.
Possible propositions assert contingency, not actuality, so conversion rules differ.
4
Obtain the conclusion
Write the converted proposition: the subject becomes predicate, predicate becomes subject, but the quantity becomes particular and the quality remains affirmative.
For example, 'Every intoxicant is forbidden' converts to 'Some forbidden thing is intoxicant'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلَمْ أنَّ القضايا الموجّهة الموجبة - كُلِّيَّةٌ كانت أو جُزئيَّةٌ - تنعكس موجبةً جزئيةً، إلا الممكنتين.
(قَوْلُهُ: وَاعْلَمْ ... إلخ) هذا بمنزلة قولِكَ: والحاصلُ .

Source pages p. 728

Commentary
This passage gives a concise rule: all affirmative categorical propositions (both universal affirmative 'A' and particular affirmative 'I') convert to a particular affirmative proposition, with the exception of the two 'possible' propositions (i.e., propositions that assert possibility rather than actuality). The commentator notes that this statement is a summary of what has been previously discussed. For example, the universal affirmative 'Every scholar is a human' converts to 'Some human is a scholar'. The exception of the two possibles refers to propositions like 'It is possible for every man to write' which does not yield a particular affirmative conversion. This rule is fundamental for understanding immediate inference.

If the specific does not convert, the general does not convert
Author states and proves a principle about conversion of general and specific propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the principle that failure of conversion of a specific proposition implies failure of conversion of its corresponding general proposition.

Core Idea
If the specific cannot convert, the general cannot either — because the general's converse would force the specific's.

In this lesson, we explore a key principle of conversion (caks) in Islamic logic. The author proves that if a more specific proposition does not convert, then the more general proposition also does not convert. This is not intuitively obvious — one might think a general proposition's conversion might hold independently. The proof uses the idea that the converse is a necessary consequence (lazim) of the original, and that what follows from the general also follows from the specific.

Guided Path
1
Identify the specific and general propositions
Read the passage and determine which proposition is more specific (أخص) and which is more general (أعم). For example, 'Some A is B' is more specific than 'Every A is B'.
The specific proposition has narrower extension.
2
Understand the contrapositive argument
The author proves the principle by contrapositive: if the general converted, then the specific would convert. Identify this conditional in the Arabic: 'لانعكس الأعم؛ لانعكس الأخص'.
The proof assumes the converse is a necessary consequent of the original.
3
Apply the transitivity of logical consequence
Recognize that if the converse is a necessary consequent (lazim) of the general, and the general is a necessary consequent of the specific (by subalternation), then the converse is also a necessary consequent of the specific.
This uses the principle that what is necessary for the more general is also necessary for the more specific.
4
Conclude: failure of specific implies failure of general
Since the specific does not convert (i.e., its converse is false), the general cannot convert. Otherwise, the specific would convert, which contradicts the assumption.
This yields the desired principle by modus tollens.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأمَّا أنَّه إذا لم ينعكس الأخصّ لم ينعكس الأعم ؛ فلأنَّه لو انعكس الأعم؛ لانعكس الأخصّ ؛ لأنَّ العكس لازم للقضيَّة، ولازم الأعم لازم الأخصّ.
And as for the fact that if the more specific does not convert, the more general does not convert – because if the more general converted, then the more specific would convert; for the converse is a necessary consequent of the proposition, and the necessary consequent of the more general is a necessary consequent of the more specific.
Source pages p. 728

Commentary
This passage proves a principle about the conversion (caks) of propositions. The author states: if a specific proposition (e.g., 'Some A is B') does not convert, then the more general proposition (e.g., 'Every A is B') also does not convert. The proof proceeds by contrapositive: if the general did convert, then the specific would also convert (since the converse is a necessary consequent of the original, and what is necessary for the general is also necessary for the specific). Therefore, since the specific does not convert, the general cannot convert either. This is a purely logical argument using the notion of logical consequence (luzum). An example: if 'Some intoxicant is wine' (specific) does not convert to 'Some wine is an intoxicant'? Actually, that is true. Better: 'Every wine is intoxicant' (general) converts to 'Some intoxicant is wine' (particular). The principle guards against the error of assuming that failure of conversion at a specific level implies nothing about the general level; it shows the opposite relation.

Key Terms

أخص
أعم
لازم

Key Takeaway
The failure of conversion of a specific proposition forces the failure of conversion of the corresponding general proposition.

Conversion of Modal Affirmative Propositions
Author gives the rule for converting modal affirmative propositions and classifies the types that convert.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify which modal affirmative propositions convert to affirmative particular and identify the type of converse based on descriptive permanence and universality.

Core Idea
Modal affirmatives convert to particular, not universal, except possibility.

In this passage from the Tadhheeb, the author lays out the rules for converting modal affirmative propositions (qadaya muwajjaha mujiba). He emphasizes that all such propositions convert to affirmative particular, with the exception of the two possibility propositions (al-mumkinatan). He then provides a detailed classification based on whether the proposition has general universality (al-itlaq al-amm) and descriptive permanence (al-dawam al-wasfi). This classification determines the modality of the converse. Understanding these rules is essential for correctly applying conversion in syllogistic contexts within Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule for modal affirmative conversion
The author states that all modal affirmative propositions, universal or particular, convert to affirmative particular. Note the exception: the two possibility propositions (mumkinatan).
2
Distinguish between propositions with and without general universality (itlaq 'amm)
Propositions lacking itlaq 'amm are the possibility propositions (mumkinatan); their conversion status is unknown (ghayr ma'lum). For propositions with itlaq 'amm, further classification applies.
3
Apply the condition of descriptive permanence (dawam wasfi)
If the proposition does not have dawam wasfi, it converts to an affirmative particular absolute general (mutlaqa amma). If it has dawam wasfi, check if it is restricted by non-permanence (muqayyada bil-ladawam). If not restricted, it converts to affirmative particular temporal absolute (hiniya mutlaqa). If restricted, it converts to affirmative particular absolute non-permanent (mutlaqa la da'ima).
4
Count the types
The passage enumerates five types for the first case (waqtitiyan, wujudiyan, mutlaqa amma), four for the second, and two for the third. This is a memorization aid for the advanced student.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[ انعكاس السالبة الجزئية في الخاصَّتين] واعلَمْ أنَّ القضايا الموجّهة الموجبة - كُلِّيَّةٌ كانت أو جُزئيَّةٌ - تنعكس موجبةً جزئيةً، إلا الممكنتَين، والضَّابط في الموجباتِ : أَنَّ مَا لا يصدق عليه الإطلاق العالم وهو الممكناتُ فَحَالُهُ غَيْرُ مَعلومٍ، وَمَا يصدقُ عليهِ الإطلاقُ العام؛ فإن لم يصدق عليهِ الدَّوامُ الوصفيُّ ؛ انعكسَتْ موجبةً جزئيَّةً مُطلقةً عامَّةٌ، سواءٌ كانَ الأصلُ كُلّيًّا أو جزئيًّا، وَهِيَ خمس قضايا : الوقتيتَانِ والوجوديَّتَانِ والمطلقة العامَّةُ ؛ وإن صدق عليهِ الدَّوامُ الوصفي، فإن لم تكنْ مُقيَّدةً باللادوام؛ انعكس موجبةٌ جزئيَّةً حينيَّةً مُطلقةً وهي أربع قضايا وإنْ كانَ مُقيَّداً بِهِ؛ انعكس إلى موجبة جزئية مُطلقة لا دائمةٍ، وَهُمَا قضيَّتان .
[Conversion of the negative particular in the two specific] And know that the modal affirmative propositions – whether universal or particular – convert to affirmative particular, except the two possibility [propositions]. And the rule concerning affirmatives is: that which does not have true absolute universality – and these are the possibility propositions – their status is unknown; and that which has true general universality – if it does not have descriptive permanence, it converts to an affirmative particular absolute general, whether the original was universal or particular, and these are five propositions: the two temporal and the two existential and the absolute general; and if it has descriptive permanence, if it is not restricted by non-permanence, it converts to an affirmative particular temporal absolute, and these are four propositions; and if it is restricted by it, it converts to an affirmative particular absolute non-permanent, and these are two propositions.
Source pages p. 728

Commentary
This passage presents the rules for converting modal affirmative propositions (al-qadaya al-muwajjaha al-mujiba). The author first states a general rule: all such propositions, whether universal or particular, convert to affirmative particular, except for the two possibility propositions (al-mumkinatan) on the madhhab of Ibn Sina. Then a detailed classification follows. The 'rule' (al-dabit) divides affirmatives based on whether they have absolute universality (al-itlaq al-amm) or general universality. The possibility propositions are excluded because their truth conditions do not guarantee a converse. The classification enumerates various types: time-based (waqtitiyan), existential (wujudiyan), absolute general, and so on, each converting to a specific modal particular. For example, a proposition lacking descriptive permanence (al-dawam al-wasfi) converts to an absolute general particular. This is technical material for advanced students of logic, ensuring they know exactly which modal statements convert and to what form.

ممكنتان
دوام وصفي
إطلاق عام

C) It converts to an affirmative particular temporal absolute.
D) It converts to an affirmative universal absolute general.

Key Takeaway
Modal affirmative propositions (except possibility) convert to affirmative particular, with the converse's modality determined by whether the original had descriptive permanence and general universality.

Conversion of Negative Modal Propositions
Author enumerates which universal negative modal propositions convert and which do not, with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine which universal negative modal propositions convert under uniform conversion (al-aks al-mustawi) and which do not.

Core Idea
Only six universal negatives convert: the two permanent, two general, and two specific.

Uniform conversion swaps subject and predicate while preserving truth and modality. This lesson focuses on negative modal propositions, which have specific rules. Mastering these rules is essential for correctly manipulating propositions in logical proofs, especially in Islamic theology and jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Check if the proposition is universal negative
The rules for conversion depend on whether the proposition is universal or particular. First, identify if the negative proposition is universal (e.g., 'No S is P') or particular (e.g., 'Some S is not P').
This lesson mainly covers universal negatives; particulars have different rules.
2
Identify the modality
Determine the modality of the proposition: permanent (da'im), general ('amm), specific (khass), temporal (waqti), existential (wujudi), or possibility (mumkin). This classification is key to applying the conversion rules.
Modalities are defined in earlier chapters.
3
Apply the conversion rule for universal negatives
If the proposition is universal and its modality is permanent, general, or specific, it converts. If its modality is temporal, existential, or possibility, it does not convert.
The two permanent, two general, and two specific convert; the two temporal, two existential, and two possibility do not.
4
Handle special cases: absolute general as particular
The absolute general (al-mutlaqa al-amma) is treated as a particular negative. Among particulars, only the conditional (mashruta) and the two specific customary ('urfiya khassa) convert, and they convert to a specific customary proposition.
Example: 'Some writer is not still-fingered as long as he writes' converts to 'Some writer is actually always still-fingered.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(قَوْلُهُ : الشَّيْخ) أي: ابن سينا (قَوْلُهُ : وَسَبْعُ ... إلخ) ويزادُ عليها الوقتيَّةُ المطلقة، والمنتشرة المطلقة. (قَوْلُهُ : فَإِنَّهُمَا يَنْعَكِسَانِ عُرْفِيَّةٌ خَاصَّةٌ أي : تنعكس بالضرورة، أو دائماً بعضُ الكاتب ليس بساكن الأصابع ما دامَ كاتباً لا دائماً ؛ أي: بعضُ الكاتب ساكن الأصابع بالفعل دائماً ، بعضُ السَّاكن ليس كاتباً ما دام ساكناً لا دائماً ؛ أي: بعضُ السَّاكن كاتب بالفعل.
Chapter on uniform conversion: Then they both do not convert according to the school of the Shaykh. And as for negative [propositions], if they are universal, six of them convert: the two permanent, the two general, and the two specific; and seven of them do not convert: the two temporal, the two existential, and the two possibility. And the absolute general – even if it is particular – none of them convert except the conditional, and the two specific customary only, for they both convert to a specific customary. (His saying: 'the Shaykh') meaning Ibn Sina. (His saying: 'and seven ... etc.') And added to them are the absolute temporal and the absolute distributed. (His saying: 'for they both convert to a specific customary') meaning: they convert necessarily or always: some writer is not still-fingered as long as he writes, not always; i.e., some writer is actually always still-fingered; some still is not writer as long as still, not always; i.e., some still is actually writer.
Source pages p. 729

Commentary
This passage is a chapter on uniform conversion (al-aks al-mustawi). It first mentions that the two possibility propositions do not convert according to Ibn Sina (al-Shaykh). Then it lists the negative universal propositions that convert: six types (the two permanent, two general, two specific) and seven that do not (the two temporal, two existential, two possibility). Then it addresses the absolute general (al-mutlaqa al-amma), noting that even if particular, only the conditional (al-mashruta) and the two specific customary (al-urfiya al-khassatan) convert to a specific customary. The marginal notes clarify that 'the Shaykh' is Ibn Sina, and that additional types like the absolute temporal and absolute distributed are added to the non-converting list. An example is given: 'Some writer is not still-fingered as long as he writes' converts to 'Some writer is actually always still-fingered' – illustrating the conversion of a specific customary negative to a specific customary affirmative. This is a technical enumeration essential for mastering conversion in modal logic.

Conversion Rules for Universal Negatives
Rules for conversion of universal negatives

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify universal negative propositions into those that undergo direct conversion and those that do not, based on their temporal and modal characteristics.

Core Idea
Temporal and modal conditions determine whether a universal negative converts, not just its quantity.

In this passage, the author lists thirteen types of universal negatives and states which six convert and which seven do not. He also addresses the unrestricted particular proposition. You will learn to navigate this taxonomy and apply it to examples from Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposition type
Confirm that the proposition is a universal negative (sāliba kulliyya). For example, 'No intoxicant is permissible' is a universal negative.
Universal negatives deny something of every member of the subject class.
2
Classify its temporal/modal status
Determine if the proposition is perpetual (dā’imatān), general-natural (āmmatān), special-natural (khāṣṣatān), time-restricted (waqtiyyatān), existential (wujūdiyyatān), or possible (mumkinatān). The first three convert; the last three do not.
Perpetual = always true; general-natural = generally true; special-natural = mostly true; time-restricted = true only during a period; existential = true only when subject exists; possible = not necessary.
3
Apply the conversion rule
If the proposition is one of the first three types (perpetual, general-natural, special-natural), it converts to a universal negative. If it is time-restricted, existential, or possible, it does not convert.
The author lists six converting and seven non-converting universal negatives.
4
Handle the unrestricted particular exception
For unrestricted particular (muṭlaqa ‘āmma) propositions, only those that are conditional (mashrūṭa) or specific conventional (‘urfiyya khāṣṣa) convert, and they convert as specific conventional.
Example: 'Some writer is not still-fingered as long as he is writing' converts to 'Some writer is actually perpetually still-fingered'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: فِي الْعَكْسِ الْمُسْتَوِي فإنَّهما لا تنعكسانِ على مذهبِ الشَّيخ . وأمَّا السَّوالب فإن كانت كلّيَّةٌ، فَسِتٌ منها تنعكس، وهي: الدائمتان والعامَّتانِ والخاصَّتان، وسَبْعٌ منها لا تنعكس، وهي: الوقتيتان والوجوديتان والممكنتان. والمطلقة العامة - وإن كانت جزئيَّةٌ - فلا تنعكس منها إلا المشروطة، والعرفيَّةُ الخاصَّتان فقط، فإنّهما تنعكسان عرفيّةٌ خاصَّةٌ . الدسوقي (قَوْلُهُ : الشَّيْخ) أي: ابن سينا (قَوْلُهُ : وَسَبْعُ ... إلخ) ويزادُ عليها الوقتيَّةُ المطلقة، والمنتشرة المطلقة. His saying 'they convert as specific conventional' means they convert necessarily, or perpetually: some writer is not still-fingered as long as he is writing, not perpetually; i.e., some writer is actually perpetually still-fingered; some still-fingered is not a writer as long as he is still, not perpetually; i.e., some still-fingered is actually a writer.

Commentary
This passage is a classification of universal negative propositions and their behavior under direct conversion (al-aks al-mustawi). This illustrates how the temporal condition changes the conversion result.

The Three Proofs for Conversion in Categorical Logic
Author presents the method of supposition (iftirad) as a proof for conversion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and apply the three proofs for conversion: reductio, conversion of the contradictory, and supposition.

Core Idea
Conversion is proven by three methods: contradict, convert, suppose.

In the previous lesson, you learned that categorical propositions can be converted. But how do we know that conversion is valid? Logicians developed three distinct proofs: reductio (al-khalf), conversion of the contradictory (al-aks), and supposition (al-iftirad). This lesson unpacks each method and shows you how they work.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target conversion
Decide which proposition you want to convert and what the converse should be.
For example, convert 'Every intoxicant is forbidden' to 'Some forbidden things are intoxicants'.
2
Apply the reductio method
Assume the contradictory of the converse, combine it with the original proposition, and derive a logical impossibility. Since the impossibility arises from the assumption, the contradictory must be false, and thus the converse is true.
This is proof by contradiction.
3
Apply the conversion-of-contradictory method
Take the contradictory of the converse, convert it, and show that this converted proposition contradicts the original assumed true. Since what contradicts the true is false, the converted contradictory is false, which implies the contradictory of the converse is false, and therefore the converse is true.
This method uses the conversion rule itself within the proof.
4
Apply the supposition method
Posit an arbitrary individual instance of the subject. Use the original proposition to deduce something about that instance, then generalize to establish the converse.
For example, to prove 'Some trustworthy persons are just' from 'Every just person is trustworthy', suppose a specific just person Zayd; since Zayd is trustworthy, we have a trustworthy person who is just.
5
Compare the three methods
Notice that all three methods rely on a connection between truth and contradiction, but each approaches it differently. Reductio uses direct contradiction, conversion-of-contradictory uses a converted version, and supposition uses an individual instance.
These methods are not alternative rules but different logical strategies to demonstrate the same result.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والبيان في انعكاس هاتين القضيَّتين هو الافتراضُ، وذلكَ طريق آخرُ فى إثبات العكوس. ومحصِّلُهُ : فَرْضُ ذاتِ الموضوعِ شيئاً معيناً . الدسوقي (قَوْلُهُ : وَالْبَيَانُ) أي : الدليل على انعكاس ... إلخ . (قَوْلُهُ : وَذَلِكَ) أي: الافتراض. (قَوْلُهُ : طَرِيْقٌ آخَرُ ) أي : غير طريقِ الخلفي وغير طريق العكس. والحاصل : أنّ الأدلة التي يستدلَّ بها على العكس ثلاثة : دليل الخلف، ودليل العكس، ودليل الافتراض، فالأوّل : أن تضمَّ نقيض العكس للأصل ينتج المحال، وما جاء المحال إلا من نقيض العكس، فيكون نقيض العكس كاذباً والعكس صادقاً ، وهو المطلوب. والثَّاني : هو أن تعكس نقي العكس إلى ما يُناقضُ الأصل المفروض الصدق، وَمَا ناقض الصَّادق فهو كاذب، وإذا كذب عكسُ النَّقيض؛ كانَ النَّقيضُ كاذباً ؛ لأنَّ كذبَ اللازم يستلزم كذبَ الملزوم ، وإذا كان نقيضُ العكس كاذباً؛ كانَ العكس صادقاً، وهو المطلوب. (قَوْلُهُ : وَمُحَصِّلُهُ) أي : مُحصِّلُ الافتراض.
The proof for the conversion of these two propositions is supposition (iftirad). That is another method for proving conversions. Its summary: positing the essence of the subject as a specific thing. Al-Dasuqi: His saying 'the proof' means the evidence for conversion... etc. His saying 'that' means supposition. His saying 'another method' means other than the method of reductio (al-khalf) and the method of conversion (al-aks). The conclusion: the proofs used for conversion are three: the proof by reductio, the proof by conversion, and the proof by supposition. The first: you combine the contradictory of the converse with the original, producing an impossibility; the impossibility arises only from the contradictory of the converse, so the contradictory of the converse is false and the converse is true, which is desired. The second: you convert the contradictory of the converse into something that contradicts the original assumed to be true; what contradicts the true is false; when the converse of the contradictory is false, the contradictory is false, because falsity of the consequent requires falsity of the antecedent; and when the contradictory of the converse is false, the converse is true, which is desired. His saying 'its summary' means the summary of supposition.
Source pages p. 730

Commentary
This passage is a methodological exposition on the proofs for conversion (عكس) in categorical logic. The author states that the proof for the conversion of two specific propositions (likely the time-restricted and conventional ones from the previous lesson) is by means of supposition (الافتراض). He declares that this is a third method besides reductio (الخلف) and conversion of the contradictory (عكس النقيض). Al-Dasuqi then explains: there are three types of proofs for conversion. The first, reductio, works by taking the contradictory of the converse and combining it with the original proposition to produce a contradiction; since the contradiction arises only from that contradictory, the converse must be true. The second, conversion of the contradictory, takes the contradictory of the converse, converts it, and shows that it contradicts the original assumed true; since the converted contradictory is false, the original contradictory (of the converse) is false, and hence the converse is true. The third, supposition, is summarized as 'positing the essence of the subject as a specific thing' – this method involves taking an arbitrary individual instance of the subject and reasoning from the original proposition to deduce something about that instance, then generalizing to prove the converse. This passage gives the student a clear taxonomy of the proof methods available for establishing the validity of conversion rules.

دليل العكس

The Three Proofs of Conversion in Islamic Logic
Dussuqi enumerates and explains the three proofs of conversion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three proofs of conversion in Islamic logic and explain the logical mechanism of each.

Core Idea
Three proofs establish conversion: contradiction, contrapositive conversion, and assumption of an instance.

In this passage, al-Dussuqi expands on the claim that conversion can be demonstrated by 'assumption' (iftirad). He clarifies that assumption is one of three distinct proofs, alongside direct contradiction (khalf) and conversion of the contradictory ('aks). Understanding these proofs is essential for validating immediate inferences in theological and juristic reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three proofs named by Dussuqi
Locate in the Arabic the list: دليل الخلف, دليل العكس, دليل الافتراض. These are proof by contradiction, proof by conversion of the contradictory, and proof by assumption.
2
Understand proof by contradiction (khalf)
Dussuqi explains: take the contradictory of the converse (نقيض العكس) and conjoin it with the original true premise. If this yields an impossibility, the contradictory must be false, so the converse is true.
This is a standard reductio argument.
3
Understand proof by conversion of the contradictory ('aks)
Convert the contradictory of the converse into a proposition that contradicts the original premise. Since the original is assumed true, this converted contradictory must be false, implying the original contradictory is false and thus the converse is true.
This relies on the validity of conversion for the contradictory.
4
Understand proof by assumption (iftirad)
Dussuqi summarizes: 'positing the subject itself as a specific thing.' 'Aqid's gloss explains: take an individual falling under the subject, then form two propositions attributing the subject and predicate to that individual. This yields a concrete test: if the individual is indeed both subject and predicate, the conversion holds.
This method is often illustrated with variables like ج and د, representing the subject and predicate terms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والبيان في انعكاس هاتين القضيَّتين هو الافتراضُ، وذلكَ طريق آخرُ فى إثبات العكوس. ومحصِّلُهُ : فَرْضُ ذاتِ الموضوعِ شيئاً معيناً . الدسوقي (قَوْلُهُ : وَالْبَيَانُ) أي : الدليل على انعكاس ... إلخ . (قَوْلُهُ: وَذَلِكَ) أي: الافتراض. (قَوْلُهُ : طَرِيْقٌ آخَرُ ) أي : غير طريقِ الخلفي وغير طريق العكس. والحاصل : أنّ الأدلة التي يستدلَّ بها على العكس ثلاثة : دليل الخلف، ودليل العكس، ودليل الافتراض، فالأوّل : أن تضمَّ نقيض العكس للأصل ينتج المحال، وما جاء المحال إلا من نقيض العكس، فيكون نقيض العكس كاذباً والعكس صادقاً ، وهو المطلوب. والثَّاني : هو أن تعكس نقي العكس إلى ما يُناقضُ الأصل المفروض الصدق، وَمَا ناقض الصَّادق فهو كاذب، وإذا كذب عكسُ النَّقيض؛ كانَ النَّقيضُ كاذباً ؛ لأنَّ كذبَ اللازم يستلزم كذبَ الملزوم ، وإذا كان نقيضُ العكس كاذباً؛ كانَ العكس صادقاً، وهو المطلوب.
