And the demonstration of the conversion of these two propositions is assumption, and that is another way for establishing converses. Its summary: positing the subject itself as a specific thing. al-Dussuqi: His saying: 'and the demonstration' means the proof of the conversion... etc. His saying: 'and that' means assumption. His saying: 'another way' means other than the way of reductio and other than the way of conversion. The conclusion: the proofs used for conversion are three: proof by reductio, proof by conversion, and proof by assumption. The first: you join the contradictory of the converse with the original, it yields an impossibility; the impossibility arises only from the contradictory of the converse, so the contradictory of the converse is false and the converse is true, and that is the desired. The second: you convert the contradictory of the converse into something that contradicts the original assumed true; whatever contradicts the true is false, and if the converse of the contradictory is false, then the contradictory is false because the falsity of the consequent entails the falsity of the antecedent; if the contradictory of the converse is false, then the converse is true, and that is the desired. His saying: 'its summary' means summary of assumption. 'Aqd: His saying: 'positing the subject itself' and it is that to which the title of the subject applies, so there occurs a positing and attribution of the two descriptions of subject and predicate, so two propositions occur, as when we say: J (B) and attributing J and B to D by saying: D is J and D is B, and the rest will follow.
Source pages p. 730

Commentary
This passage is a commentary by Dussuqi explaining the method of assumption (iftirad) and enumerating the three proofs of conversion. The main text first states that the demonstration of the conversion of two specific propositions is by assumption, which is another way. Its summary is to posit the subject as a specific instance. Dussuqi clarifies that 'demonstration' means the proof, 'that' refers to assumption, and 'another way' means besides the two known ways: proof by contradiction (khalf) and proof by conversion of the contradictory ('aks al-naqid). He then details the three proofs. The first proof (khalf) works by combining the contradictory of the converse with the original premise to produce an impossibility, showing that the contradictory must be false and the converse true. The second proof ('aks al-naqid) works by converting the contradictory of the converse into something that contradicts the original, which must be false, leading to the falsity of the contradictory, thus the converse is true. The third proof (iftirad) is the subject of the main text. Then a commentator named 'Aqid (possibly a gloss) explains the phrase 'positing the subject itself': it means taking something that falls under the subject term, then combining the two descriptions (subject and predicate) to form two propositions, e.g., with variables J, B, and D.

دليل العكس

Key Takeaway
Dussuqi distinguishes three methods to prove conversion—contradiction, contrapositive conversion, and assumption—each with a distinct logical mechanism.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Deriving the Converse of a Modal Particular Negative Using Syllogisms
Commentator demonstrates the conversion process using syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a third-figure syllogism to derive the converse of a particular negative modal proposition and reduce it to the first figure.

Core Idea
Modal particular negatives convert via implied affirmatives and third-figure syllogisms.

This lesson walks you through the commentator's procedure for converting a particular negative modal proposition by assuming a specific individual, constructing two third-figure syllogisms, and reducing them to the first figure. By following the steps, you will understand how the converse is derived.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original proposition
The original proposition is 'Some writer is not still-fingered as long as he writes, not always'. Note the modal qualification 'not always' which implies non-permanence.
This proposition is a particular negative with a modal condition.
2
Assume a specific individual
Assume that 'some writer' refers to a specific person, Zayd. This allows us to treat the proposition as applying to an individual, which will facilitate syllogistic reasoning.
Zayd is a common placeholder in Arabic logic.
3
Construct the first syllogism (third figure)
From the assumption, we derive two premises: (1) Zayd is still-fingered (from the predicate), and (2) Zayd is not a writer as long as he is still-fingered (from the original negated predicate with subject condition). Then combine them: Zayd is still-fingered; Zayd is not a writer as long as he is still-fingered. This is a third-figure syllogism (middle term Zayd subject in both).
The third figure has the middle term as subject in both premises.
4
Reduce to first figure
Convert the minor premise 'Zayd is still-fingered' to 'Some still-fingered is Zayd' (conversion of particular affirmative). Then the syllogism becomes first figure: Some still-fingered is Zayd; Zayd is not a writer as long as he is still-fingered; therefore Some still-fingered is not a writer as long as he is still-fingered.
This conversion of the minor is valid because it is a particular affirmative proposition.
5
Derive the second part of the converse
Similarly, use the premises 'Zayd is still-fingered' and 'Zayd is a writer' (the affirmative counterpart) to derive 'Some still-fingered is a writer'. This completes the full converse of the original modal proposition.
The full converse consists of both a negative part and an affirmative part.

الشَّرنوبي.
Section: On Simple Conversion
And applying the two descriptions of subject and predicate to it, so that the concept of conversion is obtained. And you should mention for this investigation an increase of verification in the conversion of the contradictory.
Al-Dasuqi
(His saying: the two descriptions of subject and predicate) i.e., their concepts. (His saying: and you should mention for this investigation) i.e., at the statement of the author: and he explained the conversion of the two specials, and its clarification: that our statement in the preceding original, which is: 'Some writer is not still-fingered as long as he writes, not always', we assume that 'some' to be a specific thing like Zayd, and we apply to it the description of the subject, which is 'writer', so we say: Zayd is a writer. And the proof of this proposition is the truth of the description of the subject on its individuals. And we also apply to it the description of the predicate, so a second premise is obtained, which is: Zayd is still-fingered. Then we bring a third premise whose truth is established by invalidating the necessary of its contradictory, then we combine it with the second premise of the two premises of the hypothesis stating: Zayd is still-fingered, by making this the minor and that the major, there results a syllogism from the third figure, and it is reduced to the first by converting its minor thus: 'Some still-fingered is Zayd, and Zayd is not a writer as long as he is still-fingered', producing: 'Some still-fingered is not a writer as long as he is still-fingered'. And this result is exactly the first part of the converse. Then we take the two premises of the hypothesis and advance the second and make it minor, there results a syllogism from the third figure also thus: Zayd is still-fingered, and Zayd is a writer, and it is reduced to the first by converting the minor thus: 'Some still-fingered is Zayd, and Zayd is a writer', producing: 'Some still-fingered is a writer', which is the second part of the converse.
(His saying: upon it) i.e., upon the essence of the subject assumed as a specific thing.
(1) (His saying: and it is Zayd... etc.) And its proof is also the truth of the predicate on the essence of the subject due to the necessity of the identity of the predicate and subject in essence even if they differ in concept, and that by the rule of the non-permanence restricted to the original. (2) (His saying: by a third premise whose subject is the subject of each of the first or second and whose predicate is the predicate of the first, except that it is negative and restricted by the term of the predicate of the second, thus: Zayd is not a writer as long as he is still-fingered.) (3) (His saying: from the third figure, which is where the middle term is subject in each of them. And by composing it from the second and third premises mentioned, its form is thus: Zayd is still-fingered, Zayd is not a writer as long as he is still-fingered. And the manner of its reduction to the first is in the gloss.) (4) (His saying: from the converse i.e., the one just mentioned, and it is always: 'Some still-fingered is not a writer as long as he is still-fingered, not always'.)

If you say: The author mentioned at the beginning of the chapter that the particular negative does not convert, and you explicitly stated the conversion of the two specials from the particular negative. I say: The author meant by the non-conversion of the particular negative that it does not convert with respect to quantity, and we affirm its conversion with respect to modality.
Al-Dasuqi
...still-fingered, and Zayd is a writer, and it is reduced to the first by converting the minor thus: 'Some still-fingered is Zayd, and Zayd is a writer', producing: 'Some still-fingered is a writer', which is the second part of the converse.
(His saying: and we affirm its conversion with respect to modality) There is in it: that this is not apparent with respect to the conversion of the 'urfiyya khassa to 'urfiyya khassa, for the modality is the same in both, and then this answer does not appear.
Al-'Attar
(His saying: If you say) The origin of this question is the negation of the conversion of the mashruta and the 'urfiyya khassa to 'urfiyya khassa.
(His saying: I say: The author intended...) And al-Harawi answered with another answer, which is that this conversion is on account of the positive part understood from the condition of non-permanence. End. As for the glossator's analogy in his answer that the consideration in the composite proposition is only the first part, it is nonsense, because there is no mention in the answer of explaining why it is positive or negative, but rather he explained that...
(1) (His saying: 'writer' meaning actually, and his saying: 'which is the second part of the converse' which is referred to as 'not always'.) (2) (His saying: does not appear... etc.) i.e., even if it appears with respect to the particular negative mashruta khassa, so what is this answer? And similarly the second, because the converse is necessary and does not differ, and they may differ in the particular... completed: the particular negative is general in subject, and the correctness of conversion here and in the case of 'Some human is not white' is due to the particularity of the matter. So the truth in the answer is what al-Harawi said: that the correctness of conversion here is with respect to the consequent, which is non-permanence, because it indicates a particular affirmative. If the objection of al-Allama Yas is accepted against it, that the consideration in the composite is the affirmation or negation of the first part, not the consequent, then the conversion of the particular negative absolutely, even the two specials, would be invalid, otherwise contradiction would arise in their statements and inconsistency in their rules. And by what we have mentioned, you know what is in al-'Attar's criticism and verbosity without restraint. End. By al-Sharnubi.
Source pages p. 731

Commentary
This passage is a hashiya (super-commentary) on the section about simple conversion ('aks mustawi) from a matn on logic. The commentator, al-Dasuqi, begins by explaining the author's phrase 'applying the two descriptions of subject and predicate' to obtain the concept of conversion. He then focuses on the conversion of the 'two specials' (al-khassatayn), which are modal propositions with conditions of permanence and non-permanence. The original proposition is: 'Some writer is not still-fingered as long as he writes, but not always.' This is a particular negative proposition with a modal qualification. The commentator clarifies how to convert it by assuming a specific individual, Zayd. He constructs two syllogisms in the third figure to derive the two parts of the converse. First, from premises 'Zayd is still-fingered' and 'Zayd is not a writer as long as he is still-fingered', he deduces 'Some still-fingered is not a writer as long as he is still-fingered'. Then, from 'Zayd is still-fingered' and 'Zayd is a writer', he deduces 'Some still-fingered is a writer'. The first part is the negative part of the converse, the second part is the affirmative part. The syllogisms are reduced to the first figure by converting the minor premise (e.g., 'Some still-fingered is Zayd' from 'Zayd is still-fingered' by conversion of a particular affirmative).

Then a question arises: the matn author said that the particular negative does not convert, but here the commentator is converting one. The commentator answers that the matn meant non-conversion with respect to quantity (i.e., particular negative does not become particular negative in simple conversion of quantity), but here we convert with respect to modality (jiha). That is, the converse is a different modal proposition, not a change in quantity.

Al-'Attar objects that this answer does not work for the case of 'urfiyya khassa to 'urfiyya khassa because the modality is the same. Therefore, the answer fails. Al-Sharnubi then provides the correct answer from al-Harawi: the conversion is valid because it relies on the positive part understood from the condition of non-permanence (al-ladawam). The condition 'not always' implies a particular affirmative (e.g., 'Some writer is still-fingered at some time'). Thus, the conversion is actually of the implied affirmative, not the negative itself. Al-Sharnubi criticizes al-'Attar's objection as verbose and incorrect, emphasizing that the consideration in composite propositions is not only the first part but the entire meaning. He concludes that without this understanding, inconsistency would arise in logical rules.

Checkpoint
Why does the commentator argue that the particular negative proposition 'Some writer is not still-fingered as long as he writes (not always)' can be converted?
A) Because it becomes a universal negative in quantity.
B) Because the conversion is in modality, not quantity, and relies on the implied affirmative from the non-permanence condition.
C) Because all particular negatives can be converted by simple conversion.
D) Because the third figure syllogism guarantees conversion.

Key Takeaway
Particular negative modal propositions can be converted in modality, not quantity, by using a third-figure syllogism and the implied affirmative from the non-permanence condition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
1. Construct a third-figure syllogism to derive the converse of the modal proposition 'Some fasting person is not breaking the fast as long as they are conscious (not always).' Use a concrete individual like 'Zayd' and show the steps.
?
2. Reduce your syllogism to the first figure and verify the conclusion. Then identify the two parts of the converse (negative and affirmative).

Immediate Inference
advanced
10 min
Resolving the Apparent Contradiction: Conversion in Modality vs. Quantity
Dialectical resolution of the conflict between matn and commentary on conversion of modal negatives.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the apparent contradiction in the conversion of modal negatives and explain the resolution using the distinction between conversion in quantity and modality.

Core Idea
The matn's denial of particular negative conversion applies to quantity; the commentator's affirmation applies to modality.

A question arises: the matn author says particular negatives do not convert, yet the commentator proves they do. This lesson examines the dialectical exchange among logicians who debated this issue, focusing on the distinction between conversion in quantity and conversion in modality.

Guided Path
1
State the apparent contradiction
The matn author states that the particular negative proposition does not convert. However, the commentator demonstrates the conversion of a particular negative modal proposition. This seems contradictory.
The contradiction is not in the same terms: one is about quantity, the other about modality.
2
Extract the objector's challenge
The objector (mu'tarid) says: 'The author said the particular negative does not convert, but you are converting it.' This is the core of the dialectical exchange.
The objector is assuming the matn's statement is absolute.
3
Analyze the commentator's first response
The commentator responds: 'The author meant non-conversion with respect to quantity; we are converting with respect to modality.' This resolves the contradiction by distinguishing two different aspects of conversion.
Quantity refers to the number of individuals (particular vs universal); modality refers to the mode (permanence, conditionality).
4
Consider al-'Attar's objection
Al-'Attar objects that this response fails for the case of 'urfiyya khassa because the modality is the same in both original and converse. Thus, the answer does not appear satisfactory.
Al-'Attar points to a potential flaw in the commentator's defense.
5
Evaluate al-Sharnubi's resolution
Al-Sharnubi provides al-Harawi's answer: the conversion is valid because it relies on the positive part understood from the condition of non-permanence (al-ladawam). The modal condition 'not always' implies a particular affirmative, allowing conversion of the implied affirmative. This preserves consistency in logical rules.
The key is that the modal negative contains an implicit affirmative due to the non-permanence condition.

Section: On Simple Conversion
And applying the two descriptions of subject and predicate to it, so that the concept of conversion is obtained. And you should mention for this investigation an increase of verification in the conversion of the contradictory.
Al-Dasuqi
(His saying: the two descriptions of subject and predicate) i.e., their concepts. (His saying: and you should mention for this investigation) i.e., at the statement of the author: and he explained the conversion of the two specials, and its clarification: that our statement in the preceding original, which is: 'Some writer is not still-fingered as long as he writes, not always', we assume that 'some' to be a specific thing like Zayd, and we apply to it the description of the subject, which is 'writer', so we say: Zayd is a writer. And the proof of this proposition is the truth of the description of the subject on its individuals. And we also apply to it the description of the predicate, so a second premise is obtained, which is: Zayd is still-fingered. Then we bring a third premise whose truth is established by invalidating the necessary of its contradictory, then we combine it with the second premise of the two premises of the hypothesis stating: Zayd is still-fingered, by making this the minor and that the major, there results a syllogism from the third figure, and it is reduced to the first by converting its minor thus: 'Some still-fingered is Zayd, and Zayd is not a writer as long as he is still-fingered', producing: 'Some still-fingered is not a writer as long as he is still-fingered'. And this result is exactly the first part of the converse. Then we take the two premises of the hypothesis and advance the second and make it minor, there results a syllogism from the third figure also thus: Zayd is still-fingered, and Zayd is a writer, and it is reduced to the first by converting the minor thus: 'Some still-fingered is Zayd, and Zayd is a writer', producing: 'Some still-fingered is a writer', which is the second part of the converse.
(His saying: upon it) i.e., upon the essence of the subject assumed as a specific thing.
(1) (His saying: and it is Zayd... etc.) And its proof is also the truth of the predicate on the essence of the subject due to the necessity of the identity of the predicate and subject in essence even if they differ in concept, and that by the rule of the non-permanence restricted to the original. (2) (His saying: by a third premise whose subject is the subject of each of the first or second and whose predicate is the predicate of the first, except that it is negative and restricted by the term of the predicate of the second, thus: Zayd is not a writer as long as he is still-fingered.) (3) (His saying: from the third figure, which is where the middle term is subject in each of them. And by composing it from the second and third premises mentioned, its form is thus: Zayd is still-fingered, Zayd is not a writer as long as he is still-fingered. And the manner of its reduction to the first is in the gloss.) (4) (His saying: from the converse i.e., the one just mentioned, and it is always: 'Some still-fingered is not a writer as long as he is still-fingered, not always'.)

If you say: The author mentioned at the beginning of the chapter that the particular negative does not convert, and you explicitly stated the conversion of the two specials from the particular negative. I say: The author meant by the non-conversion of the particular negative that it does not convert with respect to quantity, and we affirm its conversion with respect to modality.
Al-Dasuqi
...still-fingered, and Zayd is a writer, and it is reduced to the first by converting the minor thus: 'Some still-fingered is Zayd, and Zayd is a writer', producing: 'Some still-fingered is a writer', which is the second part of the converse.
(His saying: and we affirm its conversion with respect to modality) There is in it: that this is not apparent with respect to the conversion of the 'urfiyya khassa to 'urfiyya khassa, for the modality is the same in both, and then this answer does not appear.
Al-'Attar
(His saying: If you say) The origin of this question is the negation of the conversion of the mashruta and the 'urfiyya khassa to 'urfiyya khassa.
(His saying: I say: The author intended...) And al-Harawi answered with another answer, which is that this conversion is on account of the positive part understood from the condition of non-permanence. End. As for the glossator's analogy in his answer that the consideration in the composite proposition is only the first part, it is nonsense, because there is no mention in the answer of explaining why it is positive or negative, but rather he explained that...
(1) (His saying: 'writer' meaning actually, and his saying: 'which is the second part of the converse' which is referred to as 'not always'.) (2) (His saying: does not appear... etc.) i.e., even if it appears with respect to the particular negative mashruta khassa, so what is this answer? And similarly the second, because the converse is necessary and does not differ, and they may differ in the particular... completed: the particular negative is general in subject, and the correctness of conversion here and in the case of 'Some human is not white' is due to the particularity of the matter. So the truth in the answer is what al-Harawi said: that the correctness of conversion here is with respect to the consequent, which is non-permanence, because it indicates a particular affirmative. If the objection of al-Allama Yas is accepted against it, that the consideration in the composite is the affirmation or negation of the first part, not the consequent, then the conversion of the particular negative absolutely, even the two specials, would be invalid, otherwise contradiction would arise in their statements and inconsistency in their rules. And by what we have mentioned, you know what is in al-'Attar's criticism and verbosity without restraint. End. By al-Sharnubi.
Source pages p. 731

Commentary
This passage is a hashiya (super-commentary) on the section about simple conversion ('aks mustawi) from a matn on logic. The commentator, al-Dasuqi, begins by explaining the author's phrase 'applying the two descriptions of subject and predicate' to obtain the concept of conversion. He then focuses on the conversion of the 'two specials' (al-khassatayn), which are modal propositions with conditions of permanence and non-permanence. The original proposition is: 'Some writer is not still-fingered as long as he writes, but not always.' This is a particular negative proposition with a modal qualification. The commentator clarifies how to convert it by assuming a specific individual, Zayd. He constructs two syllogisms in the third figure to derive the two parts of the converse. First, from premises 'Zayd is still-fingered' and 'Zayd is not a writer as long as he is still-fingered', he deduces 'Some still-fingered is not a writer as long as he is still-fingered'. Then, from 'Zayd is still-fingered' and 'Zayd is a writer', he deduces 'Some still-fingered is a writer'. The first part is the negative part of the converse, the second part is the affirmative part. The syllogisms are reduced to the first figure by converting the minor premise (e.g., 'Some still-fingered is Zayd' from 'Zayd is still-fingered' by conversion of a particular affirmative).

Then a question arises: the matn author said that the particular negative does not convert, but here the commentator is converting one. The commentator answers that the matn meant non-conversion with respect to quantity (i.e., particular negative does not become particular negative in simple conversion of quantity), but here we convert with respect to modality (jiha). That is, the converse is a different modal proposition, not a change in quantity.

Al-'Attar objects that this answer does not work for the case of 'urfiyya khassa to 'urfiyya khassa because the modality is the same. Therefore, the answer fails. Al-Sharnubi then provides the correct answer from al-Harawi: the conversion is valid because it relies on the positive part understood from the condition of non-permanence (al-ladawam). The condition 'not always' implies a particular affirmative (e.g., 'Some writer is still-fingered at some time'). Thus, the conversion is actually of the implied affirmative, not the negative itself. Al-Sharnubi criticizes al-'Attar's objection as verbose and incorrect, emphasizing that the consideration in composite propositions is not only the first part but the entire meaning. He concludes that without this understanding, inconsistency would arise in logical rules.

Key Takeaway

Objection and Answer: Conversion of Particular Negative by Modality
Author resolves contradiction between matn and sharh on conversion of particular negative.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between conversion by quantity and conversion by modality in immediate inferences, and resolve apparent contradictions in logical texts.

Core Idea
A particular negative can convert by modality even if it cannot by quantity.

In this passage, an objector points out a seeming contradiction: the original text (matn) says the particular negative does not convert, but the commentary (sharh) appears to allow it. The answer draws a crucial distinction between conversion by quantity (al-kamm) and conversion by modality (al-jiha). Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly applying conversion rules in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
What exactly does the objector claim? Locate the two conflicting statements: the matn's claim that the particular negative does not convert, and the sharh's allowance of conversion for two specific types.
2
Extract the distinction in the answer
The answer says the matn refers to conversion by quantity, while the sharh addresses conversion by modality. Think about what each type means in terms of the proposition's form.
Quantity concerns whether the subject term is universal or particular; modality concerns necessity, perpetuity, possibility, etc.
3
Analyze the example
The example uses 'saakin al-asabi'' (a person with still fingers) and Zaid. Trace the conversion: from 'Zaid is a saakin al-asabi'' to 'Some saakin al-asabi' is Zaid', then to 'Some saakin al-asabi' is a writer'. How does this illustrate conversion by modality?
The minor premise is converted, allowing a conclusion in the first figure.
4
Test the division
If someone says 'Some A is not B cannot convert in any way,' how would you respond using the distinction? Create a scenario from fiqh or kalam to test the rule.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قلت: قد ذكر المصنِّفُ في أوَّلِ الفصل: أنَّ السالبة الجزئية لا تنعكس، وأنتَ صرَّحْتَ بانعكاس الخاصَّتين من السالبة الجزئية. قُلْتُ: أراد المصنِّفُ بعدم انعكاس السَّالبة الجزئية، أنَّها لا تنعكس بحسب الكم، ونحن نثبتُ انعكاسها بحسب الجهة. ساكن الأصابع، وزيد كاتب، وهو يرتد للأوَّل بعكس الصغرى هكذا: بعضُ السَّاكن زيد، وزيدٌ كاتب، ينتج: بعضُ السَّاكن كاتب، وهو الجزء الثاني من العكس.
If you say: The author stated at the beginning of the chapter that the particular negative does not convert, yet you declared the conversion of the two specific propositions from the particular negative. I say: The author meant by the non-conversion of the particular negative that it does not convert with respect to quantity, and we affirm its conversion with respect to modality. [Example:] 'Someone with still fingers, and Zaid is a writer' — it returns to the first by converting the minor premise thus: 'Some of those with still fingers is Zaid, and Zaid is a writer' yields 'Some of those with still fingers is a writer', and this is the second part of the conversion.
Source pages p. 732

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical exchange (jadal) in which an objector points out an apparent inconsistency: the matn (original text) stated that particular negative propositions do not convert, yet the sharh (commentary) allowed conversion for two specific types (al-khāṣṣatān) derived from the particular negative. The answer resolves this by distinguishing two aspects: quantity (al-kamm) and modality (al-jiha). The matn's prohibition applies to conversion in terms of quantity—e.g., 'Some A is not B' cannot become 'Some B is not A' straightforwardly. However, conversion by modality means that when we consider the modality of the proposition (e.g., necessity, perpetuity), the particular negative can be converted to another proposition with a different modality. The example illustrates this: starting from a premise like 'Some person with still fingers is Zaid' (converted from 'Zaid is a person with still fingers' by converting the minor premise), we conclude 'Some person with still fingers is a writer'. The 'second part of the conversion' refers to the result of the conversion process. This distinction is a standard logical maneuver to reconcile different statements about conversion rules.

العكس

Key Takeaway
Conversion of a particular negative is possible by modality even though it is not possible by quantity, resolving apparent contradictions in logical texts.

Why There is No Contradiction in Simple Conversion (al-'Aks al-Mustawī)
Author opens chapter on simple conversion and explains why there is no contradiction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconcile an apparent contradiction between a general rule of conversion and a specific case by qualifying the rule's universality.

Core Idea
A general logical rule may admit exceptions without contradiction.

In this passage, the author opens the chapter on simple conversion and immediately addresses a potential objection: how can the conversion of specific modal propositions be valid if the rule says the particular negative does not convert? The commentator al-Dasuqi explains that the rule is not absolute; it allows for exceptions. This teaches us a key principle in logic: general rules must be interpreted in light of their universality.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule
Find the general rule about conversion: the particular negative (al-saliba al-juz'iyya) does not convert. This is stated in the matn.
The rule is a standard immediate inference principle.
2
Identify the apparent exception
Notice that the conversion of the two specific propositions (al-khāṣṣatān) to a specific conditional seems to conflict with the general rule.
The two specific propositions are modal: al-mashrūṭa al-khāṣṣa and al-'urfiyya al-khāṣṣa.
3
Understand the reconciliation
Al-Dasuqi explains that 'fa-lā taḍādda' means there is no contradiction because the general rule is not universal; it is a general statement that may have exceptions when modality is considered.
The author's phrase 'or by modality' (wa-immā bi-ḥasab al-jiha) supports this qualification.
4
Apply the principle
Recognize that in logic, rules are often qualified by conditions. The same method applies to other sciences: a general ruling may have exceptions without logical inconsistency.
This teaches critical thinking: always check if a rule is universal or general.

Source Text

فَصْلٌ: فِي الْعَكْسِ الْمُسْتَوِي فَلَا تَضَادَّ . وَيَدُلُّ عَلَى صِحَّةِ هَذَا التَّوْجِيهِ قَوْلُ الْمُصَنِّفِ: «وَإِمَّا بِحَسَبِ الْجِهَةِ». وَيُمْكِنُ أَنْ يُقَالَ : مَعْنَى قَوْلِهِ : «وَالسَّالِبَةُ الْجُزْئِيَّةُ لَا تَنْعَكِسُ»؛ أَيْ: لَا يَلْزَمُهَا الْعَكْسُ لُزُومًا كُلِّيًّا. الدسوقي (قَوْلُهُ : فَلَا تَضَادَّ) أَيْ : فَلَا تَنَافِيَ بَيْنَ عَكْسِ الْخَاصَّتَيْنِ الْمَذْكُورَتَيْنِ إِلَى الْعُرْفِيَّةِ الْخَاصَّةِ، وَبَيْنَ قَوْلِ الْمُصَنِّفِ : إِنَّ السَّالِبَةَ الْجُزْئِيَّةَ لَا تَنْعَكِسُ. (قَوْلُهُ : وَيُمْكِنُ أَنْ يُقَالَ : ( جَوَابٌ آخَرُ .
Chapter on Simple Conversion. So there is no contradiction. And it indicates the correctness of this direction the author's statement: 'or by modality.' And it is possible to say: the meaning of his statement 'the particular negative does not convert' is: it is not necessarily entailed by conversion universally. al-Dasuqi: His statement 'so there is no contradiction' means: there is no conflict between the conversion of the two specific propositions mentioned to the specific conditional, and the author's statement that the particular negative does not convert. His statement 'and it is possible to say' is another answer.
Source pages p. 733

Commentary
This passage opens a new chapter (faṣl) on simple conversion (al-'aks al-mustawī). The statement 'so there is no contradiction' (fa-lā taḍādda) directly addresses the earlier objection: the conversion of the two specific propositions (al-khāṣṣatān) to a specific conditional does not contradict the matn's rule that the particular negative does not convert. The reason is that the matn's rule is not universal; it is a general statement that admits exceptions. The author's phrase 'or by modality' (wa-immā bi-ḥasab al-jiha) supports this interpretation. Al-Dasuqi's gloss explains that 'no contradiction' means no conflict between the specific conversions and the general rule. He then notes that 'and it is possible to say' introduces another possible answer to the objection, which is discussed in the following lines. This passage exemplifies the method of reconciling apparent contradictions by qualifying general rules.

The Particular Negative Does Not Convert Universally
Al-Dasuqi refines the explanation of why the particular negative does not convert.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the particular negative proposition does not universally entail its converse and identify the meaning of 'luzuman kulliyyan'.

Core Idea
The particular negative does not convert universally, but may convert under specific restrictions.

In simple conversion ('aks mustawi), most categorical propositions convert while preserving truth. However, the particular negative (al-saliba al-juz'iyya) is an exception: it does not entail its converse in every case. The text begins by noting that the author's phrase 'or according to modality' points to this refined understanding. Let us examine the exact meaning of this rule.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposition type
Look at the proposition 'Some A is not B'. It is a particular negative (O-type). Recognize that in simple conversion, we exchange subject and predicate.
2
Understand the rule
The text states: 'والسالبة الجزئية لا تنعكس' — the particular negative does not convert. But it clarifies: 'لا يلزمها العكس لزوماً كلّياً' — it does not entail the converse universally. This means it is not a necessary entailment in all cases.
The key term is 'luzuman kulliyyan' (universally necessary).
3
Test with an example
Consider the proposition 'Some acts are not obligatory'. If we convert it to 'Some non-obligatory acts are not acts', the truth of the original does not guarantee the truth of the converse. For instance, if all acts are either obligatory or permissible, then 'Some acts are not obligatory' is true, but 'Some non-obligatory acts are not acts' is false because non-obligatory acts are still acts.
Thus the converse does not follow necessarily.
4
Recognize the possibility of conversion with restrictions
The text hints that under certain restrictions (e.g., modality), the particular negative may convert. This does not contradict the general rule because the rule means 'not universally'.
Restrictions (qiyood) can affect logical behavior.

فَصْلٌ: فِي الْعَكْسِ الْمُسْتَوِي. ويدلُّ على صحة هذا التوجيه قول المصنف: «وإما بحسب الجهة». ويمكنُ أنْ يُقالَ : معنى قوله : والسَّالبة الجزئية لا تنعكس»؛ أي: لا يلزمها العكس لزوماً كلّيًّا. قال الدسوقي: فلا تضاد أي فلا تنافي بين عكس الخاصَّتين المذكورتين إلى العرفية الخاصَّةِ، وبين قول المصنِّف : إنَّ السَّالبة الجزئية لا تنعكس. ويمكن أن يقال: جواب آخر. قال العطار: ذلك الانعكاس باعتبار الجزء الإيجابي، فَهذا بيان لمحل الانعكاس؛ لا تعليل للإيجاب أو السَّلْبِ حتّى يرد عليه ما ذكر، وكذلك تنظيرُهُ في جوابِ الشَّارحِ بِقَولِهِ بأنَّ الجهة بيان للقضيَّة، وبيان لِحالِ نِسبتها في الواقع، وإذا كان أصل القضيَّة لا ينعكس؛ فالقيد لا ينفعُ في الانعكاس ا.هـ؛ لأنَّ مَعنى كلامِ الشَّارحِ أَنَّ القضيَّةَ نفسَها انعكسَتْ باعتبارِ مُلاحظةِ الجهةِ إلى ما ذكر، كما أنَّهُ بِاعتبارِ مُلاحظةِ الكَمّ لم تنعكش، فليست الجهة بعينها منعكسةً ، وقوله : وإذا كان أصل القضيَّة . .. إلخ، كلامٌ ليسَ لهُ معنّى مُحصَّلٌ ; لأنَّ لِلقيودِ تأثيراً في اختلافِ الأحكام، فالقضيَّةُ مُجرَّدةٌ عن ملاحظة هذا القيد واعتباره لا عكس لها وباعتباره تنعكس.
Section: On Simple Conversion. The author's statement 'or according to modality' indicates the correctness of this direction. And it can be said: the meaning of his statement 'and the particular negative does not convert' is that the converse is not entailed by it universally. Al-Dasuqi said: 'So there is no contradiction' meaning there is no conflict between the conversion of the two mentioned special [propositions] to particular affirmative and the author's statement that the particular negative does not convert. And it can be said: another answer. Al-'Attar said: That conversion is considered from the affirmative part; so this is an indication of the locus of conversion, not a justification for affirmation or negation such that the mentioned objection could be raised. And likewise his analogy in the commentator's answer by his statement that the modality is an indication of the proposition and an indication of the state of its relation in reality, and if the original proposition does not convert, then the restriction is of no benefit in conversion. This is because the meaning of the commentator's statement is that the proposition itself converted by considering the modality to what was mentioned, just as by considering quantity it did not convert; so modality itself is not converted. And his statement 'If the original proposition does not convert... etc.' is a statement that has no sound meaning, because restrictions have an effect in differing rulings: the proposition stripped of considering this restriction and its consideration has no converse, and with its consideration it converts.
Source pages p. 733

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement from the super-commentator (al-Dasuqi) addressing the rule of simple conversion ('aks mustawi) for particular negative propositions. The main claim under discussion is that the particular negative proposition does not entail its converse universally. The author of the original sharh had stated that the particular negative does not convert, but the super-commentator notes that this does not contradict the conversion of two specific propositions (the 'special ones') to particular affirmative. He clarifies that 'no contradiction' means the two statements can both be true: the particular negative does not convert in every case, but those two special propositions do convert under certain conditions. Then a second answer is proposed: that the conversion is based on considering the affirmative part of the proposition, not the negative. This is explained by al-'Attar: the conversion is about the locus of conversion (the affirmative component), not a justification of affirmation or negation. The super-commentator then critiques the commentator's analogy about modality (al-jiha), arguing that if the original proposition does not convert, adding a modality restriction does not help. He calls the commentator's statement unsound because restrictions do affect logical rulings: a proposition stripped of the restriction does not convert, but with the restriction it does. This demonstrates the principle that qiyood (restrictions) can change the logical behavior of propositions. Throughout, the passage exemplifies the rigorous analytical style of the super-commentary tradition, where every nuance is scrutinized for consistency.

Key Terms
العكس المستوي
السالبة الجزئية
لزوماً كلّياً

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The particular negative proposition never converts under any condition.
B) The particular negative proposition does not entail its converse universally.
C) The particular negative proposition converts only if it is modal.
D) The particular negative proposition is always false after conversion.

Key Takeaway
The particular negative proposition does not universally entail its converse, leaving room for conversion under specific restrictions.

Resolving Apparent Contradictions: The Role of Restrictions in Conversion
Al-Dasuqi resolves the apparent contradiction between the conversion of two specific propositions and the rule that the particular negative does not convert, and critiques the commentator's use of modality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to resolve the apparent contradiction between the rule that the particular negative does not convert and the fact that some specific propositions do convert, by understanding the role of restrictions (qiyood).

Core Idea
Restrictions (qiyood) can change the logical behavior of propositions; a proposition may convert with a restriction but not without.

Al-Dasuqi addresses a potential confusion: if the particular negative does not convert, how can two special propositions (al-khāssatayn) convert to particular affirmative? He explains there is no contradiction. He also critiques the commentator's attempt to use modality as a justification, arguing that restrictions do affect rulings. This lesson unpacks his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the apparent contradiction
The author of the original sharh says 'the particular negative does not convert', yet earlier it was claimed that two specific propositions (e.g., universal negative and particular affirmative) convert to particular affirmative. This seems contradictory.
We need to understand what 'al-khāssatayn' refers to in context.
2
Understand al-Dasuqi's resolution: 'no contradiction'
Al-Dasuqi says: 'فلا تضاد' meaning there is no conflict. The rule 'does not convert' applies universally, but those two propositions convert under specific conditions (e.g., when considering modality or matter). The universal rule does not negate particular exceptions.
The rule is about universal entailment, not absolute impossibility.
3
Analyze the second answer: conversion based on affirmative part
Al-'Attar explains that the conversion of those propositions is based on considering their affirmative part, not as full particular negatives. The conversion locus is the affirmative component, so it does not violate the rule for the negative as a whole.
This is a subtle distinction: the converse is drawn from the affirmative part, not the negative.
4
Critique the commentator's modality analogy
Al-Dasuqi rejects the idea that adding a modality restriction (al-jiha) would allow the original particular negative to convert. He argues that restrictions (qiyood) do affect rulings: a proposition stripped of the restriction does not convert, but with the restriction it can convert. The commentator's statement that 'if the original does not convert, the restriction is useless' is unsound.
This demonstrates that logical behavior is context-dependent.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Clarifying the Scope of Non-Conversion for the Particular Negative
Dasuqi and 'Attar clarify the scope of non-conversion for the particular negative.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the particular negative's failure to convert in one form does not imply it never converts, and identify the specific form in which it fails.

Core Idea
The particular negative does not convert in one specific form — not absolutely.

Here, Dasuqi and 'Attar resolve a potential contradiction. The author earlier stated that the particular negative does not convert. But other passages mention that two special characteristics of the particular negative do convert. Without clarification, a student might think the author contradicted himself. The commentators show that 'does not convert' refers only to simple conversion in one specific picture, not to all types of conversion or to all pictures.

Guided Path
1
Identify the apparent contradiction
The author said 'the particular negative does not convert' but later mentions conversion of its special characteristics. Why might this seem contradictory?
2
Locate the limitation
Notice Dasuqi's explanation: the non-conversion is in one picture only, not absolutely. This restricts the claim.
3
Understand the key phrase 'in one picture'
'In one picture' means a specific form of conversion (simple conversion). The particular negative fails only there, not in all forms.
4
See how 'Attar corrects a textual issue
'Attar notes that the correct reading is 'other than here' – meaning the picture referred to is not the one currently in the text. This shows the importance of textual criticism.
5
Conclude no contradiction
The author's claim is restricted, so it does not conflict with other statements about conversion of special characteristics.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بعدم انعكاسها في صورةٍ واحدةٍ فقط، ولا يقتضي عدم وذلك يتحقق انعكاسها مطلقاً.
الدسوقي
(قَوْلُهُ : وَذَلِكَ) أي: عدم لزوم العكس لها لزوما كليا .
(قَوْلُهُ : فِي صُوْرَةٍ وَاحِدَةٍ) وهو هُنَا (۱) .
(قَوْلُهُ : وَلَا يَقْتَضِي) أي : عدم لزوم انعكاسها في صورة واحدة.
(قَوْلُهُ : عَدَمَ انْعِكَاسِهَا مُطْلَقَاً) أي: في جميعِ الصُّوَرِ، وحينئذ؛ فَلا تنافي بين انعكاس الخاصَّتين المذكورتين، وبين قول المصنف : إِنَّ السَّالبة الجزئية لا تنعكش .
العطار
(۱) الصواب : غير ما هنا .
By its non-conversion in only one picture, and that does not entail non-conversion absolutely. Dasuqi: His saying: 'and that' means: the non-necessity of its conversion universally. His saying: 'in one picture' and it is here (1). His saying: 'and does not entail' means: the non-necessity of its conversion in one picture. His saying: 'non-conversion absolutely' means: in all pictures, and then there is no contradiction between the conversion of the two mentioned special characteristics and the author's statement that the particular negative does not convert. 'Attar: (1) The correct is: other than here.
Source pages p. 734

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement by Dasuqi and 'Attar on a previous point about the conversion of propositions. They clarify that the statement 'the particular negative does not convert' refers to its non-conversion in a specific picture (i.e., a particular form), not an absolute non-conversion in all pictures. Dasuqi explains that the phrase 'and that' refers to the non-necessity of conversion for it universally. He then explains that 'in one picture' specifies the case. Then 'and does not entail' is explained as the non-necessity of its conversion in that one picture. Finally, 'non-conversion absolutely' means in all pictures. Therefore, there is no contradiction between the conversion of the two special characteristics mentioned earlier and the author's claim that the particular negative does not convert, because the author's claim is limited to that one picture. 'Attar notes that the correct reading is different from what is here (possibly a textual variant). This guards against misunderstanding that the particular negative never converts—it only fails in a specific form.

Definition of Contraposition and Its Two Types
Author defines contraposition and divides it into muwafiq and mukhalif with example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to perform contraposition on a categorical proposition and identify whether the result is muwafiq or mukhalif.

Core Idea
Contraposition is the mirror of conversion, replacing terms with their contradictories.

In this lesson, we explore the immediate inference known as contraposition ('aks al-naqid). The author first defines the operation mathematically, then divides it into two types based on agreement in quality. With the help of Dasuqi and 'Attar, we will see how to apply it and avoid a common linguistic misunderstanding.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition
The author defines contraposition as replacing each term with its contradictory and swapping them while preserving truth and quality. Note that 'quality' refers to affirmation/negation, and 'truth' ensures the contrapositive is true if the original is true.
The definition uses the phrase 'replacing the contradictories of the two extremes' which 'Attar later clarifies should be understood as 'replacing each extreme by the contradictory of the other'.
2
Apply the operation to an example
Use the example given: 'Every J is B' becomes 'Every not-B is not-J'. In Islamic legal terms, consider 'Every intoxicant is forbidden' (universal affirmative). Its contrapositive is 'Every non-forbidden is non-intoxicant', which preserves truth: if all intoxicants are forbidden, then anything not forbidden cannot be an intoxicant.
This example is muwafiq because both quality (affirmative) and quantity (universal) are preserved.
3
Distinguish muwafiq from mukhalif
The contrapositive just produced preserves both quality and quantity, so it is muwafiq. If the quality changed (e.g., affirmative to negative), it would be mukhalif. The author only defines mukhalif as disagreeing in quality; some later texts also consider quantity.
4
Note the linguistic correction by 'Attar
'Attar points out that the matn's phrasing 'replacing the contradictories' could be misunderstood. The correct process is replacing each extreme by the contradictory of the other, not replacing the contradictories themselves. This fine point avoids confusion in applying the operation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: فِي عَكس النَّقِيْضِ الْمُوَافِقِ وَالْمُخَالِفِ
عَكْسُ النَّقِيض : تَبْدِيْلُ نَقِيْضَى الطَّرَفَيْن) بأن يُجعَلَ نقيضُ الجزء الأول ثانياً، ونقيضُ الجزء الثاني أولاً، (مَعَ بَقَاءِ الصِّدْقِ وَالكَيْفِ). فقولنا : «كُلُّ ج ب ينعكس بعكس النَّقيض إلى : كلُّ ما ليس ب ليس ج»، وهذا على رأي المتقدمين.
الدسوقي
(قَوْلُهُ : عَكْسُ النَّقِيض) سُمّي بذلك؛ لأنَّه يؤخذُ نقيضُ كل من الطَّرفين أوَّلاً، ثمَّ يعكس ذلك النقيض، وهو - أي العكس - على قسمين : عكس نقيض موافق، وعكس نقيض مخالف، وسُمِّي الأَوَّلُ: موافقاً ؛ لأنَّهُ مُوافق للأصل في الكيف والكم، وسُمِّيَ الثَّاني : مُخالفاً ؛ لأنَّهُ مُخالف للأصل في الكيف.
(قَوْلُهُ : بِأَنْ يُجْعَل . . . إلخ) تصوير للتبديل .
(قَوْلُهُ : وَالْكَيْفِ) أي: الإيجاب والسلب.
(قَوْلُهُ : كُلُّ ج (ب) أي : كلُّ إنسان حيوان .
(وقوله : إلى كلّ ما ليس ب ليسَ (ج) أي : كلُّ ما ليس بحيوان ليس بإنسان، وهذه القضيَّةُ موجبة معدولةُ الطَّرفين، وهذا هو القسم المسمى بعكس النقيض الموافقِ .
فَصْلٌ: فِي عَكْسِ النَّقِيْضِ
العطار
يُطلق أيضاً على المعنى المصدري وعلى القضيَّةِ الحاصلة منه، والمراد بتبديل نقيض الطَّرفين؛ تبديلُ كُلِّ مِنَ الطَّرفَيْنِ بِنقيض الطَّرَفِ الآخَرِ كَمَا أَشَارَ لِذلِكَ الشارح بقولِهِ : بأن يجعل نقيضُ ... إلخ، وَلَوْ قال المصنِّفُ : تبديل كُلِّ مِنَ الطَّرفَيْنِ بِنقيض الآخَرِ ؛ لأنَّ المبدل هو الطَّرفانِ بِنقيضيهما ؛ لَا النَّقيضان.
Chapter on contraposition (muwafiq and mukhalif). Contraposition is the replacement of the contradictories of the two extremes: by making the contradictory of the first part the second, and the contradictory of the second part the first, while preserving truth and quality. So our statement: 'Every J is B' is converted by contraposition to: 'Every not-B is not-J'. And this is according to the view of the early scholars. Dasuqi: His saying: 'aks al-naqid' is so called because the contradictory of each of the two extremes is taken first, then that contradictory is converted. And it—i.e., the conversion—is of two types: muwafiq contraposition and mukhalif contraposition. The first is called muwafiq because it agrees with the original in quality and quantity; the second is called mukhalif because it disagrees with the original in quality. His saying: 'by making... etc' is an illustration of the replacement. His saying: 'and quality' means affirmation and negation. His saying: 'Every J is B' means: Every human is an animal. And his saying: 'to every not-B is not-J' means: Every not-animal is not-human, and this proposition is an affirmative with negated two ends, and this is the type called muwafiq contraposition. 'Attar: It is also applied to the verbal noun and to the resulting proposition. And what is meant by 'replacement of the contradictories of the two extremes' is replacing each of the two extremes by the contradictory of the other, as the commentator indicated by his saying 'by making the contradictory... etc'. And if the author had said: 'replacing each of the two extremes by the contradictory of the other' because what is replaced are the two extremes by their contradictories, not the contradictories themselves.
Source pages p. 735

Commentary
This passage defines contraposition and divides it into two types. The matn defines 'aks al-naqid as replacing each term of a proposition with its contradictory while preserving truth and quality. The example given is converting 'Every J is B' into 'Every not-B is not-J'. Dasuqi's commentary explains the terminology: the name 'aks al-naqid' comes from first taking the contradictory of each term and then converting. He then divides contraposition into muwafiq (agreeing in quality and quantity) and mukhalif (disagreeing in quality). The example given is muwafiq because both the original and the contrapositive are affirmative and universal. 'Attar adds a clarification: 'aks al-naqid can refer either to the process (verbal noun) or to the resulting proposition. He also corrects the phrasing: the author should have said 'replacing each of the two extremes by the contradictory of the other' rather than 'replacing the contradictories of the two extremes', because what is actually replaced are the extremes, not the contradictories. This guards against a misunderstanding about what is being replaced in contraposition.

Key Terms
عكس النقيض
عكس نقيض موافق
عكس نقيض مخالف

D) No intoxicant is permissible

Contraposition is an immediate inference that replaces terms with their contradictories and swaps them, with two types: muwafiq (same quality/quantity) and mukhalif (different quality).

Conforming Contraposition: Swapping Terms with Negations
Author defines 'aks al-naqid and exemplifies the conforming type.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to perform conforming contraposition on a universal affirmative categorical proposition and verify its truth preservation.

Core Idea
Conforming contraposition swaps subject and predicate with their negations while preserving truth and quality.

This lesson introduces 'aks al-naqid al-muwafiq, a type of immediate inference used in Islamic logic. Understanding it sharpens your ability to derive equivalent propositions without changing truth value. The source passage defines and illustrates this operation with an example from the Tadhheeb tradition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original proposition's subject and predicate
Take a universal affirmative proposition like 'Every J is B'. Here 'J' is the subject and 'B' is the predicate. In the Islamic example, 'J' is 'human' and 'B' is 'animal'.
2
Negate both terms
Form the contradictories: 'not J' (non-human) and 'not B' (non-animal). These are the negations of the original terms.
The negation is a term negation, not a propositional negation.
3
Swap the negated terms
Place the negated predicate (not-B) as the new subject and the negated subject (not-J) as the new predicate. The result is 'Everything that is not B is not J'.
This is opposite of simple conversion where terms are swapped without negation.
4
Preserve quality and quantity
The new proposition must be affirmative (since the original is affirmative) and universal (since the original is universal). The example 'Every non-animal is non-human' is affirmative universal with negated terms.
This is what makes it conforming (muwafiq) – it agrees with the original in both quality and quantity.
5
Verify truth preservation
If 'Every human is animal' is true, then 'Everything that is not an animal is not a human' is also true. The entailment holds because the set of non-animals is a subset of the set of non-humans.
Truth is guaranteed by the logical relationship; no extra condition is needed for universal affirmatives.

Chapter: On the Conversion of Contradiction, the Conforming and the Non-Conforming. Conforming Conversion of Contradiction: The conversion of contradiction is the exchange of the two contradictories of the two extremes, by making the contradictory of the first part the second, and the contradictory of the second part the first, while preserving truth and quality. So our saying: 'Every J is B' converts by conversion of contradiction to: 'Everything that is not B is not J.' This is according to the early scholars. Dasuqi: His statement: 'Conversion of contradiction' – it is so named because the contradictory of each of the two terms is taken first, then that contradictory is converted. And it (the conversion) is of two types: conforming conversion of contradiction and non-conforming conversion of contradiction. The first is called 'conforming' because it conforms to the original in quality and quantity, and the second is called 'non-conforming' because it opposes the original in quality. His statement: 'by making... etc.' is a depiction of the exchange. His statement: 'and quality' means affirmation and negation. His statement: 'Every J is B' means: every human is an animal. And his statement: 'to: Everything that is not B is not J' means: everything that is not an animal is not a human. This proposition is an affirmative with negated terms, and this is the type called conforming conversion of contradiction. Chapter: On the Conversion of Contradiction. Attar: It (the term) is also applied to the verbal noun meaning and to the resulting proposition. What is intended by 'exchange of the two contradictories' is the exchange of each of the two terms for the contradictory of the other, as the commentator indicated by his statement: 'by making the contradictory... etc.' If only the author had said: 'exchange of each of the two terms for the contradictory of the other', because what is exchanged are the two terms for their contradictories, not the contradictories themselves.
Source pages p. 735

Commentary
This passage defines 'aks al-naqid (conversion by contradiction) and explains its conforming type. The author begins by stating the general definition: it is the operation of swapping the two terms of a proposition with their negations, while keeping the truth and the quality (affirmative/negative) unchanged. He illustrates with the example 'Every J is B' converting to 'Everything that is not B is not J'. The commentator Dasuqi clarifies that the name derives from first taking the contradictory of each term and then converting; he also distinguishes two types: conforming (muwafiq) and non-conforming (mukhalif), the former agreeing with the original in both quality and quantity, the latter differing in quality. Dasuqi then unpacks the definition: 'by making the contradictory... etc.' is a depiction of the exchange; 'quality' refers to affirmation or negation. He instantiates the variables: 'J' stands for 'human', 'B' for 'animal', so the original is 'Every human is an animal' and the converse is 'Everything that is not an animal is not a human', which is an affirmative proposition with negated terms (i.e., 'every non-animal is non-human'). This is the conforming type. Attar adds a terminological nuance: the phrase 'exchange of the two contradictories' is ambiguous; what is actually meant is swapping each term for the contradictory of the other, not swapping the contradictories themselves. He wishes the author had phrased it more precisely.

Non-Conforming Contraposition: Definition and Distinction
Author defines non-conforming contraposition and explains the terminological dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between conforming and non-conforming contraposition and identify which type is used in a given inference.

Core Idea
Non-conforming contraposition changes the quality; it is the later logicians' distinct definition.

This passage introduces two types of contraposition: the conforming type, which preserves quality, and the non-conforming type, which flips it. The author notes that early logicians only recognized the conforming type, while later logicians adopted the non-conforming type after finding the early proofs insufficient. You will learn to distinguish these operations and understand the reasoning behind the later definition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of immediate inference
Examine the original and resulting propositions. Does the quality (affirmative/negative) remain the same? If yes, it is conforming; if it changes, it is non-conforming.
The key difference is the preservation or change of quality.
2
Check the definition given in the passage
The passage defines 'aks al-naqid as either conforming (early scholars) or non-conforming (later scholars). The phrase 'or making' introduces the non-conforming type.
3
Apply to an Islamic example
Take the proposition 'Every intoxicant is haram' (original). Conforming contrapositive: 'Every non-haram is a non-intoxicant'. Non-conforming: 'No non-haram is an intoxicant'. Notice the change from affirmative to negative in the non-conforming case.

[Non-conforming conversion of contradiction] (Or making – with nominative – is an explanatory conjunction to his statement 'exchange': i.e., conversion of contradiction is either the exchange of the contradictories of the two terms with preservation of truth and quality, according to what the early scholars chose. Dasuqi: His statement: 'Or making' – 'or' is for division and diversification. His statement: 'according to what the early scholars chose' – that is the conforming conversion of contradiction, and his statement: 'or making... etc.' is the non-conforming conversion. Attar: His statement: 'Everything that is not (1) is not' – an addition on the side of the subject for the sake of a verbal consideration; it is that 'all' is not added to 'laysa bi-hayawan' as it is added to 'la hayawan', or because 'laysa bi-hayawan' does not occur as a subject in the course of exposition as 'la-hayawan' does. End quote from Isam. His statement: 'Or making... etc.' is a variation between the two meanings according to the two terminologies, not in the division of one meaning; i.e., conversion of contradiction in the verbal noun sense is either used in the first meaning which is the terminology of the early scholars, called conforming conversion, or used in the second meaning which is the terminology of the later scholars, called non-conforming conversion. The later scholars only deviated from the method of the early scholars because their proofs were not complete for demonstrating the conversion of affirmatives and negatives to their converses according to their terminology, due to refutations being brought against them. In summary, the early scholars had only two types: simple conversion (al-'aks al-mustawi) and conversion of contradiction, and they defined it with the mentioned definition, and demonstrated it by the method of reduction to absurdity (al-khalf). They said in demonstrating the conversion of the universal affirmative: 'If it is true, then otherwise some of what is not [?]...' and they produce a contradiction. The later scholars objected that we do not concede that if the converse were not true, then the preceding contradictory would be true; the most that can be said is that our statement 'Not some of what is not [?]' would be true. (1) (Thus was left blank in his manuscript for symbols, perhaps he left them to be written in red ink, and forgot to write them, and similarly hereafter.)
Source pages p. 736

Commentary
This passage defines the non-conforming type of contraposition and explains the terminological dispute between early and late logicians. The author begins by stating that 'aks al-naqid is either the conforming type (as defined by the early scholars) or the non-conforming type, which is introduced by the phrase 'or making' (أو جعل). Dasuqi clarifies that 'or' here indicates a division between two types. Attar then provides a detailed explanation: the non-conforming type differs from the conforming in that it changes the quality (from affirmative to negative or vice versa). The later scholars adopted this new definition because they found the early scholars' proofs for contraposition insufficient. Attar recounts that the early scholars knew only two conversions: simple conversion (al-'aks al-mustawi) and contraposition, and they proved the validity of contraposition for universal affirmatives using a proof by contradiction (tariq al-khalf). Their proof attempted to show that if the contrapositive were false, an absurdity would follow. The later scholars rejected this proof, arguing that the assumption of the falsity of the contrapositive does not necessarily lead to the contradictory they claimed; at best it leads to a different statement. The exact logical formulation is garbled in the manuscript, but the core dispute is clear: the early method was abandoned because its proof was not airtight.

طريق الخلف
المتقدمون

The Early vs Later Logicians' Dispute over Contraposition
Author recounts the khalf proof and the later critique, leading to the redefinition of contraposition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the early logicians' khalf proof for contraposition and explain why later logicians rejected it.

Core Idea
The later logicians changed the definition because the early khalf proof for contraposition was not airtight.

This passage not only defines non-conforming contraposition but also recounts the methodological dispute between early and late logicians. The early logicians proved the validity of contraposition (their conforming type) using a proof by contradiction (tariq al-khalf). The later logicians found that this proof did not withstand scrutiny, leading them to redefine contraposition as the non-conforming type. In this lesson, you will examine the structure of the khalf proof and understand why it was considered insufficient.

Guided Path
1
Identify the early logicians' claim
The early logicians claimed that for a universal affirmative proposition, the conforming contrapositive follows necessarily. They attempted to prove this via khalf: assume the contrapositive is false, then derive a contradiction.
2
Reconstruct the khalf argument
Assume 'All A is B' is true. Suppose the contrapositive 'All non-B is non-A' is false. Then its contradictory 'Some non-B is not non-A' (i.e., some non-B is A) is true. But that, together with the original, would produce a contradiction (e.g., something both A and not A). The exact steps are garbled in the manuscript, but the intent is clear.
The passage gives a corrupted version: 'some of what is not...' but the logical skeleton is recognizable.
3
Understand the later logicians' objection
Later logicians objected: 'We do not concede that if the contrapositive were false, that specific contradictory necessarily follows.' They argued that the contradictory of the contrapositive might be another proposition, so the deduction is invalid. Hence the proof fails.
4
Consequence of the dispute
Because the khalf proof was deemed insufficient, later logicians redefined contraposition as non-conforming (changing quality) to avoid reliance on that proof. This terminological shift reflects a deeper methodological disagreement.

العطار
(قَوْلُهُ : كُلُّ مَا لَيْسَ (١) لَيْسَ ( زيادة على ما في جانب الموضوع لرعاية أمرٍ لفظيّ ; هو أنَّ الكُلَّ لا يُضافُ إلى ليسَ ,بحيوانٍ، كَمَا يُضافُ إلى لا حيوان، أو لأنَّ ليس بحيوان لا يقع محكوماً عليه في مجاري البيانِ كَمَا يقعُ اللاحيوانُ ا.هـ. عصام . (قَوْلُهُ : أَوْ جَعْلُ ... إلخ) ترديد بين المعنيين بحسب الاصطلاحينِ ; لا في قسمِ معنى واحد; أي عكسُ النّقيض بالمعنى المصدري; إمَّا مُستعمل في المعنى الأَوَّلِ وهو مصطلح القدماء، ويُسمَّى عكس النَّقيض الموافقِ ، أو مستعمل في المعنى الثَّاني، وهو مُصطلح المتأخّريْنَ ، ويُسمَّى عكس النَّقيض المخالفِ، وإِنَّما عدل المتأخّرونَ عَنْ طريقةِ القدماء لِعَدَم تمام أدلَّتِهِم على بيان انعكاس الموجبات والسوالب إلى عكوسها على اصطلاحِهِمْ ; لورودِ المنعِ عليها، والحاصلُ أنَّ القدماء لم يكن عندَهُم إلا قسمان : العكس المستوي وعكسُ النَّقيض، وَعرَّفُوهُ بالتَّعريف المذكور، وبيَّنوه بطريقِ الخلفِ فقالُوا في بيانِ انعكاس الكُلِّيَّةِ : موجبةٌ كُلَّيَّةٌ إذا صدقَ وإلا , فَبعضُ مَا ليسَ كُلُّ صدقَ كُلّ مَا ليسَ ويُضم إلى الأصل هكذا بعضُ ما ليسَ ليس ليس وكل ينتج : بعضُ ما وأَنَّهُ محالٌ ، وردَّهُ المتأخّرونَ بأنَّا لا نُسلّمُ أَنَّهُ لَوْ لَمْ يصدقِ العكس لَصَدَقَ النّقيضُ المتقدِّمُ ، غايةُ مَا في البابِ أَنَّهُ يلزمُ صدقُ قولنا : ليس بعضُ مَا ليسَ 
(۱) (هكذا بيضَ في خطّهِ لِلرُّموز، ولعله تركها لكتابتها بالمدادِ الأحمرِ; فَسَهَا عَن كتابتها، وهكذا فيما بَعْدُ ا.هـ.
[Non-conforming conversion of contradiction] (Or making – with nominative – is an explanatory conjunction to his statement 'exchange': i.e., conversion of contradiction is either the exchange of the contradictories of the two terms with preservation of truth and quality, according to what the early scholars chose. Dasuqi: His statement: 'Or making' – 'or' is for division and diversification. His statement: 'according to what the early scholars chose' – that is the conforming conversion of contradiction, and his statement: 'or making... etc.' is the non-conforming conversion. Attar: His statement: 'Everything that is not (1) is not' – an addition on the side of the subject for the sake of a verbal consideration; it is that 'all' is not added to 'laysa bi-hayawan' as it is added to 'la hayawan', or because 'laysa bi-hayawan' does not occur as a subject in the course of exposition as 'la-hayawan' does. End quote from Isam. His statement: 'Or making... etc.' is a variation between the two meanings according to the two terminologies, not in the division of one meaning; i.e., conversion of contradiction in the verbal noun sense is either used in the first meaning which is the terminology of the early scholars, called conforming conversion, or used in the second meaning which is the terminology of the later scholars, called non-conforming conversion. The later scholars only deviated from the method of the early scholars because their proofs were not complete for demonstrating the conversion of affirmatives and negatives to their converses according to their terminology, due to refutations being brought against them. In summary, the early scholars had only two types: simple conversion (al-'aks al-mustawi) and conversion of contradiction, and they defined it with the mentioned definition, and demonstrated it by the method of reduction to absurdity (al-khalf). They said in demonstrating the conversion of the universal affirmative: 'If it is true, then otherwise some of what is not [?]...' and they produce a contradiction. The later scholars objected that we do not concede that if the converse were not true, then the preceding contradictory would be true; the most that can be said is that our statement 'Not some of what is not [?]' would be true. (1) (Thus was left blank in his manuscript for symbols, perhaps he left them to be written in red ink, and forgot to write them, and similarly hereafter.)
Source pages p. 736

Commentary
This passage defines the non-conforming type of contraposition and explains the terminological dispute between early and late logicians. The author begins by stating that 'aks al-naqid is either the conforming type (as defined by the early scholars) or the non-conforming type, which is introduced by the phrase 'or making' (أو جعل). Dasuqi clarifies that 'or' here indicates a division between two types. Attar then provides a detailed explanation: the non-conforming type differs from the conforming in that it changes the quality (from affirmative to negative or vice versa). The later scholars adopted this new definition because they found the early scholars' proofs for contraposition insufficient. Attar recounts that the early scholars knew only two conversions: simple conversion (al-'aks al-mustawi) and contraposition, and they proved the validity of contraposition for universal affirmatives using a proof by contradiction (tariq al-khalf). Their proof attempted to show that if the contrapositive were false, an absurdity would follow. The later scholars rejected this proof, arguing that the assumption of the falsity of the contrapositive does not necessarily lead to the contradictory they claimed; at best it leads to a different statement. The exact logical formulation is garbled in the manuscript, but the core dispute is clear: the early method was abandoned because its proof was not airtight.

طريق الخلف
المتقدمون
المتأخرون

The Two Definitions of 'Aks al-Naqid: Concordant and Contradictory
Author defines the two types of 'aks al-naqid and explains the dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the 'concordant' and 'contradictory' definitions of 'aks al-naqid and identify which group of logicians used each.

Core Idea
'Aks al-naqid has two definitions reflecting a historical shift in logical precision.

This passage introduces two understandings of 'aks al-naqid (inversion of the contradictory). The earlier logicians (متقدمون) defined it as swapping both terms for their opposites while preserving truth and quality, called the 'concordant' type. The later logicians (متأخرون) refined this to a different operation called the 'contradictory' type, where the subject is negated and becomes the subject of the inverse. Understanding this distinction is essential when reading classical logical commentaries.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two definitions
Locate in the Arabic where the author presents the two definitions: the first is 'تبديل نقيض الطرفين' (substitution of the opposite of the two terms), and the second is 'جعل' (making) with a specific arrangement. Note that the first is attributed to the earlier scholars and the second to the later scholars.
The author uses the phrase 'أَوْ جَعْلُ' (or making) to indicate the hesitation between the two meanings.
2
Understand the 'concordant' definition
In the concordant type ('الموافق'), the subject of the original proposition is negated and becomes the predicate, and the predicate is negated and becomes the subject, while quality and truth are preserved. For example, from 'Every A is B' we get 'Every non-B is non-A'.
This was the terminology of the earlier logicians.
3
Understand the 'contradictory' definition
In the contradictory type ('المخالف'), the subject is negated and becomes the subject of the inverse, and the predicate is negated and becomes the predicate. Thus from 'Every A is B' we get 'Every non-A is non-B'.
This was the terminology of the later logicians.
4
Note the historical shift
The passage explains that the later scholars deviated because the earlier proofs were incomplete and open to objections. They adopted the contradictory definition to avoid counterexamples involving universal concepts.
This shift is important for understanding why different sources may give different inversion rules.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
