هو أنَّ الكُلَّ لا يُضافُ إلى ليسَ ،بحيوانٍ، كَمَا يُضافُ إلى لا حيوان، أو لأنَّ ليس بحيوان لا يقع محكوماً عليه في مجاري البيانِ كَمَا يقعُ اللاحيوانُ. وقوله : أَوْ جَعْلُ ... إلخ) ترديد بين المعنيين بحسب الاصطلاحينِ ; لا في قسمِ معنى واحد; أي عكسُ النّقيض بالمعنى المصدري; إمَّا مُستعمل في المعنى الأَوَّلِ وهو مصطلح القدماء، ويُسمَّى عكس النَّقيض الموافقِ ، أو مستعمل في المعنى الثَّاني، وهو مُصطلح المتأخّريْنَ ، ويُسمَّى عكس النَّقيض المخالفِ، وإِنَّما عدل المتأخّرونَ عَنْ طريقةِ القدماء لِعَدَم تمام أدلَّتِهِم على بيان انعكاس الموجبات والسوالب إلى عكوسها على اصطلاحِهِمْ ; لورودِ المنعِ عليها، والحاصلُ أنَّ القدماء لم يكن عندَهُم إلا قسمان : العكس المستوي وعكسُ النَّقيض، وَعرَّفُوهُ بالتَّعريف المذكور، وبيَّنوه بطريقِ الخلفِ فقالُوا في بيانِ انعكاس الكُلِّيَّةِ : موجبةٌ كُلَّيَّةٌ إذا صدقَ وإلا ، فَبعضُ مَا ليسَ ... لكنَّه لا يلزمُ مِن صدق بعض ما ليس لأنَّ السَّالبة المعدولة أعم مِنَ الموجبة المحصَّلَةِ، وصدقُ الأعمّ لَا يستلزمُ صدق الأخص، وأيضاً تنتقضُ الحمليَّاتُ بِالموجباتِ الَّتي محمولاتُهَا مِنَ المفهوماتِ الشَّاملة، والسَّوالب بالتي موضوعاتها نقائضُ الأمورِ الشَّاملة، وليسَتْ محمولاتُها مِنَ المفهوماتِ الشَّامِلةِ كَقولِنَا : كُلُّ شيءٍ أوْ كُلُّ إنسان ممكن عام، فإنَّهُ صادق مع كذب قولِنَا : كُلُّ لَا ممكن عام لا شيء أو لا إنسان، وَكَقَولِنَا : لا شيء مِنَ اللاممكن العام بلاشيء أو بلا إنسان أو بإنسان مع كذب قولِنَا : ليس بعضُ الشَّيء أو الإنسانِ أو اللا إنسانِ مُمكناً عاماً ، ودفع الأوَّلِ بأنَّا نأخذُ النَّقيض بمعنى السَّلْبِ لا بمعنى العدول، والسَّالبةُ المحمول مُساويةُ لِلسَّالبةِ، فَقولُنَا : كُلُّ ما ليس ليس هو موجبةٌ سالبة الطَّرفين في حكمِ السَّالبة في عدم اقتضاء وجودِ الموضوع، فإذا لم تصدق؛ صدق: ليس بعضُ ماليس ليس ، وكان معناه: سلب سلب عن بعض ما صدق عليه سلب ويتمُ الدَّليلُ، فَالسَّالبة المعدولة المحمول وإنْ كانَتْ أعمَّ مِنَ الموجبة المحصلة، لكنَّ السَّالبة المحمول لَيسَتْ أعم منها ، بل هِيَ مُساويةٌ لَها ; لأنَّ السَّلْبَ عَنِ الشَّيءِ وإثباتَ السَّلْبِ لهُ لا تغاير بينَهُما في نفس الأمر، بل بالاعتبارِ، فَالموجبة في حُكْم السالبة في عدم اقتضاء وجودِ الموضوع، ودفع الثَّاني بالتَّخصيص بأنْ لا يكونَ المحمولُ فيهِ من المفهوماتِ الشَّاملة، وحينئذٍ : يكون لنقيض المحمول أفراد موجودةٌ، فَتتلازمُ السَّالبة والمحصلة والمعدولةُ ، وتعميم قواعد الفنّ إِنَّما هو بقدرِ الطَّاقة، قال عبد الحكيم: وَلأجل ذلكَ كانَ المستعمل في العلوم عكس النَّقيضِ على رأي المتقدمين، إذ لا مسألة في العلوم يكون محمولها مِنَ المفهوماتِ الشَّامِلةِ، فليس اعتبارُ المتأخّرينَ إلا لمجرَّدِ تَعميم القواعدِ مِن غير ثمرة علمية تترتب عليه
Or making by raising (رفع) – it is a conjunction to his saying 'substitution' – that is, the inversion of the contradictory is either the substitution of the opposite of the two terms while preserving truth and quality, according to what the earlier scholars chose. And his saying: 'according to what the earlier scholars chose' – and it is the concordant inversion of the contradictory. And his saying: 'or making' – it is the contradictory inversion of the contradictory. And his saying: 'everything that is not (1) is not' – an addition to what is on the side of the subject to observe a verbal matter; it is that 'all' is not added to 'is not' as an animal, as it is added to 'not an animal', or because 'is not an animal' does not occur as a predicate in the flow of discourse as 'not an animal' occurs. And his saying: 'or making... etc.' – a hesitation between the two meanings according to the two terminologies, not in a division of one meaning; that is, the inversion of the contradictory in the verbal noun sense: either used in the first meaning, which is the term of the earlier scholars, called the concordant inversion of the contradictory, or used in the second meaning, which is the term of the later scholars, called the contradictory inversion of the contradictory. And the later scholars only deviated from the method of the earlier scholars because their proofs were not complete to show the inversion of affirmatives and negatives to their inversions according to their terminology, due to the occurrence of objection against them. The summary is that the earlier scholars had only two divisions: the standard inversion and the inversion of the contradictory. They defined it with the mentioned definition and explained it by the method of contradiction, saying in the explanation of the inversion of the universal: 'An affirmative universal, if it is true, then otherwise...' But it is not necessary from the truth of 'some of what is not' because the negated privative is more general than the affirmative assertoric, and the truth of the more general does not entail the truth of the more specific. Also the categorical propositions are refuted by affirmatives whose predicates are from the universal concepts, and negatives whose subjects are the opposites of universal things, and whose predicates are not from the universal concepts, like our saying: 'Everything or every man is a universal possible', for it is true while our saying: 'Every not-universal-possible is not a thing or not a man' is false, and like our saying: 'Nothing of the not-universal-possible is without a thing or without a man or with a man' while our saying: 'Some thing or man or not-man is not universal possible' is false. And the refutation of the first (objection) is that we take the contradictory in the sense of negation (salb) not in the sense of privation ('adul), and the negative predicate is equivalent to the negative. So our saying: 'Everything that is not is not' is an affirmative with both terms negative, in the ruling of the negative in not requiring the existence of the subject. So if it is not true, then 'Not some of what is not is not' is true, and its meaning is the negation of negation about some of that which negation was true about it, and the proof is completed. So the negative with a privative predicate, although it is more general than the affirmative assertoric, yet the negative predicate is not more general than it, rather it is equivalent to it, because negativity about a thing and affirming negativity for it have no difference in themselves, only by consideration. So the affirmative is in the ruling of the negative in not requiring the existence of the subject. And the refutation of the second (objection) is by specification: that the predicate in it is not from the universal concepts. Then the opposite of the predicate has existing individuals, so the negative, the assertoric, and the privative become mutually entailing. And the generalization of the rules of the art is only to the extent of capacity. 'Abd al-Hakim said: 'For that reason, the inversion of the contradictory on the opinion of the earlier scholars is what is used in the sciences, since there is no question in the sciences whose predicate is from the universal concepts. So the consideration of the later scholars is only for the sake of generalizing the rules, with no scientific fruit resulting from it.'
Source pages p. 736

Commentary
This passage defines the two types of 'aks al-naqid (inversion of the contradictory) and explains the disagreement between the earlier (متقدمون) and later (متأخرون) logicians. The author begins by stating that 'aks al-naqid can be either a substitution of the opposite of both terms while preserving truth and quality (the view of the earlier scholars) or another definition given later. The commentary clarifies that the first definition is the 'concordant' (موافق) type and the second is the 'contradictory' (مخالف) type. The later scholars adopted the second definition because the proofs of the earlier scholars were incomplete; objections could be raised against their method of showing the inversion of affirmatives and negatives. The passage then details a specific objection: using the earlier method, one arrives at 'some of what is not' (بعض ما ليس) from a universal affirmative, but the truth of 'some of what is not' does not necessarily follow because a negated privative (السالبة المعدولة) is more general than an affirmative assertoric (الموجبة المحصلة). The earlier logicians defended themselves by taking the contradictory in the sense of negation (سلب) rather than privation (عدول), making the negative predicate equivalent to the simple negative. They also specified that the predicate should not be from the universal concepts (المفهومات الشاملة) to avoid counterexamples. 'Abd al-Hakim concludes that the earlier view is actually used in the sciences because no scientific question involves such universal predicates, so the later refinement is only for generalizing rules without practical benefit.

الموافق
المخالف
السالبة المحصلة
السالبة المعدولة
المفهومات الشاملة

Key Takeaway
The concordant and contradictory types of 'aks al-naqid represent two definitions of the same operation, differing in how the terms are rearranged after negation, and were adopted by different groups of logicians.

The Logical Dispute: Why Later Logicians Rejected the Earlier Definition of 'Aks al-Naqid
Author explains why later scholars deviated and gives the objection and refutation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the objection raised by later logicians against the earlier proof of 'aks al-naqid and the response of the earlier logicians, including the role of universal concepts.

Core Idea
The earlier proof of 'aks al-naqid was challenged due to counterexamples involving universal concepts, leading to a refined definition.

This passage delves into the logical dispute between earlier and later logicians. The later scholars argued that the earlier proof of 'aks al-naqid was incomplete, because it could produce false conclusions when applied to propositions with universal concepts (المفهومات الشاملة) like 'everything is possible'. The earlier logicians defended their method by refining the understanding of negation and by excluding such problematic cases. This lesson examines the objection and the defense.

Guided Path
1
Understand the earlier proof using contradiction
The earlier logicians proved 'aks al-naqid by contradiction: from a universal affirmative 'Every A is B', they argued that if the inverse were false, then 'Some non-B is A' would be true, which contradicts the original. But they concluded 'Some non-B is non-A', which is not always valid.
This is the 'method of contradiction' (طريق الخلف).
2
Identify the objection with universal concepts
The later scholars gave a counterexample: 'Every thing is a possible' (كل شيء ممكن). The original is true, but the inverse 'Every non-possible is a non-thing' (كل لا ممكن لا شيء) is false, because 'non-possible' has no instances. This shows the proof fails for universal concepts.
Universal concepts are those that apply to everything, like 'thing', 'possible'. Their negations have no positive instances.
3
Analyze the earlier defense: negation as salb not 'adul
The earlier logicians responded by distinguishing between negation (سلب) and privation (عدول). They took the contradictory in the sense of simple negation, making 'every non-B is non-A' equivalent to 'there is no non-B that is A'. This avoids the problem of non-existent subjects.
This defense relies on the equivalence of a negative predicate and a negative proposition.
4
Note the specification to exclude universal concepts
The earlier logicians also specified that the predicate must not be a universal concept. If the predicate is not universal, then its negation has existing individuals, and the inversion holds. Thus, the earlier proof is valid for all practical cases in the sciences.
The author quotes 'Abd al-Hakim: the sciences never use universal concepts as predicates, so the earlier definition is sufficient.

Source Text

أَوْ جَعْلُ بالرَّفع ; عطف على قوله : «تبديل»؛ أي: عكس النقيض إمَّا تبديل نقيض الطَّرفَين مع بقاء الصِّدقِ والكيف، على ما اختاره المتقدمون . وقوله : عَلَى مَا اخْتَارَهُ الْمُتَقَدِّمُونَ) وهو عكس النقيض الموافق، وقوله: (أو جعل) هو عكسُ النَّقيض المخالف. وقوله : كُلُّ مَا لَيْسَ (١) لَيْسَ ( زيادة على ما في جانب الموضوع لرعاية أمرٍ لفظيّ ; لورودِ المنعِ عليها، والحاصلُ أنَّ القدماء لم يكن عندَهُم إلا قسمان : العكس المستوي وعكسُ النَّقيض، وَعرَّفُوهُ بالتَّعريف المذكور، وبيَّنوه بطريقِ الخلفِ فقالُوا في بيانِ انعكاس الكُلِّيَّةِ : موجبةٌ كُلَّيَّةٌ إذا صدقَ وإلا ، فَبعضُ مَا ليسَ ... لكنَّه لا يلزمُ مِن صدق بعض ما ليس لأنَّ السَّالبة المعدولة أعم مِنَ الموجبة المحصَّلَةِ، وصدقُ الأعمّ لَا يستلزمُ صدق الأخص، وأيضاً تنتقضُ الحمليَّاتُ بِالموجباتِ الَّتي محمولاتُهَا مِنَ المفهوماتِ الشَّاملة، والسَّوالب بالتي موضوعاتها نقائضُ الأمورِ الشَّاملة، وليسَتْ محمولاتُها مِنَ المفهوماتِ الشَّامِلةِ كَقولِنَا : كُلُّ شيءٍ أوْ كُلُّ إنسان ممكن عام، فإنَّهُ صادق مع كذب قولِنَا : كُلُّ لَا ممكن عام لا شيء أو لا إنسان، وَكَقَولِنَا : لا شيء مِنَ اللاممكن العام بلاشيء أو بلا إنسان أو بإنسان مع كذب قولِنَا : ليس بعضُ الشَّيء أو الإنسانِ أو اللا إنسانِ مُمكناً عاماً ، ودفع الأوَّلِ بأنَّا نأخذُ النَّقيض بمعنى السَّلْبِ لا بمعنى العدول، والسَّالبةُ المحمول مُساويةُ لِلسَّالبةِ، فَقولُنَا : كُلُّ ما ليس ليس هو موجبةٌ سالبة الطَّرفين في حكمِ السَّالبة في عدم اقتضاء وجودِ الموضوع، فإذا لم تصدق؛ صدق: ليس بعضُ ماليس ليس ، وكان معناه: سلب سلب عن بعض ما صدق عليه سلب ويتمُ الدَّليلُ، فَالسَّالبة المعدولة المحمول وإنْ كانَتْ أعمَّ مِنَ الموجبة المحصلة، لكنَّ السَّالبة المحمول لَيسَتْ أعم منها ، بل هِيَ مُساويةٌ لَها ; لأنَّ السَّلْبَ عَنِ الشَّيءِ وإثباتَ السَّلْبِ لهُ لا تغاير بينَهُما في نفس الأمر، بل بالاعتبارِ، فَالموجبة في حُكْم السالبة في عدم اقتضاء وجودِ الموضوع، ودفع الثَّاني بالتَّخصيص بأنْ لا يكونَ المحمولُ فيهِ من المفهوماتِ الشَّاملة، وحينئذٍ : يكون لنقيض المحمول أفراد موجودةٌ، فَتتلازمُ السَّالبة والمحصلة والمعدولةُ ، وتعميم قواعد الفنّ إِنَّما هو بقدرِ الطَّاقة، قال عبد الحكيم: وَلأجل ذلكَ كانَ المستعمل في العلوم عكس النَّقيضِ على رأي المتقدمين، إذ لا مسألة في العلوم يكون محمولها مِنَ المفهوماتِ الشَّامِلةِ، فليس اعتبارُ المتأخّرينَ إلا لمجرَّدِ تَعميم القواعدِ مِن غير ثمرة علمية تترتب عليه
Or making by raising (رفع) – it is a conjunction to his saying 'substitution' – that is, the inversion of the contradictory is either the substitution of the opposite of the two terms while preserving truth and quality, according to what the earlier scholars chose. And his saying: 'according to what the earlier scholars chose' – and it is the concordant inversion of the contradictory. And his saying: 'or making' – it is the contradictory inversion of the contradictory. And his saying: 'everything that is not (1) is not' – an addition to what is on the side of the subject to observe a verbal matter; it is that 'all' is not added to 'is not' as an animal, as it is added to 'not an animal', or because 'is not an animal' does not occur as a predicate in the flow of discourse as 'not an animal' occurs. And his saying: 'or making... etc.' – a hesitation between the two meanings according to the two terminologies, not in a division of one meaning; that is, the inversion of the contradictory in the verbal noun sense: either used in the first meaning, which is the term of the earlier scholars, called the concordant inversion of the contradictory, or used in the second meaning, which is the term of the later scholars, called the contradictory inversion of the contradictory. And the later scholars only deviated from the method of the earlier scholars because their proofs were not complete to show the inversion of affirmatives and negatives to their inversions according to their terminology, due to the occurrence of objection against them. The summary is that the earlier scholars had only two divisions: the standard inversion and the inversion of the contradictory. They defined it with the mentioned definition and explained it by the method of contradiction, saying in the explanation of the inversion of the universal: 'An affirmative universal, if it is true, then otherwise...' But it is not necessary from the truth of 'some of what is not' because the negated privative is more general than the affirmative assertoric, and the truth of the more general does not entail the truth of the more specific. Also the categorical propositions are refuted by affirmatives whose predicates are from the universal concepts, and negatives whose subjects are the opposites of universal things, and whose predicates are not from the universal concepts, like our saying: 'Everything or every man is a universal possible', for it is true while our saying: 'Every not-universal-possible is not a thing or not a man' is false, and like our saying: 'Nothing of the not-universal-possible is without a thing or without a man or with a man' while our saying: 'Some thing or man or not-man is not universal possible' is false. And the refutation of the first (objection) is that we take the contradictory in the sense of negation (salb) not in the sense of privation ('adul), and the negative predicate is equivalent to the negative. So our saying: 'Everything that is not is not' is an affirmative with both terms negative, in the ruling of the negative in not requiring the existence of the subject. So if it is not true, then 'Not some of what is not is not' is true, and its meaning is the negation of negation about some of that which negation was true about it, and the proof is completed. So the negative with a privative predicate, although it is more general than the affirmative assertoric, yet the negative predicate is not more general than it, rather it is equivalent to it, because negativity about a thing and affirming negativity for it have no difference in themselves, only by consideration. So the affirmative is in the ruling of the negative in not requiring the existence of the subject. And the refutation of the second (objection) is by specification: that the predicate in it is not from the universal concepts. Then the opposite of the predicate has existing individuals, so the negative, the assertoric, and the privative become mutually entailing. And the generalization of the rules of the art is only to the extent of capacity. 'Abd al-Hakim said: 'For that reason, the inversion of the contradictory on the opinion of the earlier scholars is what is used in the sciences, since there is no question in the sciences whose predicate is from the universal concepts. So the consideration of the later scholars is only for the sake of generalizing the rules, with no scientific fruit resulting from it.'
Source pages p. 736

Commentary
This passage defines the two types of 'aks al-naqid (inversion of the contradictory) and explains the disagreement between the earlier (متقدمون) and later (متأخرون) logicians. The author begins by stating that 'aks al-naqid can be either a substitution of the opposite of both terms while preserving truth and quality (the view of the earlier scholars) or another definition given later. The commentary clarifies that the first definition is the 'concordant' (موافق) type and the second is the 'contradictory' (مخالف) type. The later scholars adopted the second definition because the proofs of the earlier scholars were incomplete; objections could be raised against their method of showing the inversion of affirmatives and negatives. The passage then details a specific objection: using the earlier method, one arrives at 'some of what is not' (بعض ما ليس) from a universal affirmative, but the truth of 'some of what is not' does not necessarily follow because a negated privative (السالبة المعدولة) is more general than an affirmative assertoric (الموجبة المحصلة). The earlier logicians defended themselves by taking the contradictory in the sense of negation (سلب) rather than privation (عدول), making the negative predicate equivalent to the simple negative. They also specified that the predicate should not be from the universal concepts (المفهومات الشاملة) to avoid counterexamples. 'Abd al-Hakim concludes that the earlier view is actually used in the sciences because no scientific question involves such universal predicates, so the later refinement is only for generalizing rules without practical benefit.

السالبة المعدولة

The later logicians refined the definition of 'aks al-naqid to avoid counterexamples with universal concepts, but the earlier definition remains practically valid for most scientific uses.

?

Immediate Inference

Two Types of 'Aks al-Naqid and the Objection on Universal Concepts
Author defines two types of 'aks al-naqid and addresses an objection on universal concepts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between 'aks al-naqid al-muwafiq and al-mukhalif, and explain how the early scholars' use of simple negation resolves the objection concerning universal concepts.

Core Idea
The two forms of 'aks al-naqid differ in term change and quality, and simple negation avoids problems with universal concepts.

This lesson explores the immediate inference known as conversion of the contradictory ('aks al-naqid). Two types exist: agreeing (muwafiq) and differing (mukhalif). An important objection arises when predicates are universal concepts (e.g., 'something'), but the early scholars' resolution using simple negation (salb) preserves the rule's validity. Understanding this is key to applying 'aks al-naqid in Islamic logical texts.

Guided Path
1
Define 'aks al-naqid al-muwafiq
Take the contradictory of the predicate as the new subject, keep the same quality (affirmative/negative). For example, 'Every human is mortal' converts to 'No non-mortal is a human'.
This is also called 'contraposition' in some traditions.
2
Define 'aks al-naqid al-mukhalif
Take the contradictory of the subject as the new predicate, change the quality. For example, 'Every human is mortal' converts to 'Every non-human is a non-mortal' (or with quality change).
This form is more restrictive and used by later scholars.
3
Identify the objection
When the predicate is a universal concept (e.g., 'thing', 'possible'), the conversion may fail because the negative with divergent negation (saliba mu'addala) is more general than the affirmative with simple predicate (mujiba muhassala). Example: 'Every thing is a general possible' is true, but its muwafiq conversion 'Every non-general possible is no thing' is false.
The objector al-'Attar raises this point to challenge the universal applicability of the rule.
4
See the resolution using simple negation
The early scholars (mutaqaddimun) take 'contradictory' in the sense of simple negation (salb), not divergent negation ('udul). In simple negation, the negative subject (saliba mahmula) is equivalent to the affirmative in terms of extension, so the conversion holds. The later scholars' formulation using 'udul is more general but leads to the problem, so the early view is preferred.
The resolution is attributed to al-Dasuqi and supported by 'Abd al-Hakim's observation that in actual sciences, universal concepts rarely appear as predicates.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

لكنَّه لا يلزمُ مِن صدق بعض ما ليس لأنَّ السَّالبة المعدولة أعم
مِنَ الموجبة المحصَّلَةِ، وصدقُ الأعمّ لَا يستلزمُ صدق الأخص، وأيضاً تنتقضُ الحمليَّاتُ بِالموجباتِ الَّتي محمولاتُهَا مِنَ المفهوماتِ الشَّاملة، والسَّوالب بالتي موضوعاتها نقائضُ الأمورِ الشَّاملة، وليسَتْ محمولاتُها مِنَ المفهوماتِ الشَّامِلةِ كَقولِنَا : كُلُّ شيءٍ أوْ كُلُّ إنسان ممكن عام، فإنَّهُ صادق مع كذب قولِنَا : كُلُّ لَا ممكن عام لا شيء أو لا إنسان، وَكَقَولِنَا : لا شيء مِنَ اللاممكن العام بلاشيء أو بلا إنسان أو بإنسان مع كذب قولِنَا : ليس بعضُ الشَّيء أو الإنسانِ أو اللا إنسانِ مُمكناً عاماً ، ودفع الأوَّلِ بأنَّا نأخذُ النَّقيض بمعنى السَّلْبِ لا بمعنى العدول، والسَّالبةُ المحمول مُساويةُ لِلسَّالبةِ، فَقولُنَا : كُلُّ ما ليس ليس هو موجبةٌ سالبة الطَّرفين في حكمِ السَّالبة في عدم اقتضاء وجودِ الموضوع، فإذا لم تصدق؛ صدق: ليس بعضُ ماليس ليس، وكان معناه: سلب سلب فَلَا بُدَّ أنْ يصدق على ذلكَ البعضُ، عن بعض ما صدق عـلـيـه سـلـب ويتمُ الدَّليلُ، فَالسَّالبة المعدولة المحمول وإنْ كانَتْ أعمَّ مِنَ الموجبة المحصلة، لكنَّ السَّالبة المحمول لَيسَتْ أعم منها ، بل هِيَ مُساويةٌ لَها ؛ لأنَّ السَّلْبَ عَنِ الشَّيءِ وإثباتَ السَّلْبِ لهُ لا تغاير بينَهُما في نفس الأمر، بل بالاعتبار، فَالموجبة في حُكْم السالبة في عدم اقتضاء وجودِ الموضوع، ودفع الثَّاني بالتَّخصيص بأنْ لا يكونَ المحمولُ فيهِ من المفهوماتِ الشَّاملة، وحينئذٍ : يكون لنقيض المحمول أفراد موجودةٌ، فَتتلازمُ السَّالبة والمحصلة والمعدولةُ ، وتعميم قواعد الفنّ إِنَّما هو بقدرِ الطَّاقة، قال عبد الحكيم: وَلأجل ذلكَ كانَ المستعمل في العلوم عكس النَّقيضِ على رأي المتقدمين، إذ لا مسألة في العلوم يكون محمولها مِنَ المفهوماتِ الشَّامِلةِ، فليس اعتبارُ المتأخّرينَ إلا لمجرَّدِ تَعميم القواعدِ مِن غير ثمرة علمية تترتب عليه،
A chapter on conversion of the contradictory: the agreeing and the differing. Al-Dasuqi said: The summary is that the author defined each of the agreeing and differing conversions of the contradictory, and that the early scholars affirmed the first, and the later scholars affirmed the second. Al-‘Attar said: Not so. But the truth of 'some of what is not' does not follow, because the negative with ‘udul is more general than the affirmative with simple predicate, and the truth of what is more general does not necessitate the truth of what is more specific. Also, categorical propositions are invalidated by affirmatives whose predicates are among the universal concepts, and negatives whose subjects are the contradictories of universal things, while their predicates are not among the universal concepts, like our saying: 'Every thing or every human is a general possible', for it is true while our saying: 'Every non-general possible is no thing or no human' is false; and like our saying: 'No thing from the general impossible is a thing or a human or a non-human' is false while our saying: 'Some of the thing or human or non-human is not a general possible' is false. The refutation of the first is by our taking the contradictory in the sense of negation (salb), not in the sense of divergence (‘udul), and the negative subject being equivalent to the negative, so our saying: 'Every not is not' is an affirmative with negative extremes, having the ruling of the negative in not requiring the existence of the subject, so if it is not true, then true: 'Some of what is not is not' and its meaning is negation of negation, so it must be true of that some of what the negation was true of, and the proof is completed. So the negative with divergent predicate, although it is more general than the affirmative with simple predicate, but the negative subject is not more general than it, rather it is equivalent to it, because negation from a thing and affirmation of negation for it have no difference in reality, only in consideration, so the affirmative is in the ruling of the negative in not requiring the existence of the subject. The refutation of the second is by specification: that its predicate not be among the universal concepts, and then the contradictory of the predicate has existing individuals, so the negative with simple subject, the affirmative with simple predicate, and the negative with divergent predicate become equivalent. The generalization of the rules of the art is only to the extent of capacity. ‘Abd al-Hakim said: For this reason, the used in the sciences is the conversion of the contradictory according to the early scholars, for there is no question in the sciences whose predicate is among the universal concepts, so the consideration of the later scholars is only for generalizing the rules without any scientific fruit resulting from it.
Source pages p. 737

Commentary
This passage defines two types of conversion of the contradictory ('aks al-naqid): the agreeing (muwafiq) and the differing (mukhalif). It begins by stating that early logicians (mutaqaddimun) affirmed the first type, while later scholars (muta'akhkhirun) affirmed the second. The objection raised by al-‘Attar is that the truth of 'some of what is not' does not follow from the conversion, because the negative with 'udul (saliba mu'addala) is more general than the affirmative with simple predicate (mujiba muhassala), and truth of the more general does not entail truth of the more specific. He gives examples: 'Every thing or every human is a general possible' is true, but its conversion 'Every non-general possible is no thing or no human' is false. Similarly, the negative 'No thing is a general impossible' is false, but its conversion 'Some thing or human is not a general possible' is false. The response (daf') is provided by al-Dasuqi, who explains that we take 'contradictory' in the sense of simple negation (salb), not divergent negation ('udul). The negative subject (saliba al-mahmul) is equivalent to the negative, so the affirmative with negative extremes, like 'Every not is not', has the same ruling as a negative in not requiring subject existence. Thus the proof holds. The refutation of the second objection is by specifying that the predicate in the conversion must not be among universal concepts (mafhumat shamilah), ensuring the contradictory of the predicate has existing individuals, making the three proposition types equivalent. Finally, the passage quotes 'Abd al-Hakim, noting that in the sciences, the early scholars' view is followed because predicates are never universal concepts, so the later scholars' generalization is fruitless.

الموافق
المخالف

Key Takeaway

The Differing ‘Aks al-Naqid: Definition and Example
Definition and example of the differing type of ‘aks al-naqid

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct the differing conversion of a categorical proposition according to the later scholars and verify its truth preservation using the proof by contradiction.

Core Idea
Differing conversion takes the contradictory of the predicate and swaps terms with changed quality while preserving truth.

In this lesson, we examine the second type of ‘aks al-naqid: the ‘differing’ type (al-mukhalif) as defined by later logicians. This operation is a powerful immediate inference that transforms a universal affirmative into a universal negative by taking the contradictory of the predicate. We will unpack its definition, work through an example, and prove why it must preserve truth. This tool is essential for testing implications in Islamic legal reasoning (usul al-fiqh).

Guided Path
1
Identify the original proposition
Find the original categorical proposition; e.g., 'Every A is B' (كل ج ب). Note its subject (A) and predicate (B).
In the text, the original is كل إنسان حيوان (Every human is an animal).
2
Take the contradictory of the predicate
Form the contradictory of the predicate B: 'not B' (non-B). This becomes the subject of the conversion.
The contradictory is سلب الجنس: ما ليس ب B.
3
Swap terms and change quality
Place the contradictory of the predicate (non-B) as the subject, and keep the original subject (A) as the predicate. Change the quality: if the original is affirmative, the conversion is negative; if the original is negative, the conversion is affirmative.
Here original affirmative (كل) becomes negative (لا شيء).
4
State the converted proposition
The result: 'No non-B is A' (لا شيء مما ليس ب B هو A). This is the differing conversion according to later scholars.
5
Verify truth preservation
Assume the conversion is false: then its contradictory 'Some non-B is A' is true. By simple conversion, that gives 'Some A is non-B', which contradicts the original 'All A is B'. So the conversion must be true if the original is true.
This proof is given by al-‘Attar in the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو جَعْلُ (نَقِيض) الجزء (الثَّانِي أَوَّلاً)، وعين الأوَّلِ ثانياً (مع مخالفة الكَيْفِ)، وبقاء الصدق على رأي المتأخرين، فقولُنَا : كلُّ جب ينعكس عندَهُم إلى: لا شيء مما ليس ب ج .
وقد عرفت معنى بقاء الصدقِ والكيف في العكس المستوي، فَلا نعيده .
الدسوقي
(قَوْلُهُ : وَعَيْن) عطفٌ على نقيض ; أي: وجعل عين ... إلخ .
(قَوْلُهُ: كُلُّ ج (ب) أي: كُلُّ إنسانٍ حيوانٌ
(قَوْلُهُ : لَا شَيْءٍ مِمَّا لَيْسَ ب (ج) أي : لا شيء مِمَّا ليس بحيوان إنسان، فقد حكم بسلب الإنسانية عمَّا ليسَ بحيوان فالأصلُ مُوجبةٌ والعكس سالبة. (قَوْلُهُ : مَعْنَى بَقَاءِ الصِّدْقِ وَالْكَيْفِ أي: الَّذي ذكرَهُ في عكس النَّقيضِ الموافق، وقوله : (في العكس ... إلخ) متعلّق بـ (عرفت) .
العطار
وههنا بحث، وهو أنَّ عدم تمام أدلَّةِ الأحكام بَلْ بطلانها؛ لا يقتضي تغيير الاصطلاحِ، والتَّعريفُ تَنبني عليهِ تلكَ الأحكامُ لِجَوازِ تَغييرِ الأحكامِ أَوْ تَخصيصها بغير مواد المنع والنَّقْضِ، معَ أَنَّهُ يمكن إتمام أدلَّةِ القدماء في الحمليَّاتِ بِتَخصيص أحكامهم بغير المفهوماتِ الشَّاملة ونقائضها، أو بأخذ النَّقيض سلبيا لا عدوليا، ثمَّ إِنَّ التَّسمية بعكس النَّقيض ظاهرة على تعريف القدماء؛ لأنَّا أخذنا نقيضَ الطَّرفَيْنِ وعكسنَاهُما على النمط المذكور، وأمَّا على تعريف المتأخرينَ؛ فَبِالنَّظَرِ إلى الجزء الثاني مِنَ الأصل؛ لأنَّا أخذنا نقيضه وعكسناه . (قَوْلُهُ : كُلُّ جب انْعَكَسَ عِنْدَهُمْ إِلَى لَا شَيْءٍ مِمَّا لَيْسَ ب ج) مثلاً إذا قُلْنَا : كُلّ إنسانٍ ناطق؛ ينعكس على رأيهم إلى قولنا : لا شيء مِمَّا ليسَ ناطقاً بإنسان، فإِنَّهُ لو لم يصدق; لَصَدَقَ نَقيضُهُ وَهُوَ قَولُنَا : بعضُ مَا ليس ناطقاً إنسان، وينعكس بالعكس المستوي إلى قولنا : بعضُ الإنسان ليس ناطقاً، هف، لكونه صدق الملزوم بدونِ اللازم.
Or making (the contradictory) of the second part first, and the same of the first second (with disagreement of quality), while preserving truth according to the later scholars. So our saying: 'Every A is B' converts according to them to: 'No thing that is not B is A'. And you have known the meaning of preserving truth and quality in the simple conversion, so we do not repeat it. Al-Dasuqi: (His saying: 'and the same') is conjoined to 'contradictory' i.e., making the same... etc. (His saying: 'Every A (B)') i.e., every human is an animal. (His saying: 'No thing from what is not B (A)') i.e., no thing from what is not an animal is human, for it has ruled the negation of humanity from what is not an animal; so the original is affirmative and the conversion is negative. (His saying: 'the meaning of preserving truth and quality') i.e., which he mentioned in the agreeing conversion of the contradictory, and his saying: 'in the conversion...' is attached to 'you have known'. Al-‘Attar: And here there is a discussion, which is that the incompleteness of the proofs of the rulings, nay their invalidity, does not necessitate a change of terminology, and the definition is built upon those rulings because it is possible to change the rulings or specify them to other than the materials of prevention and violation, even though it is possible to complete the proofs of the early scholars in categoricals by specifying their rulings to other than the universal concepts and their contradictories, or by taking the contradictory as negative (salb) not as divergent (‘udul). Then the naming 'conversion of the contradictory' is apparent according to the definition of the early scholars, because we took the contradictory of both extremes and converted them in the mentioned manner. As for according to the definition of the later scholars, it is with respect to the second part of the original, because we took its contradictory and converted it. (His saying: 'Every A is B converts according to them to: No thing from what is not B is A') for example, if we say: Every human is a speaker; it converts according to their view to our saying: No thing from what is not a speaker is a human. For if it were not true, then its contradictory would be true, namely our saying: Some of what is not a speaker is a human, and it converts by simple conversion to our saying: Some human is not a speaker, which is false, because the truth of the ground (the conversion) without the necessary (the truth of the original) would imply contradiction.
Source pages p. 738

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion of 'aks al-naqid by defining the differing type (mukhalif) according to the later scholars. It begins with a formula: take the contradictory of the second part (the predicate) as the first part (the subject) and keep the same of the first part as the second, but change the quality (from affirmative to negative or vice versa), while preserving truth. The example is given: 'Every A is B' (e.g., every human is an animal) converts to 'No non-B is A' (no non-animal is human). Al-Dasuqi then glosses the components, explaining that in the example, the original is affirmative and the conversion is negative. Al-‘Attar then raises a discussion (baḥth): even if the proofs of early scholars are incomplete or invalid, that does not necessitate changing the terminology or definitions, because the definitions are based on those rulings. One can preserve the early scholars' methods by specifying their rulings to cases not involving universal concepts, or by taking the contradictory as simple negation. The naming 'conversion of the contradictory' fits the early definition better, as it involves both extremes. The passage then provides a proof for the validity of the conversion using contrapositive: if the conversion 'No non-B is A' were false, its contradictory 'Some non-B is A' would be true, which by simple conversion gives 'Some A is non-B', contradicting the original 'All A is B'. Thus the conversion holds.

Key Terms
عكس النقيض المخالف
الجزء الثاني
بقاء الصدق
الملزوم واللازم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the later scholars, what is the correct conversion of 'Every intoxicant (khamr) is forbidden (haram)' using 'aks al-naqid al-mukhalif?
A) No non-forbidden thing is an intoxicant.
B) No non-intoxicant is a forbidden thing.
C) Every non-forbidden thing is a non-intoxicant.
D) Some non-forbidden thing is not an intoxicant.

Key Takeaway
The differing conversion of the contradictory transforms 'Every A is B' into 'No non-B is A' by taking the contradictory of the predicate, swapping terms, and changing quality, while preserving truth.

Immediate Inference
advanced
15 min
Al-‘Attar’s Discussion: Do Incomplete Proofs Justify Changing Terminology?
Al-‘Attar's discussion on whether incomplete proofs necessitate changing terminology

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct al-‘Attar’s argument that incomplete proofs do not necessitate changing terminology and evaluate its validity.

Core Idea
Incomplete proofs do not oblige us to change well-established definitions; definitions can be refined, not discarded.

After defining the differing conversion, al-‘Attar raises a critical discussion (baḥth): some might argue that because the early scholars' proofs for this conversion are incomplete or invalid, we should change the definition or terminology. Al-‘Attar refutes this, arguing that incomplete proofs do not necessarily invalidate the definitions, as one can complete the proofs by specifying the rulings. He also notes that the naming 'conversion of the contradictory' fits the early definition better. This lesson unpacks his reasoning and its significance for logical methodology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection being addressed
The objection is that the incompleteness (or even invalidity) of the proofs of early scholars regarding the differing conversion might necessitate changing the terminology or definition.
Al-‘Attar mentions 'عدم تمام أدلَّةِ الأحكام بَلْ بطلانها'.
2
Understand al-‘Attar's response
Al-‘Attar argues that this does not follow. Definitions are built upon the rulings, but it is possible to change the rulings or specify them to cases other than those involving prevention (man‘) and violation (naqḍ).
He says: 'لا يقتضي تغيير الاصطلاحِ'.
3
Analyze his alternative solution
He proposes that the early scholars' proofs can be completed by specifying their rulings to exclude universal concepts and their contradictories, or by taking the contradictory as simple negation (salb) rather than divergent negation (‘udul).
This preserves the core of the definition.
4
Compare the two definitions
Al-‘Attar then contrasts the early and later definitions: the early definition takes the contradictory of both extremes and swaps them, while the later definition only takes the contradictory of the predicate and swaps it with the subject. He notes that the naming 'conversion of the contradictory' is more apparent for the early definition, but both are valid.
This shows the evolution of terminology.
5
Evaluate the argument's strength
Assess whether al-‘Attar successfully defends the later definition. Does the possibility of completing proofs justify retaining the definition? Consider the role of convention in logical terminology.
Al-‘Attar’s argument is epistemological: definitions are not solely dependent on proofs; they can be refined independently.

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion of 'aks al-naqid by defining the differing type (mukhalif) according to the later scholars. It begins with a formula: take the contradictory of the second part (the predicate) as the first part (the subject) and keep the same of the first part as the second, but change the quality (from affirmative to negative or vice versa), while preserving truth. Al-‘Attar then raises a discussion (baḥth): even if the proofs of early scholars are incomplete or invalid, that does not necessitate changing the terminology or definitions, because the definitions are based on those rulings. One can preserve the early scholars' methods by specifying their rulings to cases not involving universal concepts, or by taking the contradictory as simple negation. The naming 'conversion of the contradictory' fits the early definition better, as it involves both extremes. The passage then provides a proof for the validity of the conversion using contrapositive: if the conversion 'No non-B is A' were false, its contradictory 'Some non-B is A' would be true, which by simple conversion gives 'Some A is non-B', contradicting the original 'All A is B'. Immediate Inference

Contrapositive According to Later Logicians
Author defines 'aks al-naqid and contrasts definitions of ancients and later logicians.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct the contrapositive of a categorical proposition according to the later logicians' definition and explain the difference from the ancients' definition.

Core Idea
Later logicians refined 'aks al-naqid to contrapose only the second term, preserving truth through quality change.

The definition of 'aks al-naqid (contrapositive) evolved within the Islamic logical tradition. This passage presents the refined definition of the later logicians (al-muta'akhkhirun), who limited the operation to contraposing only the second term, unlike the ancients (al-qudama) who contraposed both terms. The author also addresses an important methodological principle: definitions should not be altered merely because proofs derived from them are incomplete. Understanding this refinement is essential for correctly applying immediate inferences in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two definitions
Read the passage and distinguish between the ancient definition (contraposing both terms) and the later logicians' definition (contraposing only the second term).
The ancients took naqid of both terms; the later logicians take naqid of only the second term and keep the first term as is.
2
Extract the formal operation
According to the later logicians, the contrapositive of 'Every J is B' is 'Nothing that is not B is J'. Note the changes: subject becomes naqid of the original predicate, predicate becomes the original subject, and quality changes from affirmative to negative.
The operation preserves truth if the original is true.
3
Apply to an Islamic example
Take the proposition 'Every intoxicant is forbidden'. Following the later logicians, its contrapositive is 'Nothing that is not forbidden is an intoxicant'. Check that this is logically equivalent.
This conversion is crucial in usul al-fiqh for extending rulings.
4
Understand the research point
The passage argues that the definition itself should not be changed even if proofs based on it are defective. Instead, the rules can be modified or restricted. This shows that definitions are foundational.
The author suggests completing the ancients' proofs by restricting to non-comprehensive concepts or using privative negation.
5
Test via reductio
The passage proves the contrapositive by assuming its denial: if 'Nothing that is not B is J' is false, then its contradictory 'Some non-B is J' is true. Convert that by simple conversion to 'Some J is not B', which is false if the original 'Every J is B' is true. Hence the contrapositive must be true.
This demonstrates the truth-preserving nature of the inference.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو جَعْلُ (نَقِيض) الجزء (الثَّانِي أَوَّلاً)، وعين الأوَّلِ ثانياً (مع مخالفة الكَيْفِ)، وبقاء الصدق على رأي المتأخرين، فقولُنَا : كلُّ جب» ينعكس عندَهُم إلى: الا شيء مما ليس ب ج . وههنا بحث، وهو أنَّ عدم تمام أدلَّةِ الأحكام بَلْ بطلانها ؛ لا يقتضي تغيير الاصطلاحِ، والتَّعريفُ تَنبني عليهِ تلكَ الأحكامُ لِجَوازِ تَغييرِ الأحكامِ أَوْ تَخصيصها بغير مواد المنع والنَّقْضِ ، معَ أَنَّهُ يمكن إتمام أدلَّةِ القدماء في الحمليَّاتِ بِتَخصيص أحكامهم بغير المفهوماتِ الشَّاملة ونقائضها ، أو بأخذ النَّقيض سلبيا لا عدوليا، ثمَّ إِنَّ التَّسمية بعكس النَّقيض ظاهرة على تعريف القدماء؛ لأنَّا أخذنا نقيضَ الطَّرفَيْنِ وعكسنَاهُما على النمط المذكور، وأمَّا على تعريف المتأخرينَ؛ فَبِالنَّظَرِ إلى الجزء الثاني مِنَ الأصل؛ لأنَّا أخذنا نقيضه وعكسناه . مثلاً إذا قُلْنَا : كُلّ إنسانٍ ناطق ؛ ينعكس على رأيهم إلى قولنا : لا شيء مِمَّا ليسَ ناطقاً بإنسان، فإِنَّهُ لو لم يصدق؛ لَصَدَقَ نَقيضُهُ وَهُوَ قَولُنَا : بعضُ مَا ليس ناطقاً إنسان، وينعكش بالعكس المستوي إلى قولنا : بعضُ الإنسان ليس ناطقاً، هف، لكونه صدق الملزوم بدون اللازم.
Or making the contrapositive of the second term first, and the first term itself second (with change of quality), and preservation of truth according to the later logicians. So our statement 'Every J is B' converts according to them to 'Nothing that is not B is J'. And here is a research point: that the incompleteness of proofs of the rules, rather their falsity, does not necessitate changing the terminology, and the definition is built upon those rules because it is possible to change the rules or restrict them to situations without prevention and refutation, and that the proofs of the ancients concerning categoricals can be completed by restricting their rules to non-comprehensive concepts and their contradictories, or by taking the contrapositive as privative not adelphic. Then the name 'contrapositive' is apparent according to the definition of the ancients because we took the contrapositive of both terms and converted them in the mentioned manner, but according to the definition of the later logicians it is with respect to the second part of the original because we took its contrapositive and converted it. For example, if we say 'Every human is rational', it converts according to their view to 'Nothing that is not rational is human'. For if it were not true, its contradictory would be true, which is our statement 'Some of what is not rational is human', and it would convert by simple conversion to our statement 'Some human is not rational', which is a false consequent, because the truth of the antecedent without the necessary consequent is false.
Source pages p. 738

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'aks al-naqid (contrapositive) according to the later logicians (al-muta'akhkhirun) and contrasts it with the definition of the ancients (al-qudama). The definition states that contrapositive consists of making the contrapositive (naqid) of the second term the subject, and the first term itself the predicate, while changing the quality (from affirmative to negative or vice versa), and preserving truth. The author illustrates with the proposition 'Every J is B', which converts to 'Nothing that is not B is J'. The passage then raises a research point (bahth): the fact that the proofs of the rules are incomplete or even false does not necessitate changing the terminology, because the definition is prior to the rules; the rules can be modified or restricted. Moreover, the proofs of the ancients for categoricals can be fixed by restricting their rules to non-comprehensive concepts and their contradictories, or by taking the contrapositive as privative (salbii) rather than adelphic ('aduli). The name 'contrapositive' is more appropriate on the ancient definition (which takes the contrapositive of both terms), while on the later definition it is only with respect to the second part. The example given is 'Every human is rational' converting to 'Nothing that is not rational is human'. If this were false, its contradictory ('Some non-rational is human') would be true, which converts to 'Some human is not rational', a false consequent, proving the original conversion true by reductio.

Key Terms
عكس النقيض
المتأخرون
القدماء

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Some human is not rational
C) Every non-human is non-rational
D) Something not rational is human

Key Takeaway
Later logicians define 'aks al-naqid as converting a proposition by taking the contradictory of the second term as subject, the first term as predicate, and changing the quality, preserving truth.

Contrapositive by Agreement for Universal Affirmatives
Author states the rule for positive propositions in contrapositive by agreement and gives examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct the contrapositive by agreement of any universal affirmative proposition and verify its validity using the reductio method.

Core Idea
Contrapositive by agreement preserves quality and universality for affirmative universals.

This passage defines the rule for contrapositive by agreement (muwafiq) for affirmative propositions. Unlike simple conversion, a universal affirmative remains universal after contraposition. Al-'Attar's commentary demonstrates this with a proof by contradiction. Mastering this rule sharpens your skill in immediate inference, a tool used to validate arguments in Islamic jurisprudence and theology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposition type
Determine if the proposition is universal affirmative (e.g., 'Every intoxicant is haram') or particular affirmative. The rule applies differently.
Only universal affirmatives have a valid contrapositive by agreement.
2
Apply the contrapositive rule
Contrapose the subject and predicate: the subject becomes 'what is not the predicate' and predicate becomes 'what is not the subject'. Keep the quality affirmative and the quantity universal.
For 'Every intoxicant is haram', the contrapositive is 'Everything that is not haram is not an intoxicant'.
3
Validate by reductio
Assume the contrapositive is false. Then 'something that is not haram is an intoxicant' is true. By simple conversion, 'some intoxicant is not haram', contradicting the original universal affirmative.
This proof shows the contrapositive must be true if the original is true.
4
Check particular affirmatives
Test if a particular affirmative, like 'Some animal is human', has a valid contrapositive by agreement. The result would be 'Some non-human is non-animal', but this is not guaranteed true.
The text gives a counterexample: 'Some animal is not human' is true, but its contrapositive 'Some human is not animal' is false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: فِي عَكْسِ النَّقِيْضِ الْمُوَافِقِ وَالْمُخَالِفِ. وَحُكْمُ الْمُوجِبَاتِ هَهُنَا; أي: في عكس النَّقيض، (حُكْمُ السَّوَالِبِ العَكْسِ الْمُسْتَوِي; أي: وبالعكس، حتَّى إِنَّ الموجبة الكليَّةَ هَهُنا تنعكش موجبةً كلّيَّةً، والجزئية لا تنعكش. ثم شرح: قال العطار: حَتَّى إِنَّ الْمُوْجِبَةَ الْكُلِّيَّةَ فإذا صدقَ قَولُنا مثلاً: كُلُّ إنسانٍ حيوان; انعكس إلى قولنا : كُلُّ ما ليس بحيوان ليس بإنسان، وإلا ; فَبعضُ ما ليس بحيوان إنسان، وينعكس بالعكس المستوي إلى قولنا : بعضُ الإنسان ليس بحيوان، وقد كانَ الأصلُ : كُلُّ إنسانٍ حيوان . هف. وَالْجُزْنِيَّةُ لَا تَنْعَكِسُ لِصِدْقٍ قولِنَا : بعضُ الحيوانِ لا إنسان، وكذب : بعضُ الإنسان لا حيوان.
Chapter: On contrapositive by agreement and opposition. And the rule of positives here – i.e., in contrapositive – is the rule of negatives in simple conversion; i.e., conversely, such that the universal positive here converts as universal positive, and the particular does not convert. Then explanation: al-'Attar said: Such that the universal positive, if our statement 'Every human is an animal' is true, it converts to our statement 'Everything that is not an animal is not a human'; otherwise, some of what is not an animal is a human, and it converts by simple conversion to our statement 'Some human is not an animal', whereas the original was 'Every human is an animal', which is false. And the particular does not convert because our statement 'Some animal is not human' is true and our statement 'Some human is not animal' is false.
Source pages p. 739

Commentary
This passage is a new section (fasl) on contrapositive by agreement (muwafiq) and opposition (mukhalif). It states the rule for affirmative propositions in contrapositive: they follow the same pattern as negative propositions in simple conversion ('aks mustawi) – that is, a universal affirmative becomes a universal affirmative (unlike simple conversion where it becomes particular), and a particular affirmative does not convert. The commentary by al-'Attar elaborates with an example: the universal affirmative 'Every human is an animal' converts to 'Everything that is not an animal is not a human'. He proves this by reductio: if it were false, then 'Something that is not an animal is a human' would be true, which by simple conversion yields 'Some human is not an animal', contradicting the original. For the particular affirmative, it does not convert because the statement 'Some animal is not human' is true while 'Some human is not animal' is false.

Key Takeaway
In contrapositive by agreement, a universal affirmative proposition converts to a universal affirmative, not to a particular, and this is verified by reductio.

Contraposition with Agreement and Opposition
Defines contraposition and its two types: al-muwafiq and al-mukhalif.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the two types of contraposition (muwafiq and mukhalif) and correctly apply their conversion rules to categorical propositions in Islamic logical arguments.

Core Idea
Contraposition transforms a proposition by negating and swapping terms, with or without changing quality.

In this lesson, we explore the two types of contrapositive (عكس النقيض) as explained by the commentators: al-muwafiq (with agreement) and al-mukhalif (with opposition). These are immediate inferences that allow you to derive a new proposition from a given categorical proposition by negating both subject and predicate and swapping them, while optionally changing the quality. Understanding these rules is crucial for validating arguments in Islamic theology and jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original proposition
Determine the quantity (universal/particular) and quality (affirmative/negative) of the given categorical proposition. For example, 'Every human is an animal' is universal affirmative (A).
2
Choose the type of contraposition
Decide whether you are performing al-muwafiq (quality unchanged) or al-mukhalif (quality reversed). The passage defines mukhalif as flipping the quality: affirmative becomes negative and vice versa.
Muwafiq keeps the same quality; mukhalif reverses it.
3
Apply the rule for quantified propositions
For muwafiq, the universal affirmative (A) converts to universal affirmative (A); universal negative (E) converts to particular negative (O); particular affirmative (I) does not convert; particular negative (O) does not convert. For mukhalif, first reverse the quality, then apply the same rule.
Al-Dassuqi and al-‘Attar provide the analogy with simple conversion rules.
4
Verify with an example
Test the rule using the passage's example: 'Every human is an animal' (A) contrapose to 'Everything that is not an animal is not a human' (A) in muwafiq. Check that the result is equivalent.
If the original were false, the contrapositive might not hold; the passage assumes truth.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: فِي عَكْسِ النَّقِيْضِ الْمُوَافِقِ وَالْمُخَالِفِ. وأمَّا معنى مخالفة الكيف، فهو أنَّ الأصلَ إِنْ كَانَ مُوجباً؛ كَانَ العكس سالباً، وإن كان سالباً فَموجباً، وعليك بتصفح المثال؛ لِتَطَّلِعَ على حقيقة المقال. عكس النقيض الموافق للقضايا المحصورة: وحكم الموجبات ههنا؛ أي: في عكس النقيض، حكم السوالب في العكس المستوي؛ أي: وبالعكس، حتى إن الموجبة الكلية ههنا تنعكس موجبة كلية، والجزئية لا تنعكس. قال الدسوقي: حاصله أنه تقدم أن السالبة الكلية تنعكس كنفسها، وتقدم أن السالبة الجزئية لا تنعكس، فكذلك الموجبة الجزئية هنا لا تنعكس. وتقدم أن الموجبة كلية كانت أو جزئية تنعكس جزئية، فكذلك السالبة هنا كلية كانت أو جزئية تنعكس جزئية. وقال العطار: حتى إن الموجبة الكلية فإذا صدق قولنا مثلاً: كل إنسان حيوان؛ انعكس إلى قولنا: كل ما ليس بحيوان ليس بإنسان، وإلا فبعض ما ليس بحيوان إنسان، وينعكس بالعكس المستوي إلى قولنا: بعض الإنسان ليس بحيوان، وقد كان الأصل: كل إنسان حيوان. والجزئية لا تنعكس؛ يعني أن الموجبة الجزئية لا تنعكس لصدق قولنا: بعض الحيوان لا إنسان، وكذب: بعض الإنسان لا حيوان. مطلقاً، والسالبة - كلية كانت أو جزئية - تنعكس جزئية. واعلم أن هذا الحكم والذي سيجيء بعده إنما هو في عكس النقيض على رأي المتقدمين.
Chapter: On Contrapositive with Agreement and Opposition. As for the meaning of opposition of quality, it is that if the original is affirmative, the converse is negative; and if the original is negative, the converse is affirmative. And you should examine the example to understand the reality of the statement. Contrapositive with agreement for quantified propositions: The rule of affirmatives here—i.e., in contraposition—is the rule of negatives in simple conversion; i.e., and vice versa, such that the universal affirmative here converts to a universal affirmative, and the particular does not convert. Al-Dassuqi said: Its summary is that it was previously stated that the universal negative converts as itself, and that the particular negative does not convert; similarly, the particular affirmative here does not convert. And it was previously stated that the affirmative, whether universal or particular, converts to a particular; similarly, the negative here, whether universal or particular, converts to a particular. Al-‘Attar said: Such that the universal affirmative—for example, if our statement 'Every human is an animal' is true—it converts to our statement 'Everything that is not an animal is not a human'; otherwise, some of what is not an animal is a human, and it converts by simple conversion to our statement 'Some human is not an animal', whereas the original was 'Every human is an animal'. And the particular does not convert; i.e., the particular affirmative does not convert because of the truth of our statement 'Some animal is not a human' and the falsehood of 'Some human is not an animal'. Absolutely, and the negative—whether universal or particular—converts to a particular. Know that this rule and what comes after it is only in contraposition according to the view of the early scholars.
Source pages p. 739

Commentary
This passage defines two types of contraposition (عكس النقيض) and states their rules. The first type, 'aks al-naqid al-mukhalif (contrapositive with opposition), involves changing the quality: if the original is affirmative, the converse is negative, and vice versa. The second type, 'aks al-naqid al-muwafiq (contrapositive with agreement), applies to quantified propositions and follows rules analogous to simple conversion but with negation. The commentator al-Dassuqi explains that the universal negative in simple conversion remains universal negative; similarly, in contrapositive with agreement, the universal affirmative remains universal affirmative. The particular affirmative in simple conversion does not convert; likewise, in contrapositive, the particular affirmative does not convert. However, the universal affirmative in simple conversion converts to a particular affirmative; analogously, in contrapositive, the universal negative converts to a particular negative. Al-‘Attar provides an example: 'Every human is an animal' contraposes to 'Everything that is not an animal is not a human' (universal affirmative). He warns that if the original were false, the contrapositive might not hold. For the particular affirmative, he shows that 'Some animal is not a human' is true but its contrapositive 'Some human is not an animal' is false, so it does not convert. Finally, the passage notes that these rules are according to the early scholars (al-mutaqaddimin).

Key Terms
عكس النقيض
مخالفة الكيف
الموافق
المخالف

Key Takeaway
Contraposition with agreement preserves the quality for universal affirmative and universal negative, while contraposition with opposition reverses the quality; the rules for quantified propositions mirror those of simple conversion but with negation.

Rule for Negative Propositions in Conversion by Contraposition
Rule for converting negative propositions by contraposition with commentary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule for conversion by contraposition to negative propositions and identify the resulting proposition type.

Core Idea
Every negative proposition, universal or particular, converts by contraposition to a particular negative.

This lesson examines the key rule for conversion by contraposition (عكس النقيض) as it applies to negative propositions. The matn states a simple rule, and the commentary clarifies its scope with examples. Understanding this rule is essential for manipulating propositions in syllogistic contexts, especially in theological proofs.

Guided Path
1
Identify quantity and quality
Examine the proposition: is it universal negative (e.g., 'No S is P') or particular negative (e.g., 'Some S is not P')? The rule applies to both.
Affirmative propositions follow different rules.
2
Apply conversion by contraposition
Swap the subject and predicate while negating both: the original 'No S is P' becomes 'Some non-P is not S'. The result is always a particular negative proposition.
For universal negatives, the contrapositive is particular negative, not universal.
3
Verify with source example
The passage gives an example: 'No human is a stone' converts to 'Some non-stone is not a human'. Check that the conversion matches the rule.
The example illustrates the rule concretely.
4
Note the exception
The commentary mentions that two particular negative propositions (the two juz'iyyat) have a different conversion, which was covered earlier. Most negatives follow this rule.
Be aware of this nuance, though it is beyond the immediate scope.

Why Later Logicians' 'Aks al-Naqid Was Omitted
Commentator explains two reasons for excluding later logicians' 'aks al-naqid

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the two reasons the commentator gives for omitting the later logicians' 'aks al-naqid and identify the proof shared with simple inversion.

Core Idea
Later logicians' 'aks al-naqid was omitted because it is impractical and its rules differ from simple inversion.

This passage from the commentary explains why the author of the matn did not include the later logicians' version of inversion by contradictory ('aks al-naqid). The commentator gives two reasons: it is not used in the sciences, and its rules are not the same as simple inversion, so including it would require lengthy explanation. He then clarifies that the method of proof for inversion by contradictory is identical to that for simple inversion, which allows the author to rely on the earlier discussion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two reasons for omission
Find the two reasons given for why the author omitted 'aks al-naqid of the later logicians.
2
Understand each reason
First reason: it is not used in the sciences (citing al-Sayyid al-'Allama). Second reason: its rulings are not the same as simple inversion, so it would require separate lengthy explanation.
3
Note the author's concern for brevity
The commentator says the author omitted it for brevity (ikhtisar) and to avoid prolixity.
4
Recognize the shared proof
The proof (bayān) for inversion by contradictory is the same as for simple inversion; thus the earlier explanation suffices.

فَصْلٌ: فِي عَكْسِ النَّقِيضِ الْمُوَافِقِ وَالْمُخَالِفِ. لَا الْمُتَأَخِّرِينَ، وَإِنَّمَا لَمْ يَذْكُرْ عَكْسَ النَّقِيضِ الْمُعْتَبَرَ عِنْدَ الْمُتَأَخِّرِينَ، إِمَّا لِأَنَّ عَكْسَ النَّقِيضِ بِالْمَعْنَى الَّذِي ذَكَرَهُ الْمُتَأَخِّرُونَ غَيْرُ مُسْتَعْمَلٍ فِي الْعُلُومِ، عَلَى مَا صَرَّحَ بِهِ السَّيِّدُ الْعَلَّامَةُ فِي حَوَاشِيهِ. وَإِمَّا لِأَنَّ حُكْمَ الْقَضَايَا فِي عَكْسِ النَّقِيضِ الْمُعْتَبَرِ عِنْدَ الْمُتَأَخِّرِينَ لَيْسَ تَحَكَّمَهَا فِي الْمُسْتَوِي، فَلَوْ شَرَعَ فِيهِ لَاحْتَاجَ إِلَى تَطْوِيلِ الْكَلَامِ؛ إِذْ لَا يُمْكِنُهُ الْإِحَالَةُ عَلَى الْعَكْسِ الْمُسْتَوِي؛ فَلِهَذَا تَرَكَهُ اهْتِمَاماً بِشَأْنِ الِاخْتِصَارِ، وَاحْتِرَازاً عَنِ التَّطْوِيلِ وَالْإِكْثَارِ. (وَالبَيَانُ) فِي انْعِكَاسِ الْقَضَايَا بِعَكْسِ النَّقِيضِ، هُوَ (البَيَانُ) الْمَذْكُورُ فِي انْعِكَاسِهَا بِالْعَكْسِ الْمُسْتَوِي مِنْ غَيْرِ فَرْقٍ.
Section: On the inversion of the contradictory, both the compatible and the incompatible. Not the later [logicians], and he only did not mention the 'aks al-naqid considered by the later logicians, either because the 'aks al-naqid in the sense that the later logicians mentioned is not used in the sciences, as al-Sayyid al-'Allama declared in his glosses, or because the ruling of propositions in the 'aks al-naqid considered by the later logicians is not the same as their ruling in the simple inversion; so if he had embarked on it, he would have needed to lengthen the discourse, since he could not refer to the simple inversion; therefore he left it out, aiming for brevity and avoiding prolixity. And the demonstration for the inversion of propositions by 'aks al-naqid is the same demonstration mentioned for their inversion by simple inversion, without difference.
Source pages p. 741

Commentary
This passage is part of the commentary (sharh) explaining why the author of the matn did not include the later logicians' concept of 'aks al-naqid (inversion by contradictory). The commentator offers two possible reasons: first, that this concept is not actually used in the sciences, citing al-Sayyid al-'Allama's glosses; second, that the rules for 'aks al-naqid differ from those for simple inversion ('aks mustawi), so including it would require lengthy explanation that could not rely on simple inversion. The commentator then states that the proof (bayān) for inversion by contradictory is identical to the proof for simple inversion. This shows the author's concern for brevity and the practical utility of logical concepts.

Key Terms
عَكْسُ النَّقِيضِ ('aks al-naqīḍ): Inversion by contradictory: converting a proposition by negating both predicate and subject and swapping them, considered by earlier logicians; the later logicians' version is more complex and not used in sciences. [Note: Later logicians (mutā'akhkhirūn) refined 'aks al-naqīḍ with additional conditions, making it distinct from the earlier simpler version.]
الْعَكْسُ الْمُسْتَوِي (al-'aks al-mustawī): Simple inversion: swapping subject and predicate without negation, preserving truth for universal negatives and particular affirmatives, etc. [Note: Standard across tradition.]
الْبَيَان (al-bayān): The proof or demonstration that establishes the validity of an inference; here it refers to the reductio ad absurdum (dalīl al-khalf) used to prove inversion. [Note: In logical texts, bayān often means a clear argument or proof.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, why did the author of the matn omit the later logicians' concept of 'aks al-naqīḍ?
A) Because it is too difficult for beginners.
B) Because it is not used in the sciences and has different rules from simple inversion.
C) Because the author forgot to include it.
D) Because it contradicts the Qur'an.

Key Takeaway
The omission of later logicians' 'aks al-naqid demonstrates a practical prioritization of brevity and utility in logical education.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify which of the two reasons for omission applies to a hypothetical case: Suppose a logical concept is theoretically valid but requires a new set of rules different from an existing simple rule. Would the author include it? Explain using the commentator's reasoning.
?
Given a universal negative proposition in a fiqh example: 'No intoxicating drink is permissible,' convert it using simple inversion (universal negative converts to particular negative). Then, try to convert it using 'aks al-naqid: negate the predicate and subject and swap (e.g., 'What is not permissible is not intoxicating drink') – but check if this is equivalent. Reflect on why the later logicians' version might be more complicated.

Immediate Inference
intermediate
10 min
Proof of 'Aks al-Naqid by Reductio Ad Absurdum
Super-commentary provides example proofs for universal affirmative and negative via reductio, and notes that naqd is the same as in simple inversion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a reductio proof for 'aks al-naqid for both universal affirmative and negative propositions.

Core Idea
Aks al-naqid is proven by assuming the opposite and deriving a contradiction.

In this lesson, we examine the super-commentary's proof of inversion by contradictory (aks al-naqid). The proof uses reductio ad absurdum, a powerful tool in Islamic logic. We will walk through the proofs for universal affirmative and negative propositions, and see how assuming the contrary leads to a contradiction, thus establishing the inverse.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original proposition and its inverse
For the universal affirmative 'Every J is B', the inverse by contradictory is 'Every non-B is non-J'. For the universal negative 'No human is stone', the inverse is 'Some non-stone is human'.
The inverse is formed by contraposing and obverting as per the rules of 'aks al-naqid.
2
Assume the negation of the inverse
For the universal affirmative, assume 'Some non-B is J'. For the universal negative, assume 'No non-stone is human' (the contradictory of the inverse).
This is the premise for the reductio.
3
Apply simple inversion to the assumed proposition
From 'Some non-B is J', simple inversion gives 'Some J is non-B'. From 'No non-stone is human', simple inversion gives 'No human is non-stone' i.e., 'Every human is stone'.
Simple inversion is valid for particular affirmatives and universal negatives.
4
Observe the contradiction with the original
For the affirmative, 'Some J is non-B' contradicts 'Every J is B'. For the negative, 'Every human is stone' contradicts 'No human is stone'.
This contradiction shows the assumption leads to absurdity.
5
Conclude that the inverse must hold
Since assuming the negation of the inverse leads to contradiction, the inverse must be true by reductio ad absurdum.
This completes the proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَذَٰلِكَ مِثَالُ عَكْسِ النَّقِيضِ لِلْمُوجِبَةِ الْكُلِّيَّةِ: إِذَا صَدَقَ كُلُّ ج ب صَدَقَ كُلُّ مَا لَيْسَ ب لَيْسَ ج، وَإِلَّا فَبَعْضُ مَا لَيْسَ ب ج، وَتَنْعَكِسُ إِلَى بَعْضِ ج لَيْسَ ب، وَهَٰذَا تَهَافُتٌ. وَالنَّقْضُ الْمُوجِبُ لِعَدَمِ انْعِكَاسِ بَعْضِهَا إِلَى بَعْضٍ بِعَكْسِ النَّقِيضِ مِثْلُ الْبَيَانِ وَالنَّقْضِ الْمُوجِبِ لِعَدَمِ انْعِكَاسِ ذَٰلِكَ الْبَعْضِ فِي الْعَكْسِ الْمُسْتَوِي. وَنَقُولُ فِي عَكْسِ السَّالِبَةِ الْكُلِّيَّةِ: إِذَا صَدَقَ لَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الْإِنْسَانِ بِحَجَرٍ، صَدَقَ بَعْضُ مَا لَا حَجَرَ لَيْسَ بِلَا إِنْسَانٍ أَيْ إِنْسَانٍ، وَإِلَّا صَدَقَ نَقِيضُهُ وَهُوَ لَا شَيْءَ مِمَّا لَا حَجَرَ بِإِنْسَانٍ، وَيَنْعَكِسُ إِلَى لَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الْإِنْسَانِ بِلَا حَجَرٍ؛ أَيِ الْإِنْسَانُ حَجَرٌ، وَقَدْ كَانَ الْأَصْلُ لَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الْإِنْسَانِ بِحَجَرٍ إِثْبَاتُهُ بِدَلِيلِ الْخَلَفِ.
And an example of 'aks al-naqid for the universal affirmative: if 'Every J is B' is true, then 'Every non-B is non-J' is true; otherwise, some non-B is J, and that inverts to 'Some J is non-B', which is a contradiction. And the naqd that necessitates that some of them do not invert by 'aks al-naqid is like the demonstration and the naqd that necessitates that some part does not invert in simple inversion. And we say for the inverse of the universal negative: if 'No human is stone' is true, then 'Some non-stone is not non-human' i.e., human, is true; otherwise its contradictory is true, namely 'No non-stone is human', and it inverts to 'No human is non-stone'; i.e., 'human is stone', whereas the original was 'No human is stone'. Its proof is by reductio.
Source pages p. 742

Commentary
This passage, from the super-commentary, illustrates the proof of 'aks al-naqid using reductio ad absurdum (dalīl al-khalf). For the universal affirmative proposition 'Every J is B', the inverse by contradictory is 'Every non-B is non-J'. The proof shows that assuming the opposite (i.e., that some non-B is J) leads to a contradiction because it would imply 'Some J is non-B', which contradicts the original. Similarly, for the universal negative 'No human is stone', the inverse is 'Some non-stone is human'. The proof uses reductio: if the inverse is false, its contradictory 'No non-stone is human' is true, which inverts to 'No human is non-stone' i.e., 'human is stone', contradicting the original. The passage also notes that the naqd (counterexample or refutation) that prevents inversion of some particular propositions is the same as in simple inversion.

Key Terms
دَلِيلُ الْخَلَفِ
النَّقْض
تَهَافُت

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the reductio proof for 'No human is stone', what contradictory of the inverse is assumed to derive the contradiction?
A) Some non-stone is human.
B) No non-stone is human.
C) Every human is stone.
D) Some human is stone.

Key Takeaway
Aks al-naqid for universal propositions is proven by reductio ad absurdum, assuming the negation of the inverse and deriving a contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
For the universal affirmative 'Every intoxicant is forbidden', write a reductio proof for its contrapositive 'Every non-forbidden is non-intoxicant' using the same method as in the lesson.
?
For the universal negative 'No created thing is eternal', prove its inverse by contradictory using reductio, and identify the step where simple inversion is applied.
